UNDER THE GUMTREE
Master of His Fate.
HIS friends say John Fer-
guson N isbet walked to
death by the road of over-
work—a sacrifice to fero-
cious Journalism, who devours
her own children. It is likely
enough ; for he was a man of
many tasks, and did them all
doggedly. The persistence is in
his portrait-face—-not aggres-
sively Scottish, though IN'isbet
was Scottish ; but stern and
squared in the battle of life—the
face of a man who didn’t enjoy
his dinner. He seems rarely to
have relaxed his mood, never his
character; so that influenza
found him tense, without any
saving elasticities, and shattered
him for a grave in what should have been his
prime. He is decidedly a man to regret: for
himself, he regretted nothing. A Covenanting
thiuker, he made no sentimental compromises.
He has praised Calvinism as the most logical
creed ; and some hereditary germ in him wel-
comed the Calvinism of science—the predestined
lot, the inevitable destiny, of individual and race.
Deliberately he built a wall of reason which shut
out the religionist’s rosy eternities, and died with
his back to the wall.
I fear that Nisbet’s two books are little known
in Australia : they are certainly less known than
they deserve to be. “The Human Machine,”
indeed, is a posthumous publication, and has not
had time to obtain a wide audience. The publisher
should include it in his “colonial library,” and
should arrange to re-issue Nisbet’s earlier book,
“ The Insanity of Genius,” in a cheaper form.
Both books are worth their weight in silver to a
thoughtful reader. Their doctrines and lessons
are often hinted elsewhere, but nowhere are they
put so completely and compactly for the average
man. Nisbet assimilated many scientific hints
and treatises ; and he had the generalising mind
which concentrates and expands, rejecting the
unessential, firmly gripping the significant, aiid
always endeavoring to refer facts to a principle,
experiences to their,Jaw. It is a rare type of
mind, and valuable : ‘for most men are mentally
near-sighted, and need to be forcibly dragged from
the domination of details.
Nisbet was fortunate in that he early found his
characteristic theory, and never lost it. As a
journalist he wrote of many things—he was for
17 years dramatic critic of the Times, and he re-
viewed literature from the ordinary external stand-
point, ,as was desired of him ; but his main idea
was the idea of the physical basis of psychology.
That is a nevv idea for most people to-day :
twenty years ago, when Nisbet grasped it, it was
an explorer’s dream. He pressed it insistently ;
fitting it to fresh discoveries, to old history, to the
news of the daily press—not in any Procrustean
way, but by arguing down objections and arguing
up to truth. He has been one of the most strenu-
ous pioneers of the not-completed revolution in
modern thought which has superseded meta-
physics, or, rather, which has swallowed up meta-
physics in physics.
“The Insanity of Genius” develops a single
phase of Nisbet’s idea. To some extent the
title is misleading, for it is the non-sanity,
the unsoundness, of genius which Nisbet affirms ;
and though he has included evidence which is
doubtful as well as evidence which is clear, his
case is straitly proven for unprejudiced people.
Sanity—peifect health of body and brain —is
associated with the human norm from generation
to generation : the special cerebral development
which genius implies is a departure from the
norm, and in-sane in proportion to the extent of
its departure. Genius possesses in excess a single
faculty or group of faculties, and pays for its
possession by a corresponding deficiency in some
other faculty or group of faculties. Or, in physi-
cal terms, genius is hypertrophy of one particular
area of the brain, compensated by atrophy
or lesser development of the remaining areas.
To buttress this truth Nisbet brings in-
numerable facts and deductions, testifying to
solid industry and wide reading. Lombroso gained
similar facts at first-hand, and Nordau advocated
similar deductions more rhetorically, but neither
“The Man of Genius ” nor “Degeneration” is as
convincing or as valuable as Nisbet’s earlier book.
“ The Human Machine,” as the publisher omits
to say, is compiled from Nisbet’s contributions to
“The Handbook ” in London Referee; and it wants
the continuity of “The Insanity of Genius.”
It ranges in many mental fields, and reaches
no particular climax. It shows, too, the
effect of stress on the writer’s mind : it is not
always closely argued; the statements are some-
times vague or fallacious; there is a want of
adaptation to recent ideas, as if Nisbet’s brain had
lost some power of receiving new impressions ;
and it does not seem to have had the benefit of
final revision. On page 238, for example, there
is a repetition of the note upon page 215, which
remarks woman’s comparative deficiency in honour
or business principle. Nisbet might have said
why —because the sense of honour and business
principle are recent acquirements in the pro-
cess of evolution, and woman, busy in pre-
serving the human type, has lagged behind
evolutionally, and has not been able to embody all
the male variations from the aboriginal character.
By a whole range of theories, of the kind of which
Havelock Ellis is the most popular exponent,
Nisbet appears unimpressed. “ The Human
Machine ” is in no sense novel, as “The Insanity
of Genius ” was. It seems rather an additional
commentary on the author’s ideas of ten years ago
than a statement of fresh ideas—as if his mind had
yetritied towards his death. He expounds Weis-
maan theories which Weismann himself has modi-
fied. In view of the nervous changes caused by
abuse of alcohol, which probably react on
every function of the body, it is not correct
to say (p. 201) that “ The children of Philip
drunk inherit exactly the same qualities and
defects as those of Philip sober.” Even the doc-
trine of the permanence of the germ-plasm does
•lot declare that it goes unaffected by revolutions
in the nervous system. The germ-plasm may not
be affected by the loss of a tooth or of an arm;
but it is not walled off from the rest of the body;
it is subjected to constant change ; and it must be
influenced by any vital alteration of the state of
the organism. At p. 20G Nisbet goes so far as to
say that “It does not appear that the age of the
parents exercises any effect upon the robust-
ness of the offspring ” —which is almost ludi-
crously opposed to common sense and common
science. The sounder doctrine is contained in the
Sentence with which Ellis closes “The Criminal ”
Every falling away from health, every new strain and
stress, in man or woman, may lay an additional burden
on a man or woman yet unborn, and perhaps wreck a life
or a succession of lives.
I “The Human Machine,”' then, is not always
; but it is eminently suggestive. Though it
Surveys knowledge from Shakespeare to musical
glasses, it is in sum a series of re-statements of
jNisbet’s main idea—a series of references of con-
cepts and emotions to physical standards. It is
astonishingly pithy. The author packs in a page
thought-food which many men would spread over
a book. He is very shrewd, though not always
[impartial, and while he makes his points bril-
liantly he does not always state the objections—
(sometimes he does not seem to perceive them.
That he is solid, and lucid, and strong, is shown
by the first sentences in “ The Human Machine ”
i Broadly speaking, all manifestations of human activity,
physical, moral, and intellectual, have their seat in the
brain and nervous system. The living man is in some
sort an automaton, acted upon by influences or stimuli
derived from the outer world, through the channels of
his senses, and whether he is long-lived or short-lived,
healthy or ailing, a genius or a fool—in a word, sound or
unsound according to the standard of his species—de-
pends upon the relative efficiency of the cerebro-spinal
mechanism with which he has been endowed at birth, or
rather at conception.
And his constitutional pessimism, his scientific
position, are indicated in the last sentences
The most probable end of the world, as far as the
human race is concerned, is that in process of time the
earth, like the moon, will become unfit to support life.
It will be a very slow process, we may be sure—a process
extending over hundreds of thousands, perhaps millions,
of years; and as intelligence will probably,decay as life
becomes more and more reduced, the end will be un-
noticed. Nature, as a rule,* not violent in her methods ;
and it is extremely likely that that will be her method
with us, so closing an experiment the most obvious lesson
of which is that the individual counts for nothing in the
history of the race, the race for nothing in the life of the
planet, and the planet for nothing in the duration of the
universe.

Mark Twain—Huckleberry Finn.
William Watson lias written what an admirer
calls some “ fine verses ” for the anti-vivisection-
ists, commencing with an invocation of—
Wild nature not by kindness won, because
So seldom wooed that way ; rapt melodist,
Victorious singer of the eternal songs.
That, changeless through the ages, conquerest Time \
Shy, antlered, forest lord ; nor ye alone,
The eminent and splendid ones of Earth,
But creatures more familiar to Man's walk ;
Thou timorous fugitive, obscurely housed
In populous labyrinth under hillock and helm
and so on, with great parade of ponderous verbi-
age. Mr. Watson, like some of his Australian
imitators, is never satisfied till he has obscured one
little meaning with nine long words. What is
that “ timorous fugitive ” ? “ Hillock ” seems to
suggest something which scratches ; helm
something which is scratched. But they are
“ fine verses ” against vivisectionists : Bunny wins.
American Speaker Reed is as apt at retort as
the local Reid, and has far more Attic salt. It
was Reed who defined a statesman as. a successful
politician who is dead. It was Reed in his maiden
speech who finished an attack on an opponent
with
And now, having embalmed that fly in the liquid
amber of my remarks, I will proceed.
Of another opponent he said that “ he never opens
his mouth without subtracting from the sum of
human knowledge. ” And this was keen :
So far as I know, I have but two enemies in the State
of Maine. One of them President Harrison appointed
Collector at Portland ; the other he pardoned out of the
penitentiary.
Which recalls Heine’s comment upon a professor
at the obnoxious university of Gottingen who
wrote furious pamphlets against Napoleon. “It
is curious,” said Heine, “ the three greatest adver-
saries of Napoleon have all ended miserably.
Castlereagh cut his own throat; Louis the
Eighteenth rotted upon his throne ; and Prolessor
Saalfeld is still a,professor at Gottingen.”

The complaint that Mu. Dooley, in Peace and
War was too American for the general has been
blunted by the inclusion of five new papers in the
second edition. Best, and one of the best in the
series, is that which does justice to Kipling’s men-
tal agility and alacrity—
What I like about Kipling is that his pomes is r-right
off th’ bat, like me eon-versations with you, me boy. He’s
a minyit-man, a r-ready pote that sleeps like th’ dhriver
iv truck 9, with his poetic pants in his boots beside his
bed, an’ he is ready to jump out an’ slide down th’ pole
th’ ininyit th’ alarm sounds. ... Do ye hear iv a man-
hole cover bein’ blown up ? Roodyard is there with his
r-ready pen. “’T is written iv Cashum-Cadi an’ th’ book
iv th’ gr-great Gazelle that a manhole cover in anger is
tin degrees worse than hell.” No col’ storage pothry f’r
Kipling. Iverything f’fresh an’ up to date. All lays laid
this mornin’.
Kipling’s suit against American-publishers Put-
nams (=£sooo damages claimed) is the more in-
teresting because the Putnams have always posed
as Particularly Proper Publishers. One of the
members of N.S.W. Assembly wears his conscience
at his buttonhole, and irritates antagonists by
lofty invitations to admire its quality and size.
G. P. Putnam, in his book of “Author and Pub-
lisher,” leaves a similar irritation : you see
clearly what a great flea the publisher is,
and what a deplorable parasite the author.
Kipling justly considers the firm’s conduct
something more than a pious impertinence, and is
in the right not to be placated by the shocked
remonstrance that “We” could not possibly do
anything unfair —seeing “We” did it. Kipling
has one authorised American edition—“ The Out-
ward Bound,” which Scribners issue. The Put-
nams bought loose sheets of Kipling’s books;
bound them uniformly; and announced a rival
“Brushwood Edition,” in various, ways suggesting
that the edition had Kipling’s approval. Yet it
included matter which he disowned ; he was con-
sulted neither as to contents nor arrangement,
and an editor’s essay, “The Ken of Kipling,”
patted approval to unwilling Rudyard. Comes to
this : Is an author to settle the fashion in which
his copyright books are to be published—and in
what order, with what additions and omissions—
or is he not '!
&
W. B. Yeats’s play, “The Countess Cathleen,”
which opened the new Irish Literary Theatre, was'
hissed by some students and condemned by some
ecclesiastics, and as warmly approved by other
critics not too Catholic.to be catholic. “ The idea
of the play is the redeeming of the souls of starv-
ing peasants who have sold their religious convic-
tions for gold, by the sacrifice of Countess
Cathleen’s‘ own soul.” Opponents say this is un-
Catholic, and therefore un-Irish. But the terms
are not mutually exclusive. What is Irish pre-
ceded and swallowed what is Catholic. The R.C.
religion in Ireland is embroidered on a cloth of
Celtic superstition; and, though the embroidery is
abundant, the cloth is always visible.

coined, apart from, merits. Then the author may

fancy him with frock coat, silk hat, crutch-cane,
and patent-leather shoes, picturesquing Picca-
Sarcey adds another to “ruling passion ’’death-
beds. He missed his weekly article only once in
forty years ; and when he died—
“ I must get up,” he said to his doctor ;“ it is abso-
lutely necessary that I should write my article. I will do
only a demi-feuiUeton, if I must, but I must do it.” A few
moments later he returned to this fixed idea. “ I shall
go again and see ‘ Le Torrent.’ I am afraid this piece is
not well understood. It has not been rightly judged.”
Those were Sarcey’s last words. By the way,
Henri Becque, a brilliant but unpopular drama-
tist, who held that Sarcey damned him with the
Parisian public, is credited with having threatened
many years ago : “ Well, when I go to the shades,
in 48 hours I will drag that villain after me.”
And, sure enough, he died two days before Sarcey.
Every readable journalist must be more or less
of a cocksure Macaulay (his audience does not ap-
preciate half-tones and side-lights), and bright
correspondent G. W. Steevens is more. When he
accompanied Viceroy Curzon to India all Bengal
went into his generalising funnel, and some of it
came out like this—
The down-country native of Bombay is poor and weak
and insignificant. He looks as if you could break him
across your knee. His formless features express nothing:
his eyes have the shining meekness, but not the benevo-
lence, of the cow’s. The leg of a free man is straight or
a little bandy, so that he can stand on it solidly; his
calf is taper, and his thigh flat. The Bengali’s leg is
either skin and bone, the same size all the way down,
with knocking knobs for knees, or else it is very fat aDd
globular, also turning in at the knees, with round thighs
like a woman’s. The Bengali’s leg is the leg of a slave.
Except by grace of his natural masters, a slave he always
has been and always must he.
But the Babu has a gift of tongue, and he used
it —like this —
Mr. Steevens, I thought, would he a giant—giant in body
and mind. But no such thing ! He is a very poor
specimen of humanity as shoulders go. Narrow chest,
narrow shoulders—it looked as if his body was suddenly
arrested in development. It may be that his head de-
veloped to the sacrifice of the body. And his face : It had
a nose which looked as if it were the result of a con-
temptuous after-thought on the part of its creator. It
had a pair of ears rather long, which reminded—hem !—
and which looked as if they were cut and pasted at the
sides not exactly in their proper place. It had cheek-
bones which courted some prominence, and which gave
the owner thereof a half-starved appearance.
And so on, accumulating pieces de conviction which
a Rajpoot dog would despise. But admire the
aposiopesis: “Ears which reminded — hem !”
This is a different Babu from him who asked to be
forgiven a debt on the ground that his “means
are already circumcised by my large family of five
female women and three masculine, the last of
whom are still taking milk from mother’s chest.”

By E. G. Bowles' (Cockney)—

Lays her hand on the lips of Sleep 1
W. H. Malloek’s new novel, The Individualist,
reprinted from the Fortnujhtly Review, is as dull
as a dead policeman in a wet macintosh.
Fiona Maefeod says she isn’t William Sharp, or
Andrew Lang, or any other masquerading body.
She is just herself, plus as much “ Celtic spirit ”
as she can grow by careful cultivation.
Justin McCarthy, in his new book, Modern
England : “ The world will see no advance on
Tennyson and Browning.” McCarthy is 09, and
the prophet is senile.
Whoever has not read The Ordeal of Richard
Feverel can buy it in a new edition at 6:1.
Whistler’s “The Baronet and the Butterfly ” is
advertised laboured, tedious, and unworthy of the
author of “The Gentle Art.”
Coventry Patmore’s “Memoir, ” by his widow
and others, is promised this year.
The anti-climax of the recent Balzac centenary
celebration at Tours was reached when the great
man’s sere and yellow tailor was platformed to tell
a reverent.audience that the great man liked his
trousers baggy at the knee.
Obeunt: Augustin Daly, American novelist,
critic,-playwright, and manager; 61. Rosa Bon-
heur, painter; 77.
Balzac’s “ Let tree a rare Etrangere ” — the
woman for whom he longed seventeen years, are
just published. She was Countess Eve de Hau-
ska, and in the end Balzac married her—dying
three months after. Yet, as A. B. Walkley
notes, the wife seventeen years waited-for had
left him before bis death, before, three months !.
Tub: BOOk FELLOW.
As fit as a fiddle.
As cross as two sticks.
As slow as a funeral.
As straight as a dart.
As straight as a yard of pump-water.
As straight as a rush.
As tricky as a box of chips.
As clear as my conscience.
As sound as a bell.
As sharp as a needle.
Like a red rag to a bull.
As ugly as sin.
Like a dying duck in a thunderstorm.
Like a bewitched hayrick.
As weak as seven days.
Shivering like a dog in a wet sack.
As long as a late breakfast.
Getting on “like a house on fire.”
Getting on “ like a brick wall burning.”
A CARESS.
I said, “ The Sea is royal blue."
She opened her large eyes.
“ It wins away my heart from you.”
She answered me with sighs.
I said, “ The Sea has snow-white sands
That glance and gleam like silk,”
She opened her two pretty hands,
And they were white as milk.
I said, “ The Sea hides many pearls
Red coral caverns in.”
She laughed the laugh of merry girls
To show the pearls within.
I said that many moods abide
Beneath the velvet Sea.
She drooped her blue-veined lids to hide
A truant thought from me.
I said “ The Sea has winsome wiles
And singeth ‘ Come to me ! ’ "
She wreathed her rosebud mouth in smiles,
And turned as if to flee.
I caught her so—“ The Sea’s caress
Gives Death or deadly strife ;
But yours, my love—now, by this kiss
Your lips, my love, are Life ! ”

Doveton-street, Ballarat,
My dear Mr. Malyon,— August 21st, 1898.
With great pleasure I now sit down to record progress
with regard to my old enemy, “Eczema Capitis,” from
which I suffered, notwithstanding constant treatment for
some years under the prescription of Professor M‘Call
Anderson and other eminent dermatologists, with only
partial and temporary relief. When I met you I com-
menced somewhat sceptically your ointment Ligmolliene,
and after about two months’ treatment I may honestly say
my enemy is defeated, and I am perfectly well. I will only
add that since I commenced the treatment of myself I have
prescribed it to others, and am still doing so with brilliant
results. Please accept my hearty thanks, and remain yours
sincerely, THOS. LE GAY HOLTHOUSE, M.R.0.5., L.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
While a certain Yic. suburban member was
addressing the House the other evening he was ob-
served to suddenly put his hand to his mouth.
He was in the middle of a sentence. “ I main-
tain, Mr. Speaker,” said he, “ that hon. members
opposite have no right to persist in their objection
to this Bill, in the very teeth”—here he took
something from his mouth and put it carefully
into his waistcoat-pocket—“of public opinion.”
For the rest of the speech he had some difficulty
in making the “ th ” sound ; but the dentist fixed
him up all right the next morning.
According to Abdul Wade, the Bourke
(N.S.W.) Carrying Co., of which he was general
manager, and whose awful slave-agreement was
lately before the courts, isn’t an Afghan concern.
It was formed three years ago to take over his
camel-trains and business, and now consists of
himself, several big wool-broking and mortgage
co’s, at least one bank, and some Western squatters
who had found the camels the surest way of
getting supplies. But, as Abdul Wade has power
to engage and dismiss, and buy and sell, and make
contracts and vary them, he is still probably all
but the tail of the dog. The co. has now about
400 camels and SO Afghan servants.
N.S.W.’s recent heavy visitor, Higgins,
got off some of his usual Dogberryisms when the
Federal Bill was introduced in the Victorian
Assembly :
Mr. Higgins protested against the unconstitutional
manner in which the Bill had been amended. The
people had not considered the amendments, nor were
they considered by their representatives. They were in
the position of having had a pistol held to their heads
and ordered to vote “ Yes ”or “ No.” He contended the
people had been deprived of the advantages which would
result from higher education on the question.
What would satisfy Heavy Higgins, The Bulle-
tin wonders ? The people elected the men who
first drafted the Bill —elected them specially for
the purpose. The men who drafted the amend-
ments were the people’s chief elected representa-
tives. Then the people’s representatives consider
the measure and the amendments as much as they
darned well please in Parliament. Then the
people consider the measure and the amendments,
and vote for it, or against it, or don’t vote at all,
just as they please. And even now Heavy
Higgins thinks things aren’t being considered
enough.
Idols.
There’s a chamber in our hearts, where we keep'
The memories that will not die. They sleep,
Bub when the South Wind, mourning, brings the
rain,
These shrouded dreams stalk gauntly out again.
There is a drawer in most desks. Untold
The wealth we bury there—the hoop of gold,
The letter, and the photograph. Years, years,
Your sapless trunks loom through a mist of tears !
Dreamer, the years are dead—are dead and gone ;
Dreamer, the years must die. Their souls live on.
Close thou the chamber of thy heart. Alway
Do feet of dreamers’ idols turn to clay.
Sydney, June, ’99.
E. J. BRADY.
From the Strand to the Never.
Last glass of the last of our revels !
Last song to be sung without ruth !
Let us give what is due to our devils
’Ere the curtain comes down on our youth !
In the old lands we’re vagabonds ever,
In the new such as we bear the brunt,
And the path from the “Strand to the Never”—
Why ashamed of it ?—lights up in front!
Then here’s to the cross-bearer silent,
To the lips that are grim to the end,
To the pledge that was broken for frien lship,
And the lie that was sworn for a friend ;
To the lie for the lie that was noble —
To the heart that would perjure the soul
For the sake of a woman in trouble,
For the sake of a mate “ in a hole.”
We drifted and saw not the dangers
Of wearing the heart on the sleeve;
We saw the fresh kindness of strangers
And deemed it was best to believe.
If we borrow’d and passed when we needed,
If we loved for awhile and were gone
In the end ’twill be surely conceded
That we gave all we had—and passed on 1
On the stage set for good or for evil—
111 the Youth-play we’re leaving behind—
Were the prompter man angel or devil,
For the most part the promptings were kind !
And whatever the future before us,
And whatever the heart-burn it brings,
Here’s a glass to the spirit that bore us !
And—the “rag” is waved down from the wings.
Sydney.
HENRY LAWSON.
The Idol of the King.
The Idol of the King was cast in cunning mould,
It stood on feet of clay—its head was carven gold.
It loomed in vision vast, a drear and awful Thing ;
It smote the heart with dread —the Idol of the
King !
But when the night had passed—it fled with Dawn
o’ Day,
This god of gold and brass, this god of ore and
clay.
4> * * *
Ah—better now we build. No stately olden fane
Beholds us bend the back to god of visions vain ;
For We who worship now—We know the Wiser
way:
Our Idol’s all of gold—Our Soul is all of clay !
P. LUFTIG.

yankee Ooodle
IN AMERICA.
The Legend.
IT was in the summer of 1778, Wash-
ington, with his army ever active,
determined to oppose the British
general, Sir Henry Clinton, who, having
evacuated Philadelphia, was retreating
through New Jersey on New York. The
forces met on 28th June at Monmouth.
La Payette conducted the attack. The
heat was so intense that some refused to
fight, and many fell dead from exposure
to the sun’s rays. Both sides fought
desperately. As the day waned, and the
flagging spirits of La Fayette’s soldiers
were at a low ebb, a strange sight aroused
them. Marching with * drum and fife
was an old man, Jeremiah Wynton, and
his son, and grandson. The old father,
with silvery flowing locks, sturdily strode
through the field. The son with ban-
daged head fifing lustily, the youth,
catching the spirit of his grandsire, rolled
out with him the soul-inspiring rattle of
the drum Father, son, and grandson
had accompanied the troops as volunteers,
and were ever in the forefront of the
fight. The fierce light of battle was in
their eyes, they knew no fear, and many
a wild cheer arose, as on that, fateful
day Yankee Doodle was heard as never
was heard before or since.

SATURDAY, JULY 22, 1899.
Queensland’s Twenty-Two-and-Sixpenny
Lie.
The old Twenty-two-and-Sixpenny Lie which
was invented by the anti-Federalists of New
South Wales, and did so much service under
their auspices, has spread to Queensland.
There were some variations about the old
N.S.W. lie ; for instance, the amount Fede-
ration was to cost annually in extra taxation
was sometimes put down at £1 per head,
sometimes at 225. 6d., sometimes 255. 6d.
or 295. 4|d. ; and the variations have spread
to Queensland also. The lie w 7 as specially
constructed to fit certain peculiar N.S.W.
circumstances, and it can’t possibly fit the
circumstances of any other province. In
Queensland it is as grotesque a misfit as a
fur overcoat would be at Cooktown on the
longest day—the one when you have no
shadow; or a mailed crusader would be
in Brisbane ; or a gorilla in church.
But in a dull, sheep-like fashion the
Queensland anti-Federal papers borrowed
this purely Cornstalk lie ; and having
not even sense enough to know how it
originated or what it referred to, they didn’t
see any discrepancy about it. The Sydney
parochialist organs reasoned the matter out
with a resemblance of plausibility ; the Bris-
bane ones merely dab down the bald state-
ment, without knowing what on earth it is
all about. So the papers published in the
city of many floods are breaking out in
harrowing, ungrammatical groans like this,
quoted word for word from a Brisbane
paper :
In addition to being the most heavily-taxed people
under Heaven, it is proposed to ruin our factories,
farmers and shopkeepers, and add a tax of £1 ss. 6d. per
head on every man, woman and child in Queensland, in
order that a Governor-General, two Houses of Parliament,
with members receiving £4OO a-year each, and a Federal
city 1000 miles away from Brisbane, at a cost of £5,000,000,
to be purchased and jobbed off by Southern auctioneers.
As regards the capital it can only be said
that Queensland is hard to satisfy. By the
terms of the agreement the capital may be
anywhere in N.S.W, except within 100 miles
of Sydney. Therefore it can’t be within 100
miles of Sydney, within about 160 of Mel-
bourne or Adelaide, within 1600 miles of
Perth or 400 of Hobart, but it may be on
the northern N.S.W. border not more than
80 miles in a bee-line from Brisbane. And
it can’t by any human possibility be over
800 miles from Brisbane no matter how
things are strained. And Queensland gains
new territory by it; 100 square miles are to
becutoutof N.S.W. to make the Federal
territory, and Queensland will be part owner
thereof. And if the new capital does cost
£5,000,000 N.S.W. and Victoria will have
to supply £3,500,000 of the money. Alto-
gether Brisbane comes out of the deal
rather better than any other State capital,
and still it seems Brisbane isn’t satisfied.
But this is a mere side issue, and has no
connection with the great Twenty-two or
Twenty-five-and-Sixpenny Lie.
* * * *
Said lie was based on the fact that there
must of necessity be a uniform Federal tariff,
and on the fact that New South Wales has,
at present, the lowest tariff in Australasia.
For the year ended 30th June, 1898, the
customs and excise revenue of the eastern
and central States was :
Customs and Excise
Revenue per head
per annum.
Queensland £2 14 7
Tasmania ... 2 3 11
Victoria 1 15 14
South Australia 1 13 5
New South Wales 13 1
It was on these figures and on certain base-
less assumptions tacked on to them, that the
original Twenty-two-and-Sixpenny- Lie had
its shaky foundations. It was assumed : (1)
That the extra cost of the Federal Govern-
ment would run to 2s. 6d. or 3s. 6d. per in-
habitant per annum, or thereabouts ; (2)
That there would be no saving to counter-
balance this by conversion of the public
debt, by the abolition of superfluous
Postmasters-General, Agents-General, mili-
tary commandants, &c., or by the reduction
of the number of members in provincial
Parliaments; (3) That Tasmania, S.A., and
Victoria couldn’t do with less customs
revenue, or raise more direct taxation, than
they now receive ; (4) That they had no sur-
plus under present conditions ; (5) That it
would therefore be necessary to raise the
customs taxation of N.S.W., Victoria, and
S.A. to the Tasmanian level—Westralia was
left out of the calculation because it is
specially provided for by being allowed to
temporarily retain its border duties, and in
the days when the Twenty-two-and-sixpenny
Lie was erected Queensland was standing
out of the negotiation ; and (6)That N.S.W.,
though it would thus have about a million a
year extra revenue from its tariff, couldn’t,
for some inscrutable reason, let the people
have the money back by abolishing other
forms of taxation, endowing municipalities,
reducing railway fares, building much-
needed public works, abolishing school fees,
and the like, but must throw the cash
overboard and make it a dead loss. By
assuming all these absurd things it was
figured out on a process of insane arithmetic
that N.S.W. would have to endure an extra
burden of 225. 6d. per head annually under
Federation, S.A. about 10s. 6d., and Vic-
toria about Bs., in order to put their customs
and excise revenue on the Tasmanian level.
The fact that Tasmania can afford to cut its
tariff down by nearly 10s. per head per
annum was left out altogether.
» * * *
There was a faint and flabby pretence of
reason about this so far as the Southern
States were concerned ; but when the
Queensland anti-Federalists, not being able
to construct a lie of their own, borrow
N.S.W.’s Twenty-Two-and-Sixpenny snake-
story, they show a pitiable poverty of inven-
tion. The tale doesn’t fit anywhere. Queens-
land’s customs and excise revenue can’t,
under a uniform tariff, be levelled up to keep
pace with that of any other province, for it
is already at the top. It might be alleged,
with some purely bogus show of plausibility,
that the inclusion of Queensland would
cost Tasmania 10s. Bd. per inhabi-
tant per year, and the other provinces
more, in levelling up their tariffs to
the Queensland level, but the idea of
it doing anything of the kind to Queensland
is a huge and very clumsy joke. Federation,
and the uniform tariff that necessarily goes
with it, may save the mass of Queenslanders
a great deal by lowering their tariff burden
to the level of that of Tasmania or Victoria
or S.A. ; in which case the State Govern-
ment may have to make up part of the differ-
ence by economy and by direct taxation on
the Fat Men of the MTlwraith clan—a
result which MTlwraith himself admits to
be more than possible. But to increase
Queensland’s burden by £1 ss. 6d. per head
per annum would mean creating a uniform
Federal tariff that would yield £4 per head
per annum all round. It would mean that
the N.S.W. members of the Federal Parlia-
ment were going to vote for increasing the
burdens on their own people by £2 16s. lid.
per head per annum; that the South Austra-
lians were yearning to pay £2 6s. 7d. per
head annually more than they do now ; that
the Victorians desired to load every Vic-
torian up with £2 4s. Id. extra taxation per
year ; and that the Tasmanians wanted to
revel in an additional load of £1 16s. Id.
Not one of these people could vote for taxing
the Queenslander an extra £1 ss. 6d. without
also voting to tax themselves a great deal
more. And are they built that way ? I hey
don’t look like it.
* » * *
The Finance Committee appointed in
N.S.W. to consider the possibilities of the
case decided, by a majority of two bitter
anti-Federalists to one Federalist, that the
extra cost of Federation would be, as nearly
as possible, £500,000 a • year—about 3s. per
inhabitant per annum. This was taking no
account of savings by the conversion of the
public debt or otherwise. As Queensland
can’t possibly lose anything by the levelling
up of its tariff—its tariff being distinctly up
already— this is all it can by any chance have
to pay. On the other hand, Queensland’s
public debt now costs it £3 15s. lOd. per
cent, interest, and when the Federal Govern-
ment takes this over and -converts it into a
uniform Australian stock at 3 per cent. —and
the Commonwealth’s credit will be quite good
enough for that—the saving to the Queens-
land people will be about £250,000 a-year—
about 10s. per inhabitant per annum. There-
fore, Federation means a net saving of 7s.
per annum for every Queenslander —man,
woman and infant. By way of further
savings, Queensland will have no Postmaster-
General, Agent-General or military com-
mandant to pay ; it can reduce the dimen-
sions of its State Parliament, and it can stop
paying its State Governor, and make him a
purely honorary official. But, in the visible
savings alone, Queensland can afford to have
its tariff-burden reduced under Federation
from £2 14s. 7d. per inhabitant to £2 7s. 7d.,
and still be as sound financially as it is now.
It can further afford to have its tariff-burden
cut down to £2 per head, and make
up the difference by direct taxation on large
incomes and large estates, and still be about
the most lightly direct-taxed province in
Australasia. For Queensland is not, as
alleged, the most heavily-taxed country in
the world, even reckoning direct and indirect
taxation both together—not by a long, long
way. And if it was, so much the more
reason why it should join the Common-
wealth.
* * * *
For the plea of the Queensland anti-
Federalist literally is : “You are the
most heavily-taxed people in the world.
THEREFORE, ADHERE TO THE
PROVINCIAL SYSTEM WHICH MAKES
YOU THE MOST HEAVILY - TAXED
PEOPLE IN THE WORLD ! Hang
on manfully to the old arrangement
under which you have become the
most heavily-taxed people in the world.
You will know the goodness or badness
of a system by its results. The provin-
cial policy has made you the most heavily-
taxed people in the world ; therefore it
is a good policy, and he who lets it go is a
fool, and he who doesn’t hold on to it is an
ass, and he who isn’t ready to perish in the
last ditch in defence of the policy which has
made his country the most heavily-taxed
country on earth is partly suicide and partly
traitor. All the great men—the M‘ll-
wraiths and their kind — who made this
the most heavily-taxed country on earth are
against Federation, and they ought to know ;
financiers like Reid, Turner, Fysh and
Holder who didn’t lead their provinces into
the same heavily-burdened condition are in
favor of Federation, but they evidently
don’t know. The only genuine financial
authorities are the boomers who wrecked our
big bank, and muddled themselves into in-
solvency, and made us the most heavily-
taxed people on earth ; let us go by their
advice and stand by their policy. Above all,
keep your eye on Nelson. In 1893 he per-
sonally audited our big bank—the only bank
now existing in Australia which was so
utterly bad that it had to offer its deposi-
tors 15s. in the £ —and he was so shamefully
ignorant of finance that he said it was quite
sound. He now says the Federal financial
scheme is unsound, and he ought to know.”
* * * *
The Queensland provincialists don’t put it
in exactly these words, but the meaning is
there to a hair.
The Pacific Cable.
The Pacific Cable is a dull subject ; and The
Bulletin imagines itself taking the fatigued
and reluctant Australian by the nose and filling
him with facts he ought to know. And it cau-
tions him that if he skips them now he will miss
them later on.
# * * •
Australia’s cable communication with the
rest of the world is maintained on a single line,
which is owned by various countries and com-
panies, but is practically controlled by the
Eastern Extension Telegraph Co., whose head-
quarters are in London. This co. is the fattest
kind of monopoly. It has a capital of mil-
lions, and its £lO-paid shares are worth about
£l6 10s. It pays only 7 per cent., being studi-
ously moderate in the matter of dividends ; but
if it chose it could pay double that. But a 14-
per-cent. div. would invite competition, and
competition is the last thing the Eastern
Extension Co. w’ants. So it is credited
instead with the familiar devices of stock-
watering : accumulation of huge reserves ;
writing down plant below value ; carrying
heavy stocks of material; and so on. Never
was there a more amiable monopolist. If a
prominent Australian politician whose goodwill
may be of value goes to London he can
send practically whatever cables he wishes,
without charge. If he does not call round, the
co. is good enough to look him up and let him
know his privileges. Then the prominent Aus-
tralian is supposed to come home grateful, and
very much averse from interfering with a nice,
kind monopoly like the Eastern Extension Co.
Yet there is no doubt that, sooner or later,
the Eastern Extension co.’s monopoly must be
destroyed ; and the sooner the better for Aus-
tralia. One cable, one way round the world,
is not enough for this country to-day ; in ten
years’ time it will be inconveniently little.
Even in peace-time, our one cable is subject to
frequent and costly interruptions ; in war-time
we should probably be cut off from the world
altogether. Further, it is foolish to bring our
messages to and-from Canada and the U.S.
round via England, when they can be brought
direct with a saving in time and money.
* * * •
Then there are our pockets to consider.
Up to 1891 the rate of cabling to Eng-
land was 9s. 4d. per word ; and the monopoly
waxed fat. Since 1891 it has been 4s. lOd. to ss.
sd.;and the monopoly has actually waxed fatter,
for the increase in messages has returned a
greater revenue with practically no addi-
tional expenditure. Messages would pay
at 3s. per word ; and by-and-by they will
pay at less. With trade increasing yearly,
cheaper communication with customers means
a great deal to Australia. The cost of a new
cable would be repaid in a very few years. It
is not clear that the cost of press messages
would be much reduced by a new cable ; never-
theless a new cable would be a great boon to
the Australian press. Just now monopoly
helps monopoly. You cannot start a daily
newspaper in Australia without arranging for
cables : and the Eastern Extension Co. is not
desirous of arranging for cables. It deals with
the existing ring of daily newspapers, who-
naturally do not want competition. In prac-
tice the unwritten understanding between the-
newspaper monopoly and the cable monopoly
works out this way : “If we agree not to say
unkind things about your monopoly, and not to-
incite opposition, you will discourage opposi-
tion to our newspaper monopoly.” That is a
very good understanding for the monopolists ;;
but a very bad one. for the public, who often
get meagre and worthless cable-intelligence-
where newspaper competition would give them,
full and valuable cable-intelligence.
• ♦ * *
Theoretically, it is obvious that the Pacific-
cable-game is worth the candle. Practically,
the trouble is : Who shall provide the candle?
Britain is interested, for reasons of empire and
trade ; Canada is interested, for reasons of im-
perial sentiment and trade ; Australia is inte-
rested, for reasons of security and trade. The
cost of a cable is estimated at from <£1,500,000
to £1,800,000. It could go from E. Australia to
Norfolk Island ; to Fiji ; to Fanning Island (a
little island between Fiji and Canada) ; to Van-
couver. At the Premiers’ Conference, last
year, it was arranged that, if Britain ano
Canada would each contribute five-eighteenths
of the cost, the Parliaments of N.S. W..
Vic., and Queensland should be asked to
guarantee one-ninth each. (South Australia
and Westralia stand out because they help to
control the existing cable, and are interested in
maintaining the existing monopoly.) Maoriland
Ministry came in with the offer of another
ninth. Canada was agreeable. But Britain
refused. British Columbia then offered to
guarantee an additional one-ninth, leaving
three-eighteenths to Britain. Britain stilt
refused. But Britain munificently offered
to guarantee five-eighteenths of the annual
deficit, if any, in working the cable, providing
its utmost liability did not exceed £5,555 per
annum. In return for guaranteeing a possible
£5,555 per annum out of an expenditure of
If millions or more, Britain demanded—
1. That the cable be constructed under its superin-
tendence and to its approval, and stations be put where
it saw fit, to be worked by British subjects.
112. That British Govt, messages take priority of all
others, at half ordinary rates.
3. That all rates charged be subject to its approval.
4. That complete accounts be rendered to it for inspec-
tion and approval.
6. Three other stipulations.
Good old Britain !
* * * *
The latest cable says that Britain has recon-
sidered things in a less niggardly spirit. Britain
now offers to take a full share of the cash
responsibility, and proposes that commissioners
be appointed to manage the undertaking.
Britain wants three commissioners to Aus-
tralasia’s three and Canada’s two ; but if Aus-
tralasia is to contribute four-ninths of the cost
she should have four, and Britain might be
satisfied with two, the same as Canada.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Concerning a Certain Ass.
After reading about 45 explanations of the
Federal Constitution by all sorts and conditions
of anti-Billious, one is struck by the fact that a
very small percentage of the human race seems
to understand its own language, even when put
clearly in large print. The latest Bill-explainer
who has come to this paper’s knowledge is
“Junius,” the man who is paid for not know-
ing things in Dunedin (M.L.) Star. He tells
the people of the Scotch-whisky city that:
The present Parliaments will be abolished and a Senate
with House of Representatives substituted.
The qualification of members of the House of Repre-
sentatives in the first election will be just the same as
are now in force in each State, but (here One-man-one-
vote and our woman suffrage are in danger) what after
the first election ?
The Customs, with our other various departments,
would have their control diverted to the capital; roads,
railways, shipping and navigation the same.
This Commonwealth Bill does not even concede to the
States what the Home Rule Bill of 1886 conceded to Ire-
land. A leading politician of the day—Mr. Bryce -thus
tersely and briefly explained its meaning : —“ Whilst the
ultimate right to legislate will reside in the Imperial Par-
liament, we shall have conceded to the Irish Legislature
the right to legislate on subjects upon which we do not
intend to legislate ourselves.” Mr. Want may well say
the present Bill is worse than the last one.
The Bill in question takes away almost every liberty
that we have so nobly fought to maintain.
Protection has triumphed.
I have enumerated sufficient to demonstrate the ultra-
Toryism of this so-called liberty-taking measure.
Think of any paper paying so stupendously
ignorant a lout as this for his misinformation.
The Bulletin also desires to address the people
of the whisky-loving city in a few words :
The Bill doesn’t abolish any one of the present Parlia-
ments. The very term “ Federation ”is always used to
imply a league of States, each one of which retains its
own State Legislature, and in every Federation every


State has its own Legislature. Therefore, " Junius” is
a Vhe Qualification of electors of the House of Represen-
. the first election will not be the same as at
nresent the Bill specifically abolishes all plural voting.
Therefore “Junius” is another ass.
man-one-vote is not in danger afterwards. In
clauses 8 and 30 it is fixed as part of the Constitution
whatever changes may be made later on in the
method of voting, still, both at elections for the Senate
and for the House of Representatives, “ each elector
shall only vote once.” Consequently “Junius” is the
Sa No mmnorwoman who has been enfranchised by the
can lose his or her vote for the Federal Parliament.
The woman suffrage is NOT in danger. Clause 41 says :
.. w 0 a( | u it person who has or acquires a right to vote at
ole,•lions for the more numerous House (i.e., the Lower
l ouse) of the Parliament of a State shall, while the
continues, be prevented by any law of the Com-
monwea?th from voting at elections for EITHER HOUSE
OF THE PARLIAMENT OF THE COMMONWEALTH.
For which reason “Junius” is the animal above-men-
Commonwealth Parliament doesn’t interfere with
anv State’s roads and railways except by the permission
of the State. Which again shows up “Junius” as the
nnrespected beast of burden already described.
It leaves the State Parliaments much larger powers
than the Bill of 1886 offered to the proposed Irish Parlia-
ment, though “ Junius,” being the object hereinbefore
mentioned, didn’t know it.
It doesn’t contain a single word or line committing the
Commonwealth to Protection. That matter is left to the
people, as it always has been. The reason why “ Junius”
didn’t know this has been already set forth.
It offers the people two Houses of Parliament, both
paid both elected by absolute manhood, or manhood and
womanhood, suffrage, both dissolvable, both fully respon-
sible to the people, with no property qualification for
either House, with a remedy for deadlocks, and with the
referendum as a means of deciding alterations of the Con-
stitution. In fact it offers Maoriland a Federal Parlia-
ment which is vastly more democratic than its present
State Parliament with its undissolvable Council of
nominees. And “ Junius,” who thinks this is pure Tory-
ism, is evidently the long-eared, unthinking bearer of
heavy burdens already specified.
For all which, and many other, reasons The
Bulletin urges the people of Maoriland to con-
sider the advisability of joining the most com-
pletely democratic Commonwealth this world
has ever known, and calls “Junius,” of the
Dunedin evening paper, an ass once more just
for the sake of variety.
The City of Mean Streets.
Mayor Harris’s scheme for the municipal re-
organisation of Sydney is satisfactory in spots
while in other spots it isn’t. It starts well
wit h a recognition of many things that are not
done when they should be, and are done when
they shouldn’t be. At present the Government
owns the Sydney trams, which should be a
source of revenue to the municipalities. The
municipalities have no power to compulsorily
resume land for the straightening and widening
of streets, whereas they should have power to
not only resume the land actually needed, but
extensive adjacent areas as well, so as to secure
for the municipality the increased values arising
through the improvements made with public
money. They should also be authorised to
levy general improvement rates and special
betterment rates. At present the Gas Co.
and the Hydraulic Power Co. have authority to
tear up the streets anywhere and everywhere
at their pleasure, and these and all other ser-
vices which take violent liberties with the
public thoroughfares should be municipally
owned. Also, the Government reserves the
right to obstruct the streets in any and every
way, as witness the blocking of the centre of
narrow, crooked George-street with a row of
huge pillars to carry electric wires. It likewise
reserves the right to break or ignore the Build-
ing Act at its pleasure, and to put up structures
in the city which, if put up by any private per-
son, would involve prosecution—the extraordi-
nary cow-shed which the Postal Goths have
erected in Martin Place is a case in point. All
these are tilings which require remedy. Some
of the Sydney suburbs have no Building Act at
all. None of the 41 municipalities have any
control over street traffic. They have very
little power to compel the improvement
or demolition of slum properties. They
have 233 aldermen among them, which is a
great and cumbersome army for a population
of 420,000 people. They have practically no
power to construct anything, whereas their
powers-should be of the widest. And it is
estimated, in addition to many other grievances
of the present system, that it involves a need-
less loss of £45,000 a year through the useless
multiplication of officials, and through the high
interest rates which the pocket-handkerchief
municipalities have to pay on their loans and
overdrafts. And having drawn attention to
these and many other things, the report con-
cludes with the suggestion that the advisability
or otherwise of combining the 41 municipalities,
and saving the £45,000 a year, and putting the
whole area under one strong municipal govern-
ment with power to make Sydney something
like a decent modern city, be buried in the
trackless wastes of a royal commission. Which
is a lame and shabby conclusion to a
great idea. A royal commission gener-
ally consists of eight or ten Parlia-
mentary bores. They are put on it
because they have nothing better to do ; because
they are small enough to feel flattered at having
the job offered to them ; and because the House
hopes it will keep them out of mischief. And
they will call the 233 aldermen as witnesses,
and ask them solemnly, as men who have
mostly spent all their- lives in Sydney, and
have no idea what a decent-looking metropolis
is like, what they think should be done to
make Sydney a really modern, up-to-date
capital—a capital .like ; the world’s half-dozen
model cities which most of the 233 aldermen
never saw and seldom hear of. So the 233 will
give a valuable and unbiassed opinion on the
subject, and their complete evidence, in eight
volumes, will come out in eight years or there-
abouts. In these volumes they will set forth that
in their opinion cleanliness isn’t so dirty as dirt,
and a straight thoroughfare 99ft. wide is
straigliter and wider than a crooked thorough-
fare,4oft. wide ; and that per cent, interest
isn’t so much to pay as 4J. Why can’t N.S.W.
Parliament deal with the subject direct with-
out the wearisome circumlocution of a Royal
Commission which can only find out at great
length what is already known?
Federation and the Great Tired Feeling 1 .
One thing which every country very much
needs is a periodical cleaning-up of its law—
tlie repeal of statutes which have become out
of date, and the consolidation of the three or
four or a dozen live statutes which bear on the
same subject and cover it with confusion.
Among the many advantages of Federation is
the fact that, on several important subjects, it
will cause a great cleaning-up of the Statute-
Book. The Federal Parliament takes over certain
departments of legislation—the regulation of
banking, insurance, insolvency, marriage, di-
vorce,invalid and old-age pensions,immigration,
exclusion of aliens, conciliation and arbitration
in industrial disputes, &c. In these matters it
must make uniform laws for all Australia. At
present Victoria has the best banking law in
Australia—butitdoes not make the issueof notes
a State monopoly. The Queensland banking
law does make the issue of notes a State mono-
poly and makes partial provision for State
audit. N.S. W., Westralia, and Tasmania have
practically no banking law at all. These pro-
vinces, in the restful Australian fashion, might
fix up some kind of decent statute bearing on
this subject in 10 or 45 years —there is iMtoiiig
to hurry them. But the necessity for a uni-
form Federal law will force the Commonwealth
Parliament to consider the matter —probably
50 years sooner than the last of the provinces
would have done it. It will have to decide
whether or not to extend to all Australia the
Queensland idea of a State-note issue, and the
Queensland partial State-audit as applied to
the Q.N. Bank ; and the Victorian law dealing
with the form of balance-sheet and the respon-
sibilities of directors. It can’t well go back on
the progressive ideas of these two pro-
vinces, so the probabilities are that the
reforms which they have made in banking law
will be applied to Australia in general.
If Premier Turner succeeds in carrying his
Old Age Pension Bill this or next year, or if
any other province carries such a Bill, Federa-
tion will hurry up the spread of this reform
also. Under the Constitution, Old Age
Pensions are a Federal matter. Therefore, if
any one of the Australian provinces has an
Old Age Pension law in force when Federation
begins, or if Maoriland joins later on and brings
its pension system into the partnership, the
Commonwealth presumably must take that
pension system over. Then it must either
abolish the Old Age Pension law where it
already exists (which, in a Legislature elected
by one-man-one-vote is utterly impossible) or
else extend the pension principle all over the
Commonwealth, and the probable result is that
Federation will hurry up this great reform, so
far as some of the provinces are concerned, by
thirty years or more. The Commonwealth will
have to decide on a uniform anti-nigger-
and-Chinaman law for all Australia, where-
by Queensland, Tasmania, and Victoria
will probably get the benefit of N.S.W.’s
Chinese restriction system many years sooner
than they otherwise would ; and in the same
way the arbitration and conciliation laws of
Victoria and S.A. will probably be spread all
over the continent; also their laws to prevent
the influx of foreign criminals, and the S.A.
female-suffrage system. In most of the matters
which are handed over to the Federal authority
there are one or two States that are dis-
tinctly ahead of the rest. The others mean to
catch up some day—next year or 40 years
hence —when they have time to consider the
matter, but under the separate system there is
nothing to compel them to tackle the question
now, or 25 years hence, or at any other fixed
date. But the Commonwealth Government,
having to make uniform laws for all Australia,
must tackle a whole lot of neglected questions,
and the chances are 1000 to 1 that in every case
it will do so by bringing all the provinces abreast
of the one that happens to be most advanced
at the moment. Almost every Lower House in
Australasia believes more or less in female
suffrage, State regulation and audit of banks,
State note-issue, arbitration and conciliation,
regulation of insurance companies, cheap post-
age, old-age and invalid pensions, the shutting
out of undesirable aliens, and the like. And
they all mean to consider them when they have
time, and some outside circumstance forces
them to wake up, and the propertied Upper
House ceases from obstructing. There will be
no propertied Upper House in the Common-
wealth Parliament. The necessity for making
uniform laws will force the Commonwealth to
wake up. And, when this necessity overcomes
the great national Tired Feeling, it is probable
that the first five years of Federation will show
better results in the way of Democratic legis-
lation than 100 years of provincialism w'ould
have done.
Tlie Flyspeck Deadhead.
One hundred pounds a year is considered ade-
quate payment for Tas. members of Parliament.
As an inevitable result of placing ill-paid ser-
vants in positions where they can reach the
cash drawer there is an awful leakage in the
takings, and Tas. M’s.P. are in a fair way to
emulate the exploits of “ Middleton’s Rouse-
about.” The franking privileges allowed the
needy law-makers of the Isle of Sleep are ex-
ploited for all they are worth, and the member
who used to send his washing to the laundry
by free railway seems to have set the fashion
for all present members. It is openly
averred that lodge - officials, who are also
M’s.P., frank all their correspondence through
the Post-office ; invitations to private parties
have been franked by a complaisant M.P.
at the request of his relatives or friends ;
and it is even stated locally that a business firm
has been known to send out its monthly
accounts to customers free-passed at the ex-
pense of the bleeding country. One mercantile
firm with an M.P. on its official staff must have
gained hundreds a year by free telegrams ; and
other businesses have been left-handedlv subsi-
dised in a similar manner by a Government
which doesn’t profess to subsidise any industry
and strenuously opposes “ on principle ” any
interference with Private Enterprise ! So far
is the franking system used that the autographs
of M.’sP. often appear on the private letters of
influential constituents desirous of saving the
cost of a 2d. stamp. The other day in the
Assembly a member had a lot of correspond-
ence tabled concerning a personal grievance
—the Railway Department had objected to
allow his Mightiness to carry free on the line
more than 801 b. of alleged luggage. His claim
was that, as he only used the railway one day
a week, he should be allowed to carry some-
thing like 4801 b. on that day if he wanted to !
Of course, particular instances are not easy of
exact quotation, but figures culled from the
1898 annual report of the Post and Telegraph
Department, disclose a state of deadheadism
unparalleled outside Tasmania. According to
the report there were franked in Tasmania,
during 1897, 1,059,507 letters and packets;
and in 1898, 1,146,598 items of correspondence
were carried at the taxpayers’ expense about 7
for every man, woman, and child on the island.
Reckoned at each (a fair average) this means
a loss to Tas. Postal Dept, of over ,£7OOO a year.
The deadhead is on the increase in telegrams
also. Telegrams franked in 1897, 38,341, value
£4441 13s. 7d. ; in 1898, 47,313, value £4867
Is. 3d. Excluding interprovincial and foreign
messages, the total number of telegrams handled
by the Tas. E.T. Dept, in 1898 was 195,724, so
that the “deadhead” messages for the same
year amounted to nearly one-fourth of the
whole Tasmanian inland business 1 The visible
cost of Raspberry Land’s deadhead is therefore :
Letters, £7167 3s. lOd. ; telegrams, £4867
Is. 3d. Free rides on railways granted
to M’sP., editors of newspapers, and distin-
guished visitors would probably bring the grand
total to £15,000 a year. For a province con-
taining a mere handful of people (170,000), and
whose longest distance doesn’t stretch farther
than from Sydney to Yass, this looks anything
but illiberal encouragement to native industry.
No wonder Tas. legislators profess to consid r
£lOO per annum adequate payment for mem-
bers of Parliament ! If they were paid the
cash value of their franking privileges the som-
nolent Tas. taxpa3'er, when asked to foot the
bill, would suddenly awake and fling his shame-
less deadhe id Parliament down a steep place
into the sea. But being blissfully ignorant of
figures, he mostly stays asleep at present and
dreams of better times. It is very doubtful if
there is one M.P. of all Vandemonia’s 57 whose
hands are clean enough tostop this wholesaleloot-
ing of the public till, for they have all used and
probably abused the privilege. And even if a
member could be found willing to lift up his
voice in opposition to the steal, his constitu-
ents would arise and fire him out with much
indignation as a traitor to their best business
interests, and a disobliging person who tried to
stop influential citizens from turning an honest
Denny. A Northern business firm (say) wants
to get a 200-word message through to its
Hobart agents. A shilling wire is sent to the
local member, who, if Parliament is in session,
is at Hobart: “ Wire us if anything fresh.”
Jones, M.P., replies : “ Barraeouta selling two
for sixpence. Derwent wet. Reply free.” Then
comes the 200-word message “ on the nod,”
and Jones, M.P., hands it to the agents indi-
cated. Meanwhile the telegraph operators and
the head of the “got at” department pray for
relief from the dreadful deadhead, but, so far,
prayer seems unavailing.

The Melbourne Tramway Sweaters.
The Bill introduced by Bromley, M.L.A., fix-
ing the hours of Melbourne tram-conductors at
eight daily, isn’t really anything new, so far as
this clause is concerned. To all intents it
merely re-enacts the agreement on which the
Tramway Co. got its concession, and which
agreement the co. has disgracefully broken ever
since. The proposal to fix the conductor’s wage
at a minimum of 7s. a day is something new,
and the Tramway Co., which has persistently
broken its own contract as regards hours and
overtime, is filling the air with wails about this
“breach of faith/’ It says there was nothing
about a minimum wage in the contract when it
leased the tramways, and it is quite true that
there wasn’t. If there had been, it is practi-
cally certain that the co.’s solemn swear and
promise and word of honor to pay a certain
wage would have proved just as worthless as
its undertaking to observe certain conditions as
to hours and overtime. If the minimum-wage
idea is persisted in, the probabilities are that
the Melbourne Tramway Co. will offer to
accept the idea provided it gets another long
extension of its lease. It has got one extension
already, and it has hinted at a second on condi-
tion that it promises to begin to keep its pre-
vious promise about working hours and payment
for overtime; and it will quite likely want a
third as the price of its promise—probably as
worthless as the other one proved —to pay a
certain minimum wage. Here is the case in a
nutshell:
The Melbourne Tramway Co. pays 9 per cent, dividends
on the capital actually paid up by the shareholders. It
pays only 5 per cent, on its nominal paid-up capital, but
a large part of that is really capitalised profits ; on the
amount which the shareholders actually furnished the
dividend has never been below 9 per cent. A concern
which pays 9 per cent, can hardly plead poverty as a
reason for cutting down wages. In its present agreement
there are certain conditions about hours of labor and
payment for overtime, which conditions have been sys-
tematically ignored. The Tramway Co.’s concession is
in all probability legally forfeitable by reason of this
breach of the terms of the contract. And if the State is
willing to overlook this gross breach of contract, on con-
dition" that the Tramway Co. adheres strictly for the
future to its agreement about hours of labor and payment
for overtime, and also pays its employes a minimum wage
of 7s. a day, it seems a very fair deal.
But if the co. asks that nothing should happen to
it for breaking its agreement about hours and overtime
in the past; and that it should receive a large concession
in the shape of an extension of lease on condition it gives
its promise, already proved worthless, to keep its promise
on this point in the future; and that it should receive
some other special consideration—probably another ex-
tension of time—on condition it leaves off sweating its
employes’ wages, when other employers are required by
law to leave off sweating without any such payment-
well, it is a cheeky and aggressive monopoly, and its
petition should be promptly kicked down the big
marble steps into Bourke-street. The Victorian law has
begun to recognise that sweating is a crime, and if the
Melbourne Tramway Co. expects to be paid for leaving off
crime, instead of being run-in like other people for not
leaving it off, the Melbourne Tramway Co. evidently has
a very high opinion of itself.
That is about how the Melbourne Tramway
difficulty rests at present. It is an eminently
restful difficulty, and always stays in about the
same place.

To Heroes and Others.
An Australian surgeon discourses to The Bul-
letin on the modern rifle-bullet: —
The alleged humanity of the modern rifle-bullet forms
a touching example of public fatuity. “ It just makes a
clean hole,” says the oracle of the bar-parlor ; “ stops a
chap from fighting, you know, but doesn’t smash him
up.” It’s a nice, kindly theory, and encouraging to the
youthful warrior yearning for khakee and glory, but
active-service men tell a different tale. A newspaper
correspondent in Cuba was severely sat upon by the army
chief for giving a detailed description of the American
killed and wounded after a smart skirmish. “Long
strips of flesh were hanging from deep jagged wounds,
and, in some cases, the head or limbs weie mangled out
of human semblance. Altogether, it looked more like
the result of an Afghan skirmish after the women had
been out, than a civilised battle-ground.” This was not
good reading for recruits, and the indiscreet scribe was
muzzled. Weird yarns of deliberate mutilations flew
about, but military surgeons quickly testified that such
injuries were but the expected results of the Mauser rifle
at short range. And the Mauser bullet in all respects is
very similar to the rifle projectiles of other European
nations, Lee-Metford, Lebel, Mauser, or Krag-Jorgensen.
At the Hague Peace Conference a certain von Bruns,
of Subingen, has been attempting to prove that the
English modified bullet is practically an explosive projec-
tile of the kind forbidden by the Geneva Convention in
'6B. The faithful English press has gone for him like a
bull-pup, charging him with a base attempt on the
character of the Dum-dum bullet. They are either
ignorant of, or discreetly mis-remember the fact, that
he refers to the (mark iv.) C, or Woolwich model bullet,
made for service in the Soudan, and which is distinctly
explosive in its action.
The fully-mantled Lee-Metford bullet consists of a core
of solid lead covered by a nickel jacket, or mantle, as it
is scientifically termed. The object of the mantle is to
prevent the lead from “stripping "under the tremendous
velocity and force with which it is shot along the rifling.
It is also supposed by certain persons to prevent the lead
from mushrooming on a bone, or other hard substance, to
the great detriment of the surrounding tissues, but recent
experiments have shown that it is in the habit of parting
company from its core, and either making a track of
its own or being left in fragments along the course of the
wound. Out of it came the Dum-dum, the tip of the
mantle being filed off, so that the soft lead was free to
protrude on impact and make a mushroom-shaped mass.
The result was that the energy of the bullet was all spent
on the part struck, and the alteration of shape, together
with the tremendous velocity, produced a semi-explo-
sive effect of great “stopping" powers. Later on,
another model was introduced for the benefit of
the Soudan dervishes. It was termed the (mark iv.) Gor
Woolwich model, and consisted of a mantled lead projec-
ti’?, containing an air-chamber opening at its top after
the fashion of the modified Snider. As a “ smasher ”it
is unsurpassed, the enormous compression of the air in
the chamber on impact, together with the hydraulic
effect of soft tissues forced into it, exploding the leaden
walls like a powder-charge. It produces a shattering
effect, especially on soft semi-fluid tissues, and competent
authorities have stated that but for its use the British
casualties at Omdurman would have been doubled. The
British bullet won, not the famed British bull dog.
Continental surgeons have come out strong on the
subject. Von Bruns mistook the Mauser sporling bullet
for the Dum-durn in some of his experiments, but he made
no error over the Woolwich model. Esmarch, of Kiel,
states that in future warfare, with this bullet, there will
be no choice but to amputate the limbs of those who have
not bled to death from extensive wounds of the soft parts,
and that “it would be a matter of deep regret should
such barbarous engines of destruction come into use in
European wars.”
A few of the results are striking. Up to 450yds. with
the hollow-fronted Woolwich bullet the entrance wound
was small (sin.), the exit a gaping, lacerated opening from
IJ4 to inches in diam., with long strips of skin hang-
ing from it. Fired through a limb, taking the bone on
the way, it made an exit wound which in many cases
measured eight inches by six ! Out of the great rent
hung shattered strips of muscle and tendon mixed with
fragments of splintered bone, and pieces of mantle and
bullet were scattered for long distances through the sur-
rounding tissues. One shot tore a bullock’s heart to
small pieces, studding the fragments with splashes of
lead and mantle. And so on.
All this sounds very nasty, and the Peace crowd has
made the most of it, to the great bepraiseinent of the full
mantle. An enterprising Frenchman, however, did a few
experiments with the Lebel and regulation bullet, on
horses. He found that, just as with the execrated modi-
fied bullet, muscles were rent into pieces and internal
organs torn to shreds. Lungs were pulped, large bones
splintered, while one shot striking the skull smashed it
wide open and reduced the brain to a sort of soup.
Bullets traversing the body-cavities tore up the internal
organs without actually touching them, and everywhere
the exit wounds were at least three times as big as those
of entrance. Beyer found similar results with the
American navy rifle firing a projectile of 112grs. at 2560 ft.
per sec. of muzzle-velocity. He states that “ wounds
produced by it will have to be treated from the first as
infected injuries,” and prophesies a very high percentage
of mortality from the scattering of pieces of lead and
copper mantle about the wound.
Above 700 or 800 yds. the shattering effect of the modern
bullet is less, but it still persists in considerable degree
up to 1500yds. The majority of hits in actual battle will
probably lie made at medium ranges (600 to 800 yds.) and
at close quarters. The ambulance and surgical arrange-
ments are almost sure to break down under the strain,
and the condition of the wounded in future wars will be
infinitely worse than any of the horrors of Plevna or
Sedan, and this is one of the things about which the War
Office and the Patriotic Press remain discreetly silent,
while endeavoring to run the A.M.S. on an economic
basis.

From Mr. Alfred Deakin :
Dear Bulletin. —The contention that the absence of
Bible readings in Vic. State schools can be traced in our
crime statistics, which you attribute to me, is one that I
have never used, and have expressly' disclaimed. As to
my real contention that religion is an essential of
complete culture, and that therefore its elementary
principles ought to be taught in an elementary'
school (so far as they are common to both Protestants
and Catholics, and so long as parents do not object), is it
not written in last year’s Hansard ? My' motion is simply
Higinbotham’s proposal revived, and aims at unity and
not strife. You have in .vour own province Scriptural
teaching of a much more doctrinal kind than we ask for
in Vic. Your powerful cartoon at our expense becomes
even funnier to those who note that the flaming Gehenna
towards which you picture us as marching is—New South
Wales ! —Yours truly, Alfred Deakin.

The unhappy Baker, who, at Sydney Criminal
Court in June, was tried for murder, and
acquitted as insane, committed suicide in gaol.
This was how it happened, according to
inquest evidence :
He frequently showed suicidal tendencies, and, being
aware of this, at his own request he was placed in a
padded cell at various times, and in addition a warder
was appointed to keep him under observation, assisted by
a prisoner. About. 1 p.m. yesterday deceased was left in
a padded cell with the door open. The prisoner ap-
pointed to watch him, who also carried out duties in the
store, had gone to report himself at “ muster.” On his
return he found deceased lying in the corridor with his
head bleeding, and upon being examined he was found to
be dead.
After that it is an open question whether the
gaol authorities or Baker were the less sane.
Here is a known suicidal maniac put in a
padded cell for safety, with a warder and a
prisoner to watch him. The cell opens on to
a narrow gallery high up in one of the gaol
corridors, and only protected by a low iron
railing. The observing warder is somewhere
else—no one seems to have known where. The
prisoner goes away to answer his name, leaving
the cell-door open. Then the maniac sees his
chance, throws himself over the railing, and is
killed. Orders must be obeyed ; but why (1)
was not the prisoner relieved before going to
muster? (2) was he not excused going to muster ?
(3) was he not ordered to shut and fasten the
door of the cell ? It is not to be supposed that
there was a deliberate plan to give Baker a
chance to kill himself, but many a man has
been hanged on less convincing evidence of
intent. This is the official explanation as it
appears in a Sydney daily paper on July 15
Immediately Baker was seen lying on the floor in a
pool of blood the doctor was sent for. He arrived at the
gaol at about 1.30, and on seeing Baker said, “It looks
like aneurism.” He ordered him to be removed to the
Morgue and washed, and he would see him later 00. D
