Jones's “Physician,” which made its first
Australian appearanoe at Syd ney Royal last Satur-
day, is a rather strong and distinctly gloomy play
about which The Bulletin finds it difficult to
prognosticate. The cultured boniness of it may
catch on with the public, and, again, it is quite as
possible that it won’t. This paper acknowledges
light here that it can’t say whether it loves Jones’s
play or not. It desires to retire into its cave and
mumble these bones at great length, and arrive at
an opinion gradually. The physician, played by
Joseph Game, in a strong, restrained, impressive
*ashion, is a great nerve-specialist whose own
nerves have given way, and he is mooning dis-
mally on the verge of suicide because the married
woman who had been his platonic best girl has
thrown him over. This isn’t cheerful. She deepens
the gloom by calling in and bidding him a frivol-
ous good-bye, and shakes a mocking bonnet-
eather at him, and. leaves him a worse wreck
than before. This isn’t cheerful, either. And
he flies into the country, and loves a simple angel
in white muslin who doesn’t love him—her affec-
tions are fixed on an enthusiastic teetotal reformer
who drinks. He is also a nervous wreck ; only the
furtive look acquired by making sure there are no
snakes in his boots is set down to the consuming
fire of philanthropy. But the specialist pene-
trates Ins secret. He knows the shuffling gait of
the man who is pursued by snakes, and the
haunted look of the man whom snakes walk upon in
the night. So he seizes the drunken reformer
with both hands, and resolves to cure him for the
sake of the girl in white muslin, and they lead a
dreadful existence together—the keeper and his
drunk for six months ; only to find at the end of
it that the teetotal evangelist is more given to
sneaking into public-houses than ever. And this
isn t a very gladsome point in the story either.
* * • .
Also, the woman whom the physician for-
merly loved comes back, having learned suddenly
that she is growing middle-aged and battered, and
getting too old to make new conquests. She cries
to him to take her, body and soul, and keep her as
long as she has any beauty left, and then poison
her; and he brightens up the darkness a
little by telling her, as gently as he can, that he
has quite forgotten her, and wonders how her
painted graces ever attracted him. The ano-el in
white muslin spends most of her time breaking
her heart because her lover doesn’t write —he is too
drunk to write, but. she believes that be is busy
saving souls from gin. The man whose daughter
the intoxicated teetotaller seduced moons around
like a lost soul crying for vengeance—and these
things also have an ameliorating effect on the
auditor. The nice girl at last finds out that her
idol is only beer, and she crumples up for a time
under the shock. The seduced girl comes home
and dies. The burning advocate of total abstin-
ence catches cold while lying in the rain very
drunk on strange drinks in a strange land, and dies
also. And, barring the fact that the deceased’s ex-
gul gives herself to the physician in the end, there
isn t a gleam of joy in Jones’s play from first to
last. It is a strong production, but as cheerless as
a hanged gentleman dangling on a windy night
from the one lone dead tree on the Old Man Plain.
* » » *
The Bulletin is as far from a decision as
ever; in fact, it will gladly give (id. to anyone
who will tell it whether it is pleased with Jones’s
new play or not. Its utter inability to decide
whether it appreciates, or doesn’t appreciate,
Jones’s play is breaking into, its sleep. It likes
Joseph Carne for a dead certainty; his picture of
the still strong man is a gem. And it likes
Brough s incidental old parson immensely ; and
Mrs. Brough’s woman of fashion is a clever
rendering of a character that is almost repulsive.
Mjss Temple doesn’t quite get there as the saint
in white muslin—Grace Noble would have fitted
the saintly part better—but there is no special
cause of complaint. As for Lovell’s intoxicated
teetotaller the apostle with the burning gin-
bottle at his vitals—he is a careful study in
debility, nervous wreckage, and the slinking man-
ners and customs of the person who is always try-
ing to get round the corner unobserved. And if
the advt. of J. P. T. Caulfield’s drink -cure pro-
cess was put on the programme things would be
complete.
** * *
The “Trip to Chinatown” will last till
next Monday night and thereafter Harry Conor's
company will quit Sydney. It won’t even wait to
produce that mysterious “Rag Baby ” which was
announced as its opening production, but which
didn’t come off either then or since. And it is
noteworthy that it opens in Melbourne with the
old and reliable “Trip to Chinatown”; it
tried the other piece (“Stranger in New
York ”) on the dog in Sydney, and appar-
ently one dog was enough. On Tuesday,
Wednesday, Thursday and Friday the “Belle of
New York ” rises up from wherever it has been
concealed and makes its final appearances, and
then departs for its bright home in the land of the
Declaration of Independence. “The King’s
Musketeer ” follows on Saturday (29th July), and
Sydney will probably receive it with great joy.
After a long course of the fluffy musical jumpedy
this should be just about the time when a
meritorious new show, which has really got some
definite remarks to make about itself, will be
welcome.
** # «
“ Our Native Home ” will unfold itself in
many acts at Sydney Lyceum on Saturday, and
for people who like the loud, painted struggle in a
large quantity of tableaux it is safe to say in
advance that they will have lots of struggle for their
money. Personally, The Bulletin would like a
peaceful, domestic drama for a rest. It would
like Charles Holloway to turn the guillotine side-
ways, and fasten a horse to it, and make it into a
ploughshare ; and it would be pleased to see Mr.
Stanford exhibiting his dynamite bomb at the
Agricultural Show as a prize turnip, and his
accomplice, the lost girl, bringing the cow home
in the dusk. There are times, late on a Saturday
first night at the Lyceum, amid the roar and
blither and explosion of the 187th act, when this
scribe feels half inclined to go out-back, and take
up a selection, and grow the gentle pastoral
chookey-hen for the rest of his days, and never
hear another explosion as long as he lives.
* 516 * *
The cannon-ball droppeth like the gentle
dew from Heaven on Cinquevalli’s neck at
Sydney Tivoli, and that wonder-worker con-
tinues to balance the smooth elusive round
thing on the thin end of the unstable long thing
with the same success as heretofore. Apparently,
it will take many weeks and many changes of
programme to exhaust Cinquevalii’s stock of
tricks, and his boom appears good for a long while
to come. Also, he is very well supported ; in
fact, the Tivoli bill just now is an unusually
strong one, all round.

The black people and the brown people
and the people of various shades are doing good
business at Sydney Criterion. A complete change
of programme is advertised for Wednesday, so by
the time this issue appears, the murky Dante will
be producing quite a different collection of articles
out of a different set of impossible places. And if
circumstances permit, and the rain keeps off, and
this powerful organ’s feet are not too bad, it will
endeavor to give further details thereof in next
issue.
** * *
Sydney Opera House closed last Saturday
night with a one-night revival of the “Alhambra
Girl” and one consecutive production of a sketch,
“Into the Night,” written by Mr. Arnold Den-
ham, one of the co. The fragment is rather good,
though somewhat lacking in novelty, and the
staging was effective—the red light of sunset
streaming through the gums, the interior of a
boundary-rider’s hut, and Jack Hardy, the
manager of the next station, who had been bushed,
moralising on his luck in striking the boundary
fence. But Jack was always a lucky dog—the
luckiest man in the country, in fact. He had
dropped into the station in a casual way, and had
become manager thereof and was about to marry
the owner’s only and pretty daughter. (All girls
with prosperous stations are pretty.) Just as he
is about to attack the mutton and damper he
hears a groan and goes out. The sunset-red has
gone, and mellow moonbeams are shining through
the gums now and show a sundowner gasping for
water. The sundowner is an ex-convict who had
served his time with Jack’s father (also a convict)
at Port Arthur, and he tells Jack that he brings
a message from the progenitor. Jack receives the
message his father’s blessing—with just the
amount of reverence that might be expected, and
remarks that the “old man ” must not come
round there. The sundowner fails to see why as
no one need know who and what the old man is.
I hen Jack thinks better of it, and, after promising
the sundowner a billet for life, tells him that
when his father does come he is going to kill the
fattest wether, provide a white shirt and many
large cigars—and damn the odds. This very
proper sentiment and declaration is responsible
for great evil, for the sundowner is Jack’s father •
ana when he hears that statement he solemnly
tells Jack the old man is dead; and when that
v ® r 7 “eeent son is asleep the heroic old man
shoulders Ins swag and goes out into the night to
perish for the drought is on and the dingoes are
howling -rather than blight his son’s prospects.
Mr. Denham personated Jack, and personated
him well, while Mr. Percy Walsh made a suffi-
ciently effective old sundowner. The dingo wasn’t
specified on the bill.
* * * *•
The weirdest public meeting this writer
ever attended was that of ex-priest Slattery, at
Sydney Protestant Hall. The grand-mastiff of
the yellow-pup order was in the chair, with city
magnates and clergymen ranged up behind him.
Ihe hall was packed by a dense crowd, which
proved how fiercely in earnest it was by utterly
scrunching and heaving out in one act the first
objector—nobody was allowed to object there.
Slattery is a tall man, with an aggressive, dome-
like forehead, solid appearance, dry wit, and
biting, incisive sarcasm. He is a good speaker
and rather interesting. In one breath he told
risky stories that shook the hall with laughter,
and m the next invoked the name of Jesus with
impressive solemnity. And —weirdest of all
every mention of that name was greeted with
cheers and wild applauso-it was the first time
this writer ever heard the Lord enthusiastically
encored. At the conclusion, there were given
three .cheers for Jesus, one for Slattery, and
four awful groans for Cardinal Moran.
** * *
Matters at Melbourne theatres are what
they have been for some time past, and will be
until the end of next week. All shows go fairly
strong whilst competition is limited; moreover,
this is the time of year when no manager puts on
a new bill until the old one has quite exhausted
its usefulness. So “ The King’s Musketeer ” con-
tinues a career of moderate success, and “ Straight
from the Heart ” prospers mildly at the Royal,
and the Rickard? management has nothing to
complain of. Paul Pelham, the latest Rickards
importation, is a persevering character-vocalist
who improves on acquaintance after one has be-
come hardened to the fact that Charles Godfrey
was more effective. Which is not to say that
Pelham, for his part, can’t give points to a lot of
other people in the same line of business.

Bland Holt’s season at Melb. Royal will
last until the middle of September. Then Hollo-
way and Anderson take possession of the house
on a ten months’ lease.
** * *
The success of Scherek’s Orchestra at the
Town Hall on Saturday afternoons is a surprise
to the culchawed jeremiahs who lately bemoaned
Melbourne’s lack of taste for high-class music.
Seherek, who is so little of a pedant that he can
get pleasure from some of the singing at Rickards’s,
has created a market for the good music that has
sugar and spice in it. He gives his public what
they can easily appreciate, whereas Marshall Hall
confines his occasional efforts to the performance
of masterpieces which they admire with difficulty
when they try to admire them at all.

Dear Bulletin.— Tell a Roman Catholic
acquaintance that Amy Castles was a Sectarian
Boom from the start, and in nine eases out of ten
the obviously true statement is resented as a sort
of personal insult, instead of being accepted as a
compliment to R.C. esprit de corps. Scotch
Presbyterians, like the Jews, seem to delight in
proclaiming their clannishness, whereas Irish
Catholics get “narked” when told (by an out-
sider) of their disposition to league together.
Why ? —Bendigo.
** * *
The boy, Sydney Smith, who appeared as
the prosecutor of Hoppytub, in “One Summer’s
Day,’’was not bad for a boy, but he might have
k e, " n ii rnuc l ( 1 t more effective. Child characters are
so full of “fat ” that a very ordinary combination
of intelligence and nerve passes for dramatic talent
in a well-trained youngster. This is more especially
the case with girls. Every Cissie Denver that the
writer has seen was voted very clever by the public
in general, though none of them spoke their lines
spontaneously.
** * *
Melb. newspaper desire to boom one after
another of the Firm’s productions is responsible
for absurdly contradictory pars. “The King’s
Musketeer,” for instance, was said to be crowding
the house (which it certainly didn’t) for the first
two or three weeks, and each successive mention
of the show laid stress upon the enthusiastic re-
ception it was getting. Then at the start of the
J , wee k comes this notice in the Argus :
‘ The increasing popularity of ‘The King’s Mus-
keteer ’ was evident on Saturday evening, ” &c.
aA n <^ 10 h an ds of a strong dramatic company,”
&c., “ the play has won more favor at each repre-
sentation.” The original notices, of course, had
left no room for increasing popularity and grow-
ing favor. The journalistic world seems to be
utterly careless of the maxim that a liar needs a
good memory.
** * *
It will be interesting to note the effect of
the Castles Boom on the concert arranged for Satur-
day (22nd) at Melb. Town Hall. This time two
young damsels from Bendigo are coming forward
—Miss Lili Sharp, soprano, and Miss Hansen-
Ivnarhoi, a violiniste—and they are both said to
have a Future, with a capital F. Also, Miss
Elsie Hall, an accomplished and charming piano-
forte player, will appear as a side-light, and
the concert altogether promises a much
better entertainment than the public got for
their money when the Castles Boom was started
on its wild career. Will the multitude fight
for admission on Saturday, and will Mon-
day morning’s musical criticisms in the news-
papers measure the young performers by their
present merits as artists, or their possible develop-
ments hereafter ? Is there a strong demand for
more “discoveries,” or did the recent paroxysm
of credulousness reduce Melbourne to a state of
weary cynicism ? To the writer’s way of thinking
Miss Lili Sharp is being brought out much too
soon after Amy Castles, and Miss Hansen-
Knarhoi, on the violin, will find it hard to seem
as wonderful as Fritz Mueller, the small-boy
pianist, but they may score big successes all the
same.

The acting landlord of the Town Hall
aforesaid seems to have hurt the feelings of John
Fuller, who states that he engaged the building
for three Saturday nights on a verbal understand-
ing that it would not be let to another tenant yet
awhile if he were prepared to renew his lease. The
Myriorama show got good support, and manager
Fuller was disposed to sign a fresli contract when
he found that the concert promoters had jumped
his claim—if it icas a claim under the municipal
circs. Wherefore the Myriorama will shift to the
Athenaeum Hall next Saturday, and the co. will
soothe its savage breast with musio on a liberal
scale.

Miss May Allen, an ambitious soprano
who, the other week, invited the world to hear her
sing at Melb. Town Hall, and who was politely re-
proved for her temerity, is seemingly a difficult
young lady to convince. On Saturday afternoon
she burst forth again at Scherek’s Orchestral Con-
cert, like one lodging an appeal against the
cold, damp notices of her previous appearance.
Miss May Allen needs to be told, once and
for all, that she is a very nice amateur with a
plain ordinary voice. She sings a trifle better
than the average girl who goes up and down the
scale every morning in the front room of a
desirable villa residence. The passing milkboy
would prnbab y remark of her warble that it is
not too bad. But Melbourne is full of not-too-
bad vocalists.

Editor Bulletin.— Re Hosea Easton. A week prior to
his death Air. W. J. Stent, of the American Banjo Club,
applied to all the principal Sydney hospitals for the ad-
mission of poor Easton, who was in a fearful condition,
hut wus refused-on the ground of what 1 Nothing definite
could be gained as to the reason for the refusals. After
sotne consideralile time Mr. Stent—who deserves all credit
for the manner in which he helped to make the poor black
musician s last hours easy—secured his admission into the
institution in w hich he died. And these hospitals are the
ones we help to maintain.—Count Brenn.
The end of Grattan Riggs was dramatic in its rugged
simplicity. The only vehicle available at little, out-of-
the-way Stralian for funeral purposes was a dray, and on
that rustic conveyance, followed only by the members of
his company, poor old man Riggs went to his grave.
Airs. M. L. Huddlestone writes from 46 Lake Place,
llo> lake, Cheshire, England, under date June 8, asking
The Bulletin for news of her husband, William Huddle-
stone, who went to Australia with George Snazelle the
entertainer. lie made the gas and worked the lantern
for Air. Snazelle s music, song and story. After that he
took up his artistic work and had a studio in the Arcade
but he seemed very unsuccessful for a time, and as it is
now two and a-half years since she heard of him, she fears
something has happened to him. Can any reader oblige
with information ?
“S.S. 'iOh, those clothes in the “King’s Musketeer ’!
ho\v they weigh down the harrowed actor and dangle at
his heels ! how desperately they afflict, him in his heroic
moments, and how they overwhelm and abash him when
he is moved to tender sentiment! At least, that is how a
sympathetic spectator feels about it., and it seems to me
that plays of this kind run unusual risks of dismal failure
if only because ihey afflict the audience with the actor’s
anxiety about bis superabundance of purple and fina
linen, and his great waste of velvet and gilt trimmings
The audience feels over-dressed ; it is togged to death "it
gets clothes on the brain. Clothes unmake the man !
Plow many good actors can be anything but uneasy
tailors’ dummies in the full dress of a musketeer’ They
all look to me like the penitent figures of chubby business
men at a fancy-dress ball. But the plays serve a godd
purpose in reconciling us to the hideous simplicity of our
habits, a.d. 1899. J

Little Fritz Alueller has a clever young sister of whom

exquisite sketch in pencil of her brother, cat-chin*' with
absolute fidelity his rapt expression and decided, square
little features. The picture is autographed by Fritz and
bears a few bars of his own composition.

Mephan Ferguson, engineer, boiler-maker, etc.,
of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, has just secured a
contract for the manufacture and supply of all the bolts,
nuts and washers which may he required by the Victorian
railways during the next three years. He has also con-

Treasurer Braddon, of Tasmania, rather late in
the day, supplies a report of the revenue and expenditure
for the financial year ended 31st Dec., ’9B. Thesurplus was
£78,055. This is how Tasmania has been getting ou in
recent years:—
1886 Deficit ...£15,851 1893 ... Deficit ... £129,453

The Tas. deficit was reduced, at the end of ’9B, to £256,321.
Braddon, who looks far ahead, reckons ou a surplus of
£90,180 at the end of ’99, reducing the old deficit to
£166,141. Also, he thinks he sees another surplus of
£98,793 at the end of 1900, reducing the old deficit to
£67,348. He hopes to wipe that off for good and all in
1901. Meanwhile, N.S.W., Queensland, Victoria, and
S.A. are pegging away at their old deficits in a way that
should wipe them off between 1910 and 1940.
* * * *
The Tasmanian Government proposes to reduce
customs taxation to an extent which, it is estimated, will
diminish the revenue by £47,000 a year. It would be
wiser to hang on to every penny of revenue till the deficit
is paid off, and then, as the next most urgent financial
reform, to start paying for the construction of roads and
bridges and other non-revenue-producing public works
out of revenue. Probably the Braddon Ministry hasn’t
courage enough. Yet Tasmania wants that latter reform
most woefully ; it has a very bad record for charging un-
productive woiks to loans in order to make the revenue
account seem less insolvent than it is. How do these
r igures look?
Loan money Loan money
spent on spent on un- Total
productive productive
works. works.
1895 .. £2,318 .. £110,960 ... £113,278
1896 .. 27,000 .. 54,801 .. 81,801
1897 .. 13,376 .. 66,226 .. 79,602
In 1895 Tasmania got through £113,278 of loan money,
and it has only £2318 of productive assets to show for it.
All the rest was used to pay for those non-revenue-pro-
dujing works which ought to be charged to ordinary
expenditure—was used, in fact, to cook the accounts
with, and make things look better than they are. To put
it shortly : £113,278 loan money spent; results : £2318 of
productive works and £110,960 worth of political swindle
and fraud.

* * * •
The N.S.W. Government proposes making
another attempt to sell the land fronting Martin Plane,
Sydney. That strip of land represents £468,975 of
Government money, saying nothing of interest, and the
land at Centennial Park is supposed to stand for £224,366.
These items used to appear simply as part of the deficit,
but they were taken out to make the deficit look smaller.
Probably the Martin Place frontages won’t fetch their
paper-value, but then if the account was debited with in-
terest and land-tax it would swell up so rapidly that they
would never fetch their paper-value. If they brought
£300,090 now, aud the money started to earn interest,
and the land became available for land-tax, it would be a
great deal better than if they brought ±500,000 20 years
hence.
• * * *
Things look rather better with the Bank of
North Queensland this half-year. The receipts for six
months ended 30th June, 1899, were £2555, but of this
£BBO consisted of recoveries from debts written off as bad
when the B.N.Q. made its great cleaning up. This isn’t
really profit; it only means that part of the bank’s capital
which was supposed to be lost, and treated accordingly,
has been unexpectedly recovered. Still it leaves a real
irailing profit of £1675, which is the best result shown
since June, 1897. Last five years’ earnings have been
December, 1894 .. £1,674 June, 1897 .. ±2,003
June, 1395 .. 1,736 December, 1897 .. 1,559
December, 1895 .. 1,182 June, 1898 .. 687
June, 1896 .. 1,703 December, 1898 .. 1,480
December, 1896 .. 1,717 June, 1899 1,675
Reckoning the whole £2555 as profit, this is the best half-
year the Bank of North Queensland has had since 1893.
Reckoning only the £1675, which the hank claims as the
trading profit of the half-year, and allowing for the fact
that all banks have a new source of revenue now through
the imposition of a charge of ss. half-yearly on
every account, things are quite as good as they were six
months ago. Also, the B.N.Q. has got rid of a heavy
burden. The late manager—the unfortunate gentleman
who committed suicide—conceived the remarkable idea of
raising £50,000 by the issue of per cent, debentures
(when money wasn’t worth more than about 3$ or 4 per
cent.) fixed for ten years certain and then redeemable at
the bank’s option at a small premium. And The Bulletin
remarked at the time that it was a very unpromising idea.
The new management has contrived to recover £31,500
of these, paying the premium out of this half-year’s
profits, and greatly reducing the future interest load.
Therefore affairs are really better than they appear on the
surface.
The trading profits represent .£3 ss. Bd. per
cent, per annum on the shareholders’ hinds or £3 7s. per
cent, per annum on capital, which is a better result than
that shown by seven or eight Australian banks. The
entire profit, including recoveries, equals £5 os. 2d. per
cent, per annum on the total funds—rather a high figure
nowadays. The management of the B.N.Q. wisely pays
no dividend. It adds £2OOO to reserve, making it £4OOO,
and the remainder, with previous balance, leaves £622 at
credit of profit and loss. This would be a good time for
the B.N.Q. to make it one of its articles of association
that at least a quarter of the profits shall be carried to
the reserve fund for the next 100 years, and that at least
half of the reserve shall be separately invested in State
securities. Then there would be a reasonably solid
guarantee that the hank, which is beginning to pros-
per again, and has a decent little business and a very
sound cash reserve, would keep on that way.
* * * •
Sydney Morning Herald which rises up at
every opportunity and with very little regard for the
truth, to point out how private enterprise is a success and
State enterprise a dead failure, has the unspeakable gall
to publish this : —“ While there is nothing to justify the
jubilations in which some political partisans of the
Government are indulging over the recently issued report
of the Bank of New Zealand, there is room for congratula-
tion on the fact that it indicates progress toward recovery.
After providing £BO,OOO interest on the £2,000,090 guaran-
teed stock, and £17,500 for dividend at the rate of 3J4 per
cent, on the preferred shares, there is a profit balance of
£93,920 remaining. This compares with £85,032 in the
previous year, being an increase of £BBBB. It is proposed
to appropriate this balance in reduction of dead assets as
follows: Payment to assets board, £50,000; written-off
estates company assets in liquidation, £30,617; written-
off debenture conversion account, £8302. In three years
the bank has written down £204,052. That the bank is
regaining the confidence of the public is gratifying : but
until it gives its shareholders some return after their long
abstinence from dividends, not on their lost capita), but
on the fresh-called money, it is rather too soon to begin
to crow about the merits of Government management.
All experience justifies the conclusion that the sooner it
gets rid of any connection with the bank the better will
it be for the public and mercantile life of New Zealand.
The private National Bank of New Zealand the other day
managed to increase its dividend return for the year from 6
per cent, to 7 per cent. And if without Government support
and prestige it sould do this, surely there is nothing to
boast about State bank management which cannot in the
same country show as great an increase of net profits.”
This utterly unscrupulous misrepresentation (in
fact, it gets beyond misrepresentation, and soars to a high
pin I acle of plain mendacity) is hereby brought under the
notice of the Herald’s moral proprietary. The Bank of
New Zealand last year, under Government management,
had a profit of £’93,920 after paying all charges and inte-
rest on the money which the Government advanced to
put the concern on its legs after private management had
made it utterly and disgracefully insolvent. In other
words, it is earning over 20 per cent, profit on the private
shareholders’ capital—twice as much as the privately-
managed National Bank of Maoriland, and five times as
much as the average earnings of all the privately-managed
banks of Australasia. The only reason why the private
shareholders don’t get dividends is that all these
splendid profits that State-management is now making
have to go to write off the huge losses which
private management made in previous years, and which
the State had to take over when private management
collapsed utterly and miserably. The B.N.Z. earns about
6 per cent, on all its capital (State and private), but after
paying the State’s preference dividend (£‘2,000,000 at 4
per cent, and £500,000 at 3£) there is over 20 per cent,
left on the private capital—or would be but for the losses
made by private management. And the Herald, a moral,
religiously-brought-up paper, which ought to know where
it will go to when it dies if it doesn’t tell the truth, calls
the State management which produces these splendid
results a fai'ure.

N.S.W. Railway Commissioners, asked for their
opinion upon the proposal to build a Govt, line from
Broken Hill to connect with theS.A. system at Coc.kburn,
report that they are not impressed. They think it might
be considered inequitable to interfere with ihe Silverton
Tramway 00. in the manner suggested. The only other
way is to wait for the co.’s 21 years to expire ; and then
Govt, may take over the works on paying nearly three
times the amount of the co.’s profit during the
preceding seven years! As the co.’s profits amount
to putting it very mildly—£Bo,ooo a year, this
would work out at about £l,6oo,ooo—for 35 miles
of narrow-gauge line! The Commissioners are good
enough to add that they could not recommend purchase
under the powers of the Act. In other words, years ago,
in the bad days before Barton’s Bill of electoral reform,
Parliament threw Broken Hill into the frying-pan ; and
now Broken Hill must stay there. But the final word is
not with the Commissioners.
* * • *
Silverton Tramway Co. is busy arguing that it
really isn’t worth the country’s while to interfere with the
gorgeous monopoly which it holds. It has accordingly
published a series of comparisons of its tariff and that of
N.S.W. Govt, railways, and triumphantly calls on the
gods to witness that in most cases its own rates are the
lower. But as the co. quotes the rates for a 35-mile jour-
ney on a N.S.W. Govt, line, the comparisons are value-
less, because the S. A. co.’s really isn’t a separate line of 35
miles ; for every practical purpose—so far, anyhow, as
Broken Hill is concerned—it is simply a 35-mile section of
a through line to the seaboard. A fair comparison
would be made if the co.’s rates were set beside N.S.W.
Govt, rates for the last 35 miles of a 320 - mile
journey. If S.A. Govt, worked a line to Broken Hill,
running at the same rate for the 30 odd miles over the
horder as it charges for the same distance on its own
lines, the freights between the border and Broken Hill
would average one-third of the Silverton co.’s charges; w'hile,
if S.A. Govt, charged for each 35-mine section what the
co. charges for its 35-mile section, the S.A. Govt, rates
would have to go up in some cases 600 per cent. And,
seeing that the trains run right through, with precisely
the same staffs, there isn’t any visible reason why the rate
on one section should be six times greater than it is on
another section. But, after all, these comparisons of
rates are not wanted : the mere fact that a co. owning
only 35 miles of narrow-gauge line, and with less than
one-fifth of the rolling-stock necessary to work its
traffic, makes a profit of nearly £lOO,OOO a-year is
sufficient. The co. shows that, if it carried goods to
Broken Hill for nothing, jam could be only l-15th of Id.
per lb. cheaper, and so on ; but all this doesn’t alter the
fact of the £BO,OOO a-year divs., with additions to reserve.
The co. did not bargain with Parliament for a monopoly ;
and that it has had one for 12 years isn’t a particularly
striking reason why it should always have it.
• * * *
The action of N.S.W. Examiner of Patents, in
refusing the cyanide co.’s application for permission to
amend the Maoarthur-Forrest patent, brings N.S.W. into
line with Victoria. A Victorian appeal is now before the
Privy Council, and presumably the N.S. Wales contest
will go there too. The cyanide people have had mixed
luck in their fighting. In Britain, the patent was
amended ; but in Germany it was quashed. Maoriland
bought the patent’s last four years for £lO,OOO, to save
bother and litigation. In W.A., Q., and Tas. things are
simmering; but the patent goes so far. In the U.S., the
patent is upheld ; but in the Transvaal, the Supreme
Court threw it out.
The point for decision is one on which arguments
are so nicely balanced that equally good Judges can
easily come to opposite decisions. It is pretty generally
admitted that the knowledge of cyanogen’s power to
separate gold was common property 50 years ago; and it
is usually admitted also that the cyanide patentees, Mac-
arthur and the Forrests, were the first to patent a practi-
cal method for using cyanogen to separate gold from its
ores. Unluckily for themselves, they specified one par-
ticular solution, which may or may not be held to embrace
the actual solution in use. Hence they were driven to
try and amend their patent. The patentees have laid
stress on their right to the idea; their opponents have
denied their right to the particular application of the
idea, and have contended that, anyway, they have only a
right to what is included in the language of their patent.
And the patentees say : Look how your industry booms
through the use of cyanide—our discovery. And the
mine-owners retort: If it is your discovery, and in your
patent, prove it. And the patentees say : You ought to
be ashamed to use our process and not pay for it. And
the mine-owners retort: The essentials of the process are
not yours; and hundreds of people would have applied
the idea long ago independently of you. And on which
side abstract justice lies is a point which may be argued
till doomsday.

Bora Creek, N.S.W. : —Veteran John Howell
seems to have got on the track of a good thing in this
locality in a mine named the Conrad. It appears that on
the surface the deposits were heavily charged with zinc,
which practically gave out at a shallow depth, leaving the
vein with good values in silver and lead. Country,
granite.

Mt. Apsley, N.S.W. Negotiations proceeding

syllable from London for over 13 months 1

The tricky Lake George Mines, N.S.W.: Last
monthly return gave an extraction of less than 1 per cent,
copper and the other metals included brought the value
up to £1 2s. sjd. only. Mining and smelting cost
17s. 10|d. ; estimated cost of realisation, 4s. 7£d. p6r ton.
A small loss admitted on the month's work, hut no allow-
ance made for office expenses in Australia and London —
and they are no bagatelle. The low recovery attributed
to all workings being at present in low-grade ore. By the
bye, is it a fact that the ore is now broken by contract at
so much per ton without any stringent conditions as to
value or selection ? If so it might account for a good
deal. What would the mine be if copper were now at last
year’s average price ? The only remaining hope seems to
be treatment of the gossans by cyanide, and although
shareholders have been several times told that this and
that were satisfactory, nothing definite has been given as
to prospective values and profits.

Four Hampden (Cloncurry) puplets were
launched last week, and they are as mixed as this
metaphor. The Hampden people didn’t take up all the

Westralian mines : all N.Q. is pegged for Melbourne
mining men.
* * * *
Wortupa, S.A. —the place where tellurides are
alleged to come from. T. Houghton, who really knows
what he is talking about, described the reef as composed
of carbonates of lime, iron, and quartz full of mineral,
said to be telluride. His assays weren’t sensational.
Sample from cap of lode showed only a trace of gold ; a
quartz-vein running away from it ditto. Another quartz-
vein, showing gold and mineral, assayed a little over an
oz., and this sample exhibited a few specks of what is
believed to he telluride of gold. Only a few telluride
specimens were brought down, and two of these were
stolen ere they had been long on show—doubtless not for
their intrinsic value, hut for boom purposes. Four co.’s
incubated in a less number of days. Scrip printed and
sold, but the boom didn’t last a week. Adelaide people
prefer Stock Exchange mining to anything else. They
are born gamblers, and will never get over their magnifi-
cent Broken Hill and Kalgoorlie luck,
• * • •
Adelaide having “gone off” one telluride find
has started another. This latter is at Wilgena, 300 miles
N.YV. of Port Augusta; Capt. Matthews, Govt. Geologist,
has been despatched there. The show “is highly
mineralised, and is nearly in a direct line with Kalgoor-
iie.” Alas ! nothing now seems to be in a direct line with
Cooigardie, or with the White Feather, or Kurnalpi, or
any of the other dead-cat places of the great cat-land,
yet they are all within easy distance of the camp that
wears the Telluride Crown of Glory—Kalgoorlie. Also,
said that mining men often remarked that this particular
spot was very much like Kalgoorlie in many ways, and
the discovery revived an old yarn. Newspapers, Adelaide
brokers, and other boomers, are quite satisfied that this
is the place referred to in the tale. Many years ago a
black-boy and a man knocked off a lot of gold with a
tomahawk. The man had to go to England to see a dying
brother, and, when he returned to Adelaide, he died
without being able to give any information that would
direct anybody there. Nothing is said of the black-boy.
Also, there is a yarn about Wilgena being most
assuredly identical with the mysterious place whence an
Adelaide resident long ago got much gold : he pegged out
without locating the spot—he only waved his dying hand
to his stepson, or somebody, faintly whispering the
while : “Go North West! ” Well, that was more or less
original, anyhow. “Go West, young man,” is quite a
chestnut.
* • * •
The finders of the telluride gold at Worturpa
(S.A.) were two brothers named Stubbs who had only been
prospecting for a few months. What may be a valuable
field was made known through the prospectors mistaking
the telluride for coal. As it is, they have almost entirely
parted with their interest in the mine—since floated at
big prices—for a few hundreds.
The rush for S.A.’s new alleged goldfield pro-
duced some funny experiences. Several capitalists from
Adelaide went up with the warden, but in the long and
intensely trying scrub journey three of them disappeared.
The man who stuck to the warden, over the boulders and
up the creeks, seemed to have the best of it, but he found
on arrival that another had been shown a desperate kind
of short cut over the range, and had bought out the
original prospectors. This first-comer, before buying,
very honestly showed the prospectors the assay of their
stuff, 270 z. to the ton, which had caused the rush, but of
which they had not heard before. The other two who
tried a short cut got bushed, and found the whole place
pegged-out when they reached the field. One of them
obtained a lift on a donkey, was thrown off, broke a rib
or jtwo, and finally despatched a wire to the city con-
demning the whole show.
• » • *
The Bulletin office often has a lot of ore in
sight, and though the practice of sending it in has never
beeh encouraged, this paper receives too many requests
for gratuitous assays and opinions as to value'and treat-
ment of samples submitted. Arrangements have now
been made for testing, by assay and otherwise, samples of
ore, provided the sum of £1 is sent with each to cover
cost. Assays alone do not serve the miner’s purpose,
hence certificates supplied to Bulletin correspondents
will not only give a statement of metallic contents —
owners will he advised by an experienced, practical metal-
lurgist as to the best methods of treating the ore in bulk,
whether by wet or dry crushing and amalgamation, smelt-
ing, cvaniding, chlorinating, etc. Parcels should not be
less than lib. nor more than 101 b.

• • • *
Real humorists are the mining oracles of the
Sydney evening papers. Thus the News on rumors : “ A
red-hot Westraliaii market between now and midsummer
—a big deal has been made in Associateds, and when the
supreme moment arrives it is going to pay the ss. div.”
Queen Margarets, which were up to 365., are going there
again. It hears nothing but good of Bayley’s from “ a
man who has been down the mine, and who is in touch
with moneyed people.” Declaims that the reason for that
all-round slump is unaccountable, except the presump-
tion that “ bears ” in London are working Kruger’s
obstinacy for all it is worth. It also wants to know why
Chaffers and Biulder Main Reefs should be so depressed.
And so on. Now, the Westralian market is having its
“ red-hot ” boom in midwinter, anti to call present market
values depressed is absurd, as the News readers will
presently find. Chaffer’s, for instance, at 225. 6d., repre-
sents haif-a-million of money—as the value of a mine with
an area of but 12 acres which has practically never had a
crushing, much less a div. It developed tosooft. before it
found pay ore, and even at this level the rich ore is con-
fined to narrow streaks, and its bulk is doubtful. Chaffers
has nothing but position to recommend it, and half-a-
million is a lot too much for that. Queen Margarets went
to 365. on the striking of a bonanza, and, as soon as the
smallness of the pocket was exposed, they went back to
225. 6d , which is very fair value. About time that papers
recognised that the judgment of the collective markets
of Australia and London is better and more reliable than
that of the horse-reporter.
* * * •
The Wilfley concentrator has only been a short
time before the mining public, but has already demon-
strated its superiority 7, over 1200 being sold within the
past two years. This machine has a capacity of from
25 to 30 tons per day, and will save more sulphuretts,
float gold, amalgam or quicksilver, and make a cleaner
and better separation than any belt, concentrator made.
It can be easily and rapidly adjusted while in motion, and
requires no expert attention. The Austral Otis Engineer-
ing Coy., South Melb, have been appointed sole manufac-
turing agents, and are now engaged constructing machines
for the colonies. The price of the machine is ±ll5.

* * * *
“Tailings” writes from Maoriland From
enclosed cutting you will see that, for week ending 19th
June, 27 public dredges got 12700 z. gold, or an average of
470 z. per dredge, and this particular week is only an
average one. As the biggest dredge working can pay all
exes, on about 9oz. per week, it can be easily seen what
bonanzas some co’s are. Local failures are few and far
between—they number, I think, only three out of our 70
private and public concerns. Many public co’s by the
end of present year will have paid back the whole of their
capital in divs., and still have from 12 to 15 years work
ahead of them. Writer reckons that over £600,000 worth
gold is being won per annum by Otago dredges alone ;
better still, all profits, including those on the building
of the dredges, go into our own people’s pockets and not
into J. Bull’s.

The next big gold boom appears likely to take
place in Rhodesia. On 30th Sept, last 130,000 quartz reefs
had been registered, extending over an area of many
hundreds of square miles. This district is said to be full
of old workings, variously attributed to Arabs, Phoeni-
cians, and Portuguese. The caps of the reefs had simply
been removed by these old workers, and the claims aban-
doned when water became troublesome. Average yield of
gold so far, 14jdwt.—without cyanide. Tailings can be
treated (so Cecil Rhodes says) by cyanide at a cost of
ldwt. per ton, and so induced to yield another 4 or sdwt.
Some lodes are said to be of enormous size. Sounds mild,
though, after Klondyke.

The weightiest influence against the
stablishment of a County Council for Sydney
and suburbs is the dexterous work of several
council-clerks. These see in a County Council
what certain anti-Jbederahsts see in federation —
loss of office or risk of displacement—and they
work underground and overground to block
Destiny.
That ghastly show, Coolgardie Exhibition,
has fizzled out at last. It was open for four
months, and was attended by 70,000 people in-
cluding deadheads and season-ticket holders.
More attended one of the big test matches in
Sydney or Melbourne, and yet Morgan, M.L.A.,
President of the Commission, gravely asserted
that through the Exhibition —
Attention had been called to the resources of the colony
by advts. distributed all over the U.S. and Europe, and
to-day hundreds of thousands of men who previously
knew nothing' at all about the colony were well acquainted
with its resources. This had all" been gained by the
establishment of the Exhibition.
Thus, according to Morgan, Westralia owes little
or nothing to its marvellous output of gold, but
everything to a paltry little tin-pot show —
despised and rejected even by the Sandgropers
themselves.
Speaking at the interment of the aforesaid evil-
smelling Exhibition, John Forrest said that the
money it had cost the country had been most
judiciously expended. The £20,000 or so which
HE had advanced the Commissioners had served
to bring Westralia prominently before the civilised
world, and it was triumphantly proved that the
province could now produce everything for the
sustenance of her population as well, if not better,
than the East. At Bunbury, only a day or two
ago, Victorian butter, Victorian bacon and cheese,
Tasmanian potatoes, South Australian jams, and
Parramatta oranges were the only samples of pro-
duce available. Butter was ticketed la. Bd.,
cheese Is. 3d., bacon Is. 2d. per lb. ; Parramatta
oranges (inferior), Is. fid. to 2s. per doz. ; eggs
(from Adelaide), Is. fid. to Is. lOd. per doz. Apples
are prohibited as an article of import, and a few
common cooking sorts were labelled Is. per lb.,
pears ditto. Bunbury is considered W.A.’s best
farming district, and is the Premier’s own con-
stituency. Why these fairy-tales ?
♦ * * *
A “survivor” of the Excelsior-Edina
collision tells a Bulletin man this little story.
All the Excelsior’s passengers were taken aboard
the much-battered Edina, and the steamer
returned to Melbourne. Shortly after she started
for home, informant noticed a man looking about
amongst the crowd with ill-disguised eagerness.
Presently the seeker came face to face with a
woman who was also seeking. The man’s face
fell a yard. “Just my cursed luck!” he said.
“Just mine, damn you !” answered the woman.
• * * •
The most excited man on board the
Excelsior when she was cut down by the Edina in
Hobson’s Bay last week was a large, raw-boned
Caledonian who was discovered wrestling energeti-
cally with the wreckage of the big awning. A
couple of sailors went to his assistance, thinking
from the mortal agony expressed in the face of the
man that his wife and family must have been
pinned down by the ruins. A section of the
wreckage was lifted, and the man went on his
knees, and dragged from under a seat a well-wotm
mackintosh. “ That’s all richt the noo an’ thank
Y e J me men.” he said,

“H.”: Wentintoapawnshoprecently. The
occupants were a little grey-headed Jewess
and a tall, powerful man with an Irish accent.
She was so decrepid that she could hardly
walk ; he was in the prime of his strength,
and a regiment of him would represent a lot
of agony to an enemy, but he stood pleading I
to that decrepid old woman to give him one
week’s grace in which to pay the interest on
a family relic, which he declared was of great
sentimental value. Also, it was mortgaged
for only one-sixth of its intrinsic value. The
little old woman behind the counter, with her
wizened face screwed into a smile like a
crumpled promissory-note, kept on laughing
a little dry laugh and repeating, “It ees not
piziness.” It was a work of political economy in a
nutshell.

* ■ * * ♦
Although there was a large demonstration
by friends in court whpn Flanagan got acquitted
for the third time on a charge of embezzling An-
drew Rowan’s money in days of vore, Melbourne
has regarded the whole Rowan-Flanagan matter
with a cynical eye thus far. Considering the “ pri-
vate and confidential ” pamphlet which Flanagan
hurled at the character of his former employer and
colleague, there was nothing inhumanely vindic-
tive in Rowan’s scheme of revenge. The one furi-
ous litigant asked the public to believe his ex-
mate guilty of dishonest financing, and the other
retaliated by bringing his accuser before a jury on
similar charges. It now remains with Flanagan
to convict Rowan of malicious— i.c., unreasonable
—prosecutions, and, when he has landed a verdict
for heavy damages, the public at large will pat the
acquitted “martyr” on the back—but not till
then. And, on the other hand, if Flanagan does
not bring his adversary to court, there will be no
feeling of sympathy for Rowan unless he, for his
part, can convict Flanagan of criminal libel in
publishing the pamphlet aforesaid. And there is
no visible reason why the public should sym-
pathise much with either side of the cat-fight.
* * * *
The Rowan-Flanagan scandal has pro-
voked a new edict from the Vic. Attorney-General
prohibiting the appearance of Crown Prosecutors
in private cases. Finlayson, the much-“ but-
tered ” C.P., held a brief for Rowan at the Police-
court, and continued the attack upon Flanagan in
the Criminal Court as representative of the
Crown. Which meant that Finlayson, after
accepting his client's biassed views and fat fee,
was called upon, in his higher capacity, to say
whether the Crown should take up Rowan’s case,
and he naturally decided in the affirmative, with
the result that a lot of public money was wasted
on abortive prosecutions. Defenders of the old
system argued that it would be impossible to get
able men as Crown Prosecutors if they were re-
stricted to Crown business, but in these times of
a crowded legal market and keen competition the
steady employment offered by the Crown billets
should command plenty of talent quite up to the
Finlayson standard.

“ What is the Melbourne Women’s Hos-
pital fussing about ? ” exclaims a Bulletin cor-
respondent. “ If they don’t want girls and
married women to fight about their babies’ com-
plexions, why don’t they settle matters by placing
them in separate wards, the same as in Sydney ?
That may be as bad as ticketing them, but some-
how nobody has made any trouble yet. The Ly-
ing-in Hospital at Sydney is not in a very nice
place—it is situated at the back of the Benevolent
Asylum, on the left wing, as you go in ; but still
they have separate wards, and one gets as good
attention as the other. And separate wards are
much more pleasant for cases of this kind. Girls
who cannot claim their husbands—the husbands
given to them by love—talk and sympathise with
each other about that pleasant day out, and where
each couple went, and how they planned for the
day’s outing. And as for the married ladies
bound by law, they talk about their husbands and
what great positions they held one time, and how
they would not be here only for reduced circum-
stances. There are more reduced circumstances in
Sydney Lying-in Hospital than in any other place
of its size that I know of. Some day, I suppose,
the people who are bursting with benevolence will
discover that the separate-ward idea is invidious
and savors of Old Cattism, and the row between
the old cats who want to keep the unmarried
mother apart, and the young cats who don’t, will

Dear Bulletin : There are two sides to
almost every case. Recently many people reared
up and snorted at the infamous conduct of the
committee in labelling the beds at Melb. Women's
Hospital “married” and “single” respectively,
according to the status of the occupant. These
democratic souls were so determined that no
woman who had found a baby for her cradle
before she had a wedding-ring for her finger should
be in any way reminded of the fact, that they
joyfully insisted that every respectable married
woman who had to take advantage of the hospital
on account of poverty should have to endure the
ignominy of being suspected to be a “loose'
woman. A very large proportion of the women
who go to the Women’s Hospital to be confined
are Totties ; and if the exceptions are not distin-
guished in any way, in course of time it will he
regarded as a proof of having passed through a
period of Tottiedom to have been an inmate «.,

BABY O’CALLAGHAN’S DAD.
In reading the newspapers, maybe,
You noticed the case of a nurse
Who took in a ninety-pound baby
And sent it away in a hearse ;
It died of acute indigestion—
An ending too commonly sad—
And hence rose the pertinent question
As to Baby O’Callaghan’s Dad.
The Coroner boldly protested
His right to an open reply,
So the taciturn nurse was arrested
For winking her sorrowful eye
At the public, instead of disclosing
The name of the party who had
Supplied her with cause for supposing
He was Baby O’Callaghan’s Dad.
But, loud as the Coroner snorted
Before the historic event,
The Truth he had openly courted
Quite staggered that truculent gent,
The news put a stop to his fury,
Though all the reporters grew mad
When he kept back the name from the jury
Of boy Baby O’Callaghan’s Dad.
The inquest was speedily ended,
And “ mum ” was the Coroner’s word.
The name, which he rashly intended
To publish, has never been heard.
The Law is again disrespected,
The friends of Corruption are glad—
And a person whom no one suspected
Is poor Baby O’Callaghan’s Dad.
He’s a dark, very dark-looking, stranger,
And “ dotty ” on one of his feet;
His glance is a warning of danger
To any he happens to meet;
There’s a sulphurous smell where he lingers ;
His figure is gaudily clad,
And he wears little claws to his fingers
Does dear Baby O’Callaghan’s Dad.
Melb.
E.F.

* * * *
“Gunner”: Be N.S.W. artillery-horses
(“Limber,” B. 15/7/’99). The excuse that they
don’t get enough exercise is nonsense, and the
acceptance thereof by Commandant French is self-
condemnatory. The Commandant, himself an
artilleryman (but without field experience), should
know that English regulations ordain three bat-
tery drills a week in marching order ; and that,
on other days, the horses shall be taken for exer-
cise in “ watering order.” If the men and horses
get drill enough to keep them in practice, there
will be no fear of their not having sufficient work.
English cavalry officers, who buy their own
chargers, are nevertheless not allowed to either
drive or “ hunt ” them.
* * * *
Half the army of N.S.W. is wild (accord-
ing to the daily papers) to distinguish itself in
South Africa, but whether Bull, after his experi-
ence with the Soudan Contingent and the Lancers,
is equally anxious to accept its services is
another question. Some of the volunteers pro-
fess readiness to take English pay; but if they
are not also willing to submit to English service
conditions (against which the N.S.W. Lancers
have been violently kicking) they would be worse
than useless. (
The N.S.W. Lancers at Aldershot are not happy.
One little wail to London E. News reporter
We hardly seem to be treated as we ought to be. I
don’t think much of Aldershot and its arrangements.
Remember we had to plank down 20 sovereigns, and
throw up our berths, a lot of us, too, to come here. All
we get back is Is. 2d. a day.
But J. Bull undertook only to treat the N.S.W.
warriors as he treats his own, and those 20 sove-
reigns didn’t go into his pocket, nor did he ask
them to throw up their billets. Also, the Lanceahs
complain that their horses (Bull’s horses, which
he lent the/.i) are “picketed in the open.” Our
experienced veterans don’t seem to know that
such is the custom with cavalry horses in the
field, and they are growling also about the English
climate, because they, or the people who sent
them, had not the common forethought to provide
suitable clothing.
Despite all that is said about Tommy Atkins’s
hard lot, the British army is by far the best fed,
housed, clad and paid in Europe. It is, in fact,
practically the only one that is paid, and to judge
by the advertisements in the Sydney papers,
there are hundreds here who would be glad of
Is. 2d. a day and keep, even if it involved a little
danger. Better, at all events, than 12 hours a
day on a. farm for 3s. 6d. a week.
“Tassy”: Launceston (Tas.) people are
a “ near ” lot. Every Sunday evening one of the
local bands gives a concert in the City Park, and
the gates used to be left open, with collection-boxes
thereat. The collections, however, were so skinny
that turnstiles were introduced, with result that
the public stays outside with great unanimity and
listens to the music from there. Not long ago a
visiting lady-entertainer gave a Sunday lecture on
the Passion Play, but, though the attendance re-
gistered was 1352, no fewer than 803 of these
planked “ tray-bits,” the rest being mostly coppers.
• » * »
At a big church in Ballarat a large dia-
phragm is reared near the parson, and tubes carry
the sound of his voice to the pews for the incon-
venience of deaf worshippers. There is no rest
for the wicked —even in church.

Clutha (M.L.), being a prohibition dis-
trict, except for wholesale licenses, liquor has to
be imported in two-gallon vessels. Every week
there is an exodus of “ returned-empty ” demi-
johns by rail. Temperance-ranter Isitt had just
got off the train there when someone told him a
deputation awaited him at the other end of the
platform. He hurried off to find that the deputa-
tion consisted of the collection of “returned
empties ” —about 200 in number. Apropos, a
man recently collected two tons of quart bottles at
Balclutha. The place, as already noted, being
prohibitionist, they must have been used to hold
eucalyptus-extract or castor oil.
The M.L. Cold-Tea cranks have been taught a
lesson by their defeat of three years ago. Instead
of trying to sweep the whole country, they are
concentrating their forces on one part alone, Tara-
naki. They have chosen their ground well. On
the North it is bounded by the already-prohibited
King country. The East is shut in by unpeopled
bush, and the only road leading into the province
from the South passes through Patea. They have
good material to work on. Taranaki was origin-
ally a Nonconformist settlement, and its popula-
tion of soulless cockatoos is subject to frequent
fits of revivalism. The Cold-Tea party has been
working them for all they are worth. For months
past the district has been perambulated by agita-
tors of all three sexes—men, women, and parsons
—and before the November elections all the big
temperance guns are to be trained on to the spot.
Even among non-prohibitionists the feeling is
largely, “ We’ll give it a trial, anyway.” So that
next election “No License” will' probably be
carried in Taranaki, and cocaine, morphia, chloral,
and sly grog will hold high carnival among the
Cowjerkers.
« * * *
S.M. Herald has at last determined on
publishing a serial story. When Melb. Argus
adopted this course some three years ago there was
a great outcry, but wisdom was justified of its
child, and the novel-publishing has been kept up
ever since. The Herald is the last big Australian
paper to take to the serial business—the idea
being to catch the feminine “ vote.”
* ♦ * *
Strange how the “great” Australian
dailies have climbed down, for the sake of the
pennies, to habits and customs that formerly they
affected to abhor. Every pressman of 20 years’
standing remembers when Melb. Argus looked
on “interviewing” as a low-down American
custom, reprehensibly introduced by the evening
papers to Australia. Now the Argus does it—
and does it badly. The young Australian native
has no notion of a slashing, photo.-like interview,
wherein a graphic general impression is produced.
His idea is to take the man’s depositions, and
make him ask the questions as well as answer
them. “ How do I like the colonies? Well, you
see,” &c. “Do we expect to begin operations
soon? Probably about,” &c., and so on, with a
dismal hunk of “solid” matter between each
batch of questions.. They might as well ask the
interviewed to write a letter to the editor. In
most cases the young man who interviews has no
ideas of his own to work on (except a few concern-
ing cricket or football), and thus the “interview ”
in the mighty Australian dailies is generally very
dry hash.
J • » » •
Query : Supposing the Victorian law
authorities do find, as they seem likely to do, that
Deverill, sentenced to three years for forgery at
Rochester, was really in Adelaide at the date of
the alleged crime, how many years will the people
who “ identified ” him so cheerfully get for per-
jury? How many years does the Crown witness
ever get for perjury in such cases? When a Vic.
prisoner swears to Ids own innocence, and the jury
finds that he is innocent, and therefore that
he told the truth, the Vic. law' very often
has a second shot at him on a charge of falsely
swearing to his own innocence—which is really
trying him twice for the same offence. But when
a Crown witness swears to an accused person’s
guilt, and the jury finds that that person is inno-
cent, and therefore that the Crown witness swore
to a lie, what happens to the Crown witness ?
Nothing at all.

“F. 0.” : Some women are fearfully and
wonderfully sudden. Writer recently “ assisted ”
at accouchement of a lady in a Richmond (Melb.)
street, at 9 o’clock in the evening. Said assist-
ance consisted in bringing a nurse on the scene
and coercing a passing carter to carry mother and
infant to hospital. Two cabbies refused the job ;
also two alleged women in adjoining houses
refused the loan of a blanket, and slammed their
doors in writer’s face, remarking that “it served
the hussy right.”

“AUSTRALIA FOR THE AUSTRALIANS"
Concerning yet another N.S.W. free news-
paper-train :
Immediately after last N.S.W. Referendum “Mister
Jamks,” of S.M. Herald, sent an emissary to Maitland,
where public meetings were at once held, at the Herald s
urgent request, with the object of asking Govt, to give
the “ great daily ” (or dailies) a free, early-morning news-
paper train to Newcastle-Maitland district. And now the
Herald people heartily wish they hadn’t called any
meeting, for instead of getting a unanimous vote in favor
of the suggestion, as was expected, the meeting hit
straight out and angrily stated that tbey didn t want any
suchtrain. Their own papers had just as much news as
the Herald, and a free, early newspaper-train from
Sydney would mean onlv bigger dividends to the Fair-
faxes and others. If the Herald proprietary wanted
their papers delivered at an earlier hour they were quite
at liberty to charter a special train—at their own ex-
pense. The meeting (a very large one) also hoped that
the free - postage of newspapers would be speedily
abolished. Besides, a lot of similar indignant remarks
were made, which clearly expressed the meeting’s
Strong disapproval of free newspaper-trains at the tax-
payers’expense. As far as the S.M.H. is concerned, it
was a plain case of “ didn’t know it was loaded.”
The Newcastle and Maitland papers are far
ahead of the rest of the N.S.W. country press, the main
reason being the accidental disposition of the railway
time-table which saves them from the interference of the
Protected-Freetrade metropolitan daily papers, which
have utterly squashed the Southern and Western press.
S.M. Herald’s coup in running a special train on June
21st, when Newcastle received its Herald at 7 a.m.
instead of 1 p.m. as usual, was an attempt to secure an
early-morning train North at the expense of the Treasury.
The Newcastle papers are very bitter at the Sydney -
paper-train petition now being hawked round the coal
city, and are fighting the proposed “ small and early”
all they know. But it will doubtless be a losing battle,
for the Sydney dailies are all powerful. No industry is
so well protected as that of the Sydney Freetrade apostles,
and no one can fight so hard for their vested interests.

Anastatia Butler, daughter of the Butler
whose charred corpse was found in his hut
at Mansfield (Vic.), is to be formally prose-
cuted for parricide. Taciturn ’Statia was taken
into custody during the inquest proceedings,
but the coroner’s jury found “ no evidence to show
how deceased came by his death,” and so the
suspect went free. The new proceedings seem to
have been started on the assumption that ’Statia
knows all about the tragedy and can be soared
into making a statement. She was apparently a
kind daughter to the old man ; the idea that she
murdered him is not warmly entertained, except
by policemen ; and seeing that the Crown has
practically no chance of proving its case on any
evidence but her evidence, the wretched woman
looks very like the victim of police chagrin. They
reckoned on getting her committed for trial by
the coroner’s jury, and the open verdict hurt their
feelings.

Sunday morning was sitting, with some 20
others, in a Sydney wine-shop, when knock
came to door, and in an instant gas was out
and most of us were for getting over back
fence. But I recognised d’tec's voice, so gas
was re-lit, and two D’s were admitted, and
joined in. We carried on for an hour after
they’d gone, and the boss told me he’d be
“ damned sorry if these D’s stopped coming,
for so long as they came no one else would.”
Again, there’s a city pub., not far from the
water, where you can see the plain-clothes
traps drinking any Sunday. Some day, of
course, this Bung will be put away, too, but
meantime he’s coining. I can get liquor in at
least 25 city pubs, on Sunday ; and, as for
Manly, Watsbn’s Bay,' Coogee, and Bondi,
the stranger need not even knock, but can
walk right in and get what he wants. Also,
I can walk into any one of a dozen Sydney
oyster-shops on Sunday evening and buy
beer with my meal in defiance of the Licens-
ing Law and a £3O fine. Often see half-
a-dozen bottles of beer down at once on as
many tables, in a place right on the main
street. No secresy about it —sometimes
I even see plain - clothes policemen eating
!\ at the tables. This is as it should be ; but
why all the cant and humbug? In
Singapore, licenses are Ist, 2nd, and
3nl class, according to whether the
licensee closes at 10, 11, or 12. He
p:>.ys acoording to his means and
profits pro rata. —C.P. *

“ Pab.” on widows and spinsters :
As to the widow’s success in bagging a husband where
the spinster fails, this may throw light on the subject.
Recently a man had his leg broken three days before date
fixed for his marriage with a widow, whereupon his
fiancee, after seeing him safely tucked in, went straight
and made all arrangements, and the wedding came oft
next day, the groom lying in bed. She gave as her
reason for the haste that she would be free to nurse him
back to health as his wife, while as his best girl
she could onlv call and send him flowers and
peaches and things. The average spinster would have
shuddered her front teeth out at the idea of such a
proceeding; but the widow wasn’t going to take the
chances of a long recovery, and the obvious dangers of
the sympathy of other girls also with flowers and peaches
and things. With a widow, in short, marriage is not
sentiment, but business. The behaviour of a M.L.
damsel is a sample of how the spinster loses her head at
the approach of marriage. She was 19, and had written
for her father’s consent, and as this hadn’t arrived on the
morning fixed for the wedding she attempted suicide.
If a man on the brink of marriage be capable of reason
at all, he would surely reflect that such an irresponsible,
iinnatient idiot would not make the best of wives. But
the man doesn’t reflect, and it is to his temporary in-
sanity, more than to her own deserts, that the maiden
owes her chance of marriage at all.
The supreme touch of humor in
the awful Melbourne tart-eating com-
petition has escaped notice. Two
vouths stuffed themselves with jam
tarts for a wager, and the prize to the
champion who ate most was—a belt! A
seidlitz-powder would have been a reasonable
prize, but the idea of offering a belt to a youth who
has just eaten nearly thirty tarts !

“Searchlight” to The Bulletin re the
Gregory case: —
“ Gumleaf ” is quite correct (B. 15'7/’99) in his statements,
but he overlooks the fact that barrister Gregory was in the
asvlum in ’92 or ’93 for a few months. I had business
dealings with him for 12 years, and went out to see him
in May ’9B, on being told that there “ was a perfectly
sane barrister in Kew.” Without professing to know any-
thin" of his sanity I can say his memory was unimpaired.
He "perfectly recalled dozens of transactions and the
fio-ures and dates of same. He complained bitterly that
his brother-in-law (C. A. Topp) being the permanent head
of the department of asylums, as Under-secretary, he
could not get fair-play. He seemed quite harmless but
got into disfavor over his fondness for carrying a revolver.
The statement that it was even possible for his brother-in-
law to intercept all his appeals for an examination should
be enquired into. If true itis a scandal— though no reflec-
tion is here made against Mr. Topp, whose reputation is
that of an honorable and compassionate man. Gregory said
to me when I visited him : “ at the best of cricket matches
I could only take an hour’s interest, but here they expect
me to get wildly delighted when some lOth-rate visiting
teams play the warders. I never cared for any but high-
class concerts, but now I am termed ‘depressed if I
don’t laugh at the amateur Christy minstrel troupes that
come to amuse us.” May he get out and keep out till he
harms somebody, say I. The class of cranks he had to
associate with were enough to madden a Solomon.

The recent jewel robberies in Melbourne
were probably managed by a man and woman
who haunted the vicinity of Denis Bros.’ shop for
some days previous to the successful snatch, and
have not been seen around since the event. The
woman—an attractive, well-dressed mystery—was
sometimes in the man’s company, though at other
times they passed one another like strangers.
