A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, July 18, 1899.
Mr Dear Mtee,—
The week’s gaiety has been unusually Catholic,
even for Sydney, where we do as we like to an
extent unknown in other certain places. Even to
wearing a Beauchamp tie with your hammer-claw,
as witness somebody at the Cardinal’s evening.
That was the Cardinal’s reception for Dr. Carr.
An uglier building than St. Mary’s Hall on a wet
night can’t be imagined by any dull person who
wasn’t asked to the reception. And those who
were there will never'want to imagine the degree
of ugliness that outbids that hall’s cast-iron roof
and rafters. Also, long refreshment-tables run-
ning down one side of the room gave an up-
country-party solemnity to the beginning of the
“At Home.” “It will not be used any more
when St. Mary’s is finished,” one jolly little priest
explained to a Melbourne lady with a temporarily-
turned-up nose. “ When will that be ? ” said she.
“ When you and I are in heaven,” he said. That
stopped her. It pulled her up and pulled her
nose down. For the most rollicking brogue in the
world can’t cast a jolly aspect over a heaven that’s
throvvn suddenly into the midst of a social earthly
evening. If your friend offends you, there are
points in wishing him to heaven; though custom
inclines otherwise.
Cardinal, archbishop, bishop, priests. They
mixed with the crowd in a gloriously aesthetic
way, touching dark clumps of black satin frocks
—and every nine out of ten frocks were black, out
of. compliment to the clericalness of the party—
with vivid reds, and.purples, and wine-colored
cassocks. ( People outside the fold of the Cardinal’s
red moire worried themselves over kissing the
hand. They all wanted to do it, but some ,didn’t
know how. Those who could kiss as to the
manner born had a great pull over the base imita-
tions. The Cardinal stood on a square of red
carpet, just inside the door, and received the
guests and their kisses with his restless smile and
ever-roving eye. Beside him was the well-beloved
little Dr. Carr, looking just like the following
little incident:
Which little story is true. Well, I don’t mean
that sort of thing, but I mean it’s a fact. No,
that is also a misleading description, so I had
better say briefly my sketch is drawn from life,
and show it to you to convince. Once, at a Mel-
bourne Exhibition, Dr. Carr adjudicated at a com-
petitive dinner, cooked by a local cooking-class.
It was a hotter summer’s day than usual, even in
Melbourne, and the dinner was cooked by a class.
The Archbishop sat at the end of the table, with
his big, round face, and big, kind, childish eyes,
and never murmured, never frowned, never looked
otherwise than exquisitely beatific, and calmly,
slowly ate the whole of the cookery-class’s
lessons. He worked his way through terrible hors
de combats — hors d’ oeuvres I mean—through
greenly-girlypea-soup ; through mysterious chops,
in masks ; through hitherto undiscovered samples
from the animal and vegetable kingdom ; through
stiff-necked jellies, and hysterical blanc-manges;
down to a savory omelette that might have saved
from the bullet a soldier who carried it in his
breast-pocket, but that had deadly qualities if you
had to use it, not as a breast-plate, but as a savory
omelette. And, through them all, Dr. Carr smiled
sweetly. And if that class has managed to live
till now, I hope it will see this, and be smitten
with the true inwardness of that uncomplaining
smile.
. About six hundred people came. The invita-
tions were rather limited, and a bigger crowd
might have improved appearances. Dr. Higgins,
Dr. Doyle, Father Milne-Curran, were only a few
of their reverences. Miss Rooney and Mr. Wood-
house warbled, and others fiddled. Naturally, the
party represented all the most ardent local Catho-
lics. Mrs. John Toohey was there in black
brocade, looking brisk and well after her year’s
tour. Mrs. John Hughes and Mrs. Tom Hughes
and their respective husbands, and Dr. Michael
Hughes, made up a picturesque party. Mrs.
Lassetter wore the tomest frock, a black spotted
net polonaise over white chiffon, with elbow
sleeves of thickly-ruched net, and nasturtium sash
tied in front. Mr. Murphy, from Cork, with a
Yankee drawl and big buttons on his coat, was
the hero of the evening. He was blown up in the
Merrimac, don’t you know. A dark, stiff-looking,
little man, suggestive of having been broken
to bits and put together again. How men,
women, and priests hung on to his descrip-
tion of that blowing up of the Merri-
mac! “Blown up into the air,” he said,
“and came down again.” “There, now, do you
hear that ? ” said everybody, and a reverent bishop
interpolated, “Ah, you thought you were going
to Heaven thin.” “ Yes,” said Mr. Murphy, “I
gass I did, ’specially when I was coming down
again.” One rude woman stared at him and said,
“ Nobody would ever think you were Irish from
your accent.” He frowned freezingly, or frozenly,
and marched off. After that, she thought he was
a hero beyond the dreams of the ’apenny novel-
lette.
Two Governors graced the opening of Parlia-
ment on Tuesday—Lord Brassey and our own
youthful Ex., who looked younger and curlier
than ever among so much white hair and bald
patches. Lady Mary (in violet hat and jacket)
and Lady Bertha (in black) were accompanied by
Captain Lindsay—with all his English rose rubbed
off by his recent adventure with a colonial nag.
As far as frocking went, all the smart dresses be-
longed to unknown members’ wives. The promi-
nent wives dressed quietly, and took every-
thing in a languid, blase way. Mayoress
Harris’s large, kind - looking personality was
graced with a violet toque and a grey-
silk polonaise over rippling frills of tur-
quoise blue. Miss Harris was in red, with a
pink hat. Mrs. French was a sweet edition of
Her Gracious in a long black silk cape, lined with
black and white silk, a bonnet of Czar violets,
and violet gloves. The ceremony was short and
solemn. Ihe only alleviating gleam shone when
His Ex. bade the Councillors be seated and all the
seats were discovered swamped with women.
r l hey say it was a record crush of visitors. After
the Go Vi had gone, a little crowd gathered in the
lounge room to the accompaniment of biscuits
and champagne. Champagne, because a bust was
being presented to President Lackey—a bust of
himself by Signor Sirnonetti.
More church at Mrs. Escaped-Nun Splattery’s
lecture to women only. The Catholic press pep-
pered the streets near the Protestant Hall with
green leaflets that denounced the Slatteries in big
black letters, but women and children trampled
over the leaflets, and took an afternoon’s horrors
with marvellous equanimity.
Listening to that shrill, shrieking voice invoking
Cod to witness at every horror, you wonder not
that she escaped. The most elastic imagination
couldn t picture anyone chasing her to bring her
back. The horrors grow, and the voice cracks
and shrieks ; and she drinks water, and shrieks
again, and hundreds of tongues say “dthth ! ” or
words to that effect, and she shrieks more, and
denounces rivrin’ mothers arid confessors, and
digs up dirt till there seems to be none
left anywhere on the earth outside this
Protestant Hall. She is an Irish lady
with a brogue, and an unceasing gush of synonyms
for the word ‘ fiend.” She was dressed in genteel
black silk, with her light hair neatly arranged,
and would have been a pert, nice-looking woman
if she had been serving you with Berlin wool, or a
new hat or anything except the stuff she was
selling thusly at three shillings a female. She
must be coining money, for the house was
packed to the walls, and crowds of women
stood outside all the afternoon, unable to get
in, but unwilling to lose all chance of a
little splatter. As for the good Mrs. Slattery
does, you don’t need to be a student of human
nature to know that all those eager, voracious
women would rather convents existed, and Mrs.
Slatteries exposed them, than that convents were
cleared and escaped-nuns’ stories ended.
One lesson you can learn from “The Physician ”
is that even Parisian weeds can’t hide the rawness
of that crude vulgarity, a smart widow. Smart
wedding, smart christening, smart funeral, smart
widow. All that can be done to smarten the body
in life, and death, and on the borders, has been
done. It only remains to catch the soul and tie a
ribbon round its leg. Ihen the decorations will
reach from here to Yonder.
Not that Mrs. Brough wears anything outre in
the way of widow’s things. She is'dressed in the
very height of fashion; that’s all. And as she
didn’t care, there’s no anachronism, so to speak.
But she illustrates the cases of the widows who do
care, and the widows of fellows who deserve a
better monument than the hideous, discordant
draper’s block that arises after the funeral, mean-
ing what it doesn’t look, rustling what it doesn’t
mean, that always reminds one of a sneak’s face,
somehow.
Of course, Mrs. Brough carries her weeds ele-
gantly. She wears a plain black cashmere frock,
with a long, sweeping train, and a tiny vest of
white ruched chiffon. Her cape’s the thing,
though. It seemed to be made of fine black cash-
mere, lined with a shimmery black silk. It was
very long and pointed at the back, with the
fronts gradually sloped away. A border of long
black silk fringe edged it all round, and the high”
outstanding collar showed little white ruches of
chiffon overlapping thickly, and giving the effect
of a white ruff round the face.
All through “The Physician” Mrs. Brough is
beautifully frocked. Her walking-gown is black
and steel, with a big black hat and lovely black
feather boa, a guimpe and sleeves of white net
spotted with black. This guimpe is going to run
over everything soon. It is a sort of round-shaped
yoke, made separately, if you like, that will turn
the lowest ball-dress into a day gown. The trail
of the guimpe will be on all our summer frocks,
and soon we shall realise the joy of turning street-
frocks into evening dresses and back again, at a
moment’s notice. If you look at Mrs. Brough’s
black frock, you can see a charming ball-dress
arising, when the guimpe and sleeves have been
taken out. Her frocks are up-to-date also in their
length. They sweep the ground in front, and
trail beautifully behind. An ordinary long frock
doesn’t look long on the stage, and these “ Physi-
cian ” skirts are all lavishly cut in the newest
London sweep - the- streets style. I hardly
know whether to like that mauve and red
foulard in a big check pattern, worn with
a little scarlet hat massed up with scar-
let flowers. Mrs. Brough has a dashing, con-
vincing way of wearing them that almost per-
suades you. But why does a certain painstaking,
good, amiable, worthy woman play the part of a
pretty young girl? It is impossible to reckon
how much the piece loses by that mis-easte. How-
ever, making the most of things, Miss Temple
gives a valuable hint to red-haired people (for the
red wig comes out again). She wears a pale golden
brown robe most successfully against her auburn
locks, softening it at the neck with a big square-
frilled collar of yellowy net. As for her white
muslin trucking—but, then, how manv people can
wear white muslin? It isn’t a slur on Miss
Temple's acting to say that she is never the girl
Jones meant her to be. It simply means that she
and white muslin don’t go well together, which
might be said of the Queen of England, mightn’t
The men are the attraction of the play. Es-
pecially Carrie, and particularly Lovell. They act
up to each other superbly and quietly, and if we
could only get Lovell out of a room with less of
the linked-sweeoness-long-drawn-out business, he
would be too beautiful for words. His stiffness is
wearing off ; or are we getting used to it ? There’s
still the slowly-struck attitude before he gets to
the door. lam living for the day when an actor
will have the courage to walk straight out of the
room without yielding to a fatal last pose at the
portiere. As far as I can remember, it is on his
way to the door that Lovell stops to pose. When
he really gets there he throws himself out
heroically. Carne looks an ideal London doctor,
with just that tender, impressive manner tiiat
goes three-quarters of the way round a big practi, e.
He and Lovell are a fine-looking couple, that hold
one’s eyes as well as wits and feelings from
beginning to end of the piece.
By the way, “ The Physician ” is the fourth
Brough piece that ends with, “Oh, my dear,”
expressed or understood. In “Lord and Lady
Algy ” it was understood. The Algies were
sitting beside each other on the sofa when the
curtain went down. “Lady Ursula,” “ A Sum-
mer’s Day,’’and “The Physician,” all leave the
stage to the lovers. This tapering away, as it
were, to the supreme points of interest, is the next
best thing to the full close that only Shakespeare
wields quite successfully.
There has been much talk about a certain en-
gagement. The fiancees are Miss Una Russell, of
Sydney, and John Downer, Q.C., of Adelaide. Miss
Russell is the eldest of the three pretty daughters
of Mr. H. F. Russell, financial agent of this city.
Sydney knows her as a belle with a dazzling com-
plexion and a Hebe-like physique. But in addi-
tion to these charms Miss Russell is a brilliant-
minded girl, a merry witty conversationalist, with
a lot of artistic talent—displayed at the Art
Society shows ; also a peculiar penchant for the
historic. This will be now gratified. The well-
preserved John is 56. Her friends say thau
she always wore a black ribbon on Napoleon’s
birthday, and hero-worshipped in away that dis-
pleased Society males, who found her rather
’ deep ” for them, In short, she is just the sort
of strong, brainy, beautiful woman to put a high
polish on a political title.

one member couldn’t make up his mind

and the Sydney Gas Supply wouldn’t suffer from

they tossed to-and-fro the pros, and cons. At last,
they fixed on “The Greatest Gift,” which sounds

like and gory, and so on internally. “ The title’s
the tiling, ’ said somebody. “Yes,” repeated
Mister Janies (said my unreliable informant), “ the
title s tlie thing ! ”

Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
Melbourne Society is still shaken over the re-
volt of one of its daughters, witnessed in its final
act by a small assemblage at a fashionable church
in St. Kilda the other day. The bride, some
months back, annoyed her family by suddenly
breaking off her engagement to one of the few
eligibles about. A lively time ensuing, by way of
distraction she took to violin-lessens, and, as
though this measure was not sufficiently violent,
to the violin master (a small German with a mass
of hair). Her people refused to hear of the match.
A wealthy and diplomatic lady friend invited the
infatuated young woman to tea, and after deli-
cately broaching the matter, made an offer. ‘My
dear, she said, ‘you are young. You are far
from ugly. You shall do as you like, go where
you like, dress as you like for six months. I’ll
chaperone you. Afterwards, if you still care for
him, marry the man. Now go home and think it
over. Ihe girl went home impressed, doubtful.
Relatives met her on the doorstep—stormed,
raved. Girl went upstairs, dangerously quiet.
Packed. Lodgings. Wedding next day.
“It seems to be the correct thing among mum-
-4 0 kiss di a promiscuous kind of fashion on
bidding farewell. This scribe happened to be
off last week per Oruba, by which
Ada Ferrar voyaged to England. Henrietta
Watson, the Bland Holts, and a large assortment
of men and women who paint their faces at night
were there. Kissing ! Why the'noise of it was
far louder than that of the waves lapping against
the pier. The men kissed the women, and the
women kissed back. There was no reserve ; plain
public kissing was the order of the day. A man—
unknown to the writer, but evidently friendly
with the theatricalities—went by the same boat.
He was a busy kisser. Yet they say it is a jealous
profession. £
“Lady Mary Lygon outshone all the local
fashion-followers at Flemington on Saturday. In
a whitey-grey coat and skirt and back-sliding
toque of fur and quills, the visiting charmer
looked particularly neat and as racy as the
smartest could desire. Her high, bare forehead,
religious nose, and conscientious smile made
everyone feel pleased that one must not be wicked-
looking to be aristocratic.

grace, to be spent in Bendigo, after her Adelaide
concert. Boom earnings have been invested, and
now only the ocean lies between her and Paris.
Her mother goes with her, to see her settled in the
far country. Frau Mueller, too, escorts her chicL
back to her fatherland.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
John Bull died in Melb. the other day.
He was a law-clerk.
Mgr. O’Brien, of St. John’s College,
Sydney, is viewed as next on the list for an Aus-
tralian R.C. bishopric.
Somebody ought to look out and procure
for an Australian museum the pen with which
Queen Victoria, in due course, will sign the Com-
monwealth Bill.
The convicted female partner in the most
sensational series of Australian infanticides to
date is now in charge of the laundry department
of Bathurst gaol.
Rumor in official circles has it that ex-
Attorney-General Want will be offered the next
vacant N.S.W. Supreme Court Judgeship, and
that he will accept.
At least one of the new N.S.W. Leg.
Councillors has been quietly working through the
recess at his trade —he is a journeyman wheel-
wright. Times have changed !
Chief-Justice Darley is the sole survivor
of the N.B.W. Bench of Supreme Court Judges
in being at the date of his elevation. Faucett,
Innes, Manning, Windeyer, are all dead.
Joubert, the Boer commander-in-chief, is
not Dutch, but of French Huguenot descent. It
is said that his wife, who accompanies him in his
campaigns, advised the attack on Majuba Hill.
Pritchard Morgan now figures as the
father-in-law of a count. His pretty eldest daugh-
ter was lately married to Count Erio Carl Piper,
described in the London papers as the representa-
tive of an old Swedish family.
Apropos Gov. Brassey’s retention of the
pen with which he signed the Victorian Federal
Enabling Act, it is said the Deas-Thomson
family possesses the pen with which the N.S.W.
Constitution Act of Wentworth’s days was
signed.
Hume Cook —the revolter against the
Turner Govt., and would-be leader of a new
party in Vic. Assembly—went to school in One-
liunga (M.L.), of she-Mayor fame. He arrived at
Melbourne in 1881 and became a working silver-
smith.
Died at Cairns n few days ago hotelkeeper
James Byrnes, brother of the late T. J. Byrnes.
A kindly, gen.al man, still comparatively young.
“ A family of big, soft men,” said one who knew
the Byrnes family well, and, apparently, he wasn’t
far wrong.
A bulldog-necked parson, with a capacious
mouth and shaggy brows, entered the office of a
Sydney daily one evening lately and
asked the editor to insert a letter. A
glance showed that the letter was
a- violent attack on the Rev.
Jedediah Smith for the hete-
rodoxy of his last Sunday’s
discourse. “ I cannot pub-
lish such severe stric-
tures,” said the editor.
“But they must be
strong to draw atten-
tion,” said the parson.
“That’s all right from
your point of view,”
rejoined the editor,
“but what would the
Rev. Jedediah Smith
say ? ” “1 am the Rev.
Jedediah Smith!” re-
plied the parson.
“Pump Court’ : Tho-
mas Howard Fellows was
turned up from his little bed and trotted
out t'other day both by Purves and
Madden. Plenty of encomium, of course,
on that “ able Judge,” which is pre-
cisely what he wasn’t. He was a great
lawyer, but not a good Judge, for he
failed in those multiform aspects where
the dry Law does not so much come in,
and where Purves is unrivalled at the
Bar. Higinbotham was an entirely differ-
ent man from Fellows, and yet I will
say that the colorless Holroyd—the man
whom nobody ever hears about, good or
bad, is judicially master of both. Fel-
lpws made one or two astounding mis-
takes, in matters of common sense,
during his brief Judgeship. In almost
the first ease he had to try he was led
away by forensic eloquence, and gave some
gold-stealing nabobs six weeks when everyone
felt they ought to have got six years.
Rev. Dr. Harris, for years the competent
head master of Kind’s School, Parramatta, but
for some time past settled in England, proposes
to give Australia another turn. One thing sure is
that the old King’s School boys will give him a
royal welcome. Dr. Harris was the unusual
blend of a manly, scholastic, and religious man.
It is said he expected an apron and gaiters on his
return to England.
It transpires that Astley Paston Cooper,
the elderly gentleman who helped Gov. Hampden
for many months to amuse Sydney society, was
in the running for the N.S. W. Agency-General in
London—and only lost by a nose. Sir Astley
Cooper is great as a Society don, and, as Hampden
engineered the negotiations, the gay old glass-
eyed spark counted on the post as a certainty.
What he said when euchrpd must have sounded
like solemn prayer.
When Premier Sedclon (M.L.) was touring
the West Coast mining districts he was appealed
to by an out-of-luck digger for a job on some of
the co-operative works. The digger produced the
receipts for gold sold during his better days as
evidence of “ what he had done for the country.”
“ Dick ” asked him what he had done with all the
money he had got for it. “ There it is ! ” replied
the digger, handing the Premier a cabinet photo,
of himself, wife, and nine children. A male Cor-
nelia ! He got a job.

branoh of his huge business in Melbourne; he
only “denies that he has taken any definite
steps.”

the local Assembly. His law-practice brings him
in a large income.

“ Hasbeen Dead :—Rev. Habens, M.L.
Inspector Gen. and Under-Secretary for Educa-
tion. A good man with a bad liver. Writer was
once going up for a higher exam, in which he had
aaain to pass in reading and writing. Wrote to
Rev. H., pointing out the absurdity of the thing
and received for answer simply: “Sir, you must
read and write. Yours, etc.” But it was altered
for next year, notwithstanding.
Ex-Prothonotary Frederick Chapman had
his 77th birthday on July 22. Who in Sydney
does not know the venerable lawyer ? He came
from England with the late Randolph Want,
father of ex-Attorney-General Want; and, with
Edwin Daintreyas partner, carried on an exten-
sive law business in Sydney, years before he en-
tered the Public Service. He retired from the
post of Prothonotary three years ago.
Frank Long—the man who first prospected
Zeehan (Tas.) for silver, and who is now past
work—was some time ago promised a life-pension
of £1 a-week by the Tasmanian Govt., which,
however, promptly fell asleep again and forgot all
about it. At a public function, lately, the hat was
sent round among the Zeehanites on Frank’s be-
half, and a meeting is to be held shortly to con-
sider the advisableness of waking the Govt. Up.

Judge Casey, now in the Vic. public eye,
began to storm the world as a newspaper-man. He
was partner a while with Angus Mackay, and
afterwards started an out-back journal of his own.
His newspaper work got him into Parliament, and
Parliament put him on the County Court Bench.
Poor old Walsh, Q.C., who acted for a
short time as Melb. County Court Judge, in Judge
Casey’s place, and found the work too heavy for
him, was the prosecutor of Deeming. The festive
wife-slaughterer remarked of Walsh’s voice that
it was like “ the squeak of a consumptive shrimp,”
but when the time caine for it to “reply on the
whole case,” Deeming didn’t laugh any more at
that feeble passionless squeak. Prom first to last,
Walsh treated Deeming as a subject to be hanged
with the least possible delay—a “ dead bird ” for
the gallows, whose mental condition was not worth
considering.
Literary vanity in the backblox. Queens-
- Labor agitator, of scribbling propensities, had
to expiate alleged political offences by spending a
few months in gaol. Ere settling down in quod,
he handed the MS. of a story he had written to
editor of local Labor paper. Put in his six
months duly and patiently, and was finally liber-
ated. Bevy of friends and sympathisers were
waiting outside, to whom the ex-prisoner rushed
up anxiously with—“ Well, what did you chaps
think of my yarn in the Democrat ? ”
When the genial pastor Lawes went to
Boro, Papua, to establish a mission, be met there
Father Verius, the R.C. apostle, who had been
in command of the district for some years. Father
Verius didn’t like the intrusion of an alien creed,
but nerved himself to be courteous to the new-
comer. Accordingly, when Lawes called on him,
Father Verius, after praying for light, said:
“ Mistair Lawes, petter go away out of here ;
your ignorance of divine tings cannot escape de
notice of even uncultured sauvages; petter go
away.”
The boys of Launceston (Tas.) R.C.
school recently asked for a holiday, and Dean
Beechinor said he’d give it if at least one boy
could tell the Pope’s birthday. But that boy
was not forthcoming. Then the Dean said he’d
grant the request if the schoolmaster knew. The
schoolmaster didn’t; and just then old Arch-
bishop Murphy popped in and was told of the
shameful ignorance of teacher and taught.
“ Well,” said the Archbishop, “ I don’t know the
Pope’s birthday! And, what’s more, I don’t
know mywim. I was born some time -about the
date of Waterloo, and they christened me 1 Daniel
Wellington Murphy.’ ” The boys got the holiday
all right.
Premier Turner rarely jokes ; but he had
his little wheeze at the expense of the “ New
Party” of Joseph Haxton Newton Hume Cook,
M.L.A. A friend said to him that Cook him-
self was the only member of the party. “ Oh,
there are five names in it,” said Turner. “In-
deed,” exclaimed the surprised politician, “ who
are they?” “Well,” replied G.T., counting on
his finger-ends, “ there’s Joseph, then there’s
Haxton, thirdly there’s Newton, fourthly there’s
Hume, and lastly there’s Cook ! ”
Pumpkin Smith, N.S.W. ex-Minister for
Mines, is still kept going by his pals who survived
the Federalist uprising long enough to become
converts and so save their portfolios. He was
first appointed to the presidency of the “ unem-
ployed Advisory Commission ” —an honorary post
which carries travelling expenses and other perks.
Next he appears as chairman of the Tuberculosis
in Cattle Commission, with fees, and now the
papers refer to a Leichhardt land-valuation case,
where the übiquitous Smith acted for the Works
Minister.

“ M‘G.” makes a correction :
Dear Bulletin.—You have the wrong end of the story
about the late “ Bob ” Burt and Parn 11. Parnell didrfffc
die until Burt was in Australia. What the recently-
deceased journalist did discover —as I heard from his own
lips—was the marriage of Parnell and Kitty O’Shea, which
was carried out so quietly as to be secret. Burt was on a
Brighton (Eng.) paper at the time, and was correspondent
for the biggest London press-agency. He lived in the
middle of Brighton Esplanade, and used to see the un-
crowned king crossing his threshold pretty frequently on
his way to his faithful Kitty. But, one day, Burt struck
the two coming out of either a church or registrar's office
(I forget which), and, scenting a marriage, he went back
later and paid a search-fee. The investigation resulted
in the news of Parnell’s marriage being at once cabled
round the world, and in Burt being offered a billet in the
London agency, which he held until leaving for Australia.
“ Tui Tea” re Maoriland’s new C.J. :
I suppose it’s a sign of the “ judicial faculty " when a
man contrives to be popular all round. Maoriland seems,
to a man, to have forgiven Stout for accepting “ that
bauble ” from Her Gracious, for having taken on religion
after reviling it, and for turning Tory because he couldn’t
be Liberal Premier. Certain it is that there has been a
chorus of praise and satisfaction from all parts over his
appointment as Chief Justice. The prohibitionists like
it because they know his sympathies are with Cold Tea;
the brewers chuckle over it, remembering that Stout’s
last big case at the bar was one he gained for them ; the
Tories are pleased, for Stout was their staunch ally of
late years in politics ; the Liberals smile, for they know
that Seddon’s most dangerous foe is now out of the way ;
and the Left Wing party, though deploring the shelving
of its only possible leader, rubs its hands and pretends to
be pleased. The newspapers, for a variety of reasons, all
applaud the appointment. Perhaps the chief reason
with them is the state of the libel law. One journal, for
lack of a more convincing ground of satisfaction, re-
joiced in the fact that Mrs. Stout was a “ charming
hostess.” The lady, by the way, is brainy, almost as
“ faddy ” as her lord, Scotch, and very deaf—her ear-
trumpet being a “ familiar figure ” at public and social
functions.

Aboriginalities.
Jim was a glutton to work. Always up an
hour or two beforehand in the morning, and not
particular to the same extra of an evening, while
on Sundays he would fill in the time cutting down
bee-trees, boxing bees, straining honey, picking
fruit, &o. He had been nursed through a severe
illness by the farmer’s housekeeper and her
daughter, a girl of 19, and was never tired of
saying what he would do to show his gratitude if
ever he got a chance. At last the chance came,
ihe daughter died suddenly. Arrangements were
made for the interment, but a grave-digger could
not be obtained as the local hands were away
haymaking; so the farmer asked Jim if he would
dig the grave. Jim pondered a bit, and then said,
You know, boss, it ain’t a nice job, so it’s worth
a bit extra.” “You’ll have to arrange that with
the mother, the farmer told him. “Well,” said
Jim, we 11 say half-a-sov. I won’t be hard on a
poor widow in distress 1 ”

“T.H.M.’' : The Maori is a quaint animal
when he gets to airi .ig his best English. Heard a
brawny brown man once in front of a country
pub. accounting for sundry cuts and bruises on
his face : “ I lide along de road—my horse he
fliten—de train no whistle—he shuck me off. By
Clist ! I summons him ! ” Also, once saw Maori
shepherd thrashing one of his sheep-dogs, while
the other tyke capered round apparently enjoying
the fun. Suddenly the Maori let go first dog
and collared the other, exclaiming—“ Come here,
you wretch ! I learn you laff at your brudder ! ”

A woman recently called at the Two-
wharves parson’s house to have her baby chris-
tened. The rev. was out; so the mother retired,
came back after a time, and, finding the parson
still absent, said, blandly: “ Couldn’t I leave the
baby here while I do some shopping, and perhaps
Mr. Blank would have him done by the time I
come back ? ”

Lakes, and when cleaning the bird found a ruby
ring inside it. Returning to town, saw an advi).
that the ring had been lost on the shore of the
lake. Returned it and was offered £2 reward.

A Western undertaker got an order for the
funeral of a country policeman—height, 6ft. On
arrival of the coffin, the cadaver was found to be
6ft. 2in., and “wouldn’t fit.” Resourceful ghoul
suggested to relatives “ a military funeral, like we
have in town.” Consent being given, a circular
piece was cut from head of coffin—corpus fitted
nicely. Then —deceased’s helmet was placed over
the protruding portion of head, the coffin-lid was
adjusted, and the baton and darbies were crossed
thereon (but at the grave were removed, being
Govt, property). Same undertaker tells this yarn :
“ Old Wee, one time he was down in town, got a
wire from his wife, ‘ Jack died this morning ; come
home.’ Wee had been on the spree for a week,

erea a first-class funeral — no expense spared.

ot street, and you’ll trap him.” Mrs. Police-

awaits that stripe, and his wife has gone home to

• * * *
He aboriginal stone axes. Near
lewantin, on Noosa River, about 60 miles N. of
Brisbane, is an outcrop of porpyhry, diorite, or
some such hard stone, and close by is a flat-stone,
with heaps of chips and hundreds of discarded
axes, probably spoilt in manufacture : Jacky only
knew of one grade—first quality. Factory must
have ceased business quite 40 years ago, yet the
place looks as if the nigs, might have been at work
yesterday. There are enough axes to supply all
the museums in the world with a sample each,
some only half-finished, but some almost com-
plete. .Local blackfellows say: “ Those fellow
leave there wah gootchal ” —that is, the relics are
useless to a blackfellow.
* * * *
Harry Stockdale re the finding of aborigi-
nal implements in districts not possessing the par-
ticular kind of wood or stone from which the im-
plement is made :
This is due to the native custom of barter, principally
practised at the great “ comings together” at some spot
maiked by abundance of fish or kangaroo or fruit in
season. On these occasions each tribe endeavored to ex-
change some weapon or implement easily obtained in its
special locality for something only procurable elsewhere.
Invis a tribe possessing the best tomahawk-stone would
exchange supplies of this raw material perhaps for an
equivalent of good cement-gum, brought from another
aistrict. The stones for grinding “ardoo” and other
hard seeds were, though extremely heavy, carried hun-
ct reds of miles. When any particular article was not ob-
tainable at these gatherings, special trips were made by
j oung warriors painted so to make known to all other
tribes the nature of their errand, and thus protected
horn molestation. Occasionally at these barter-meetings
was held a native tournament, in which a number of the
most expert boomerang men, painted with white bars of
kaolin, and provided with shields, would face each other
or . 40 ,P aces a P a i’t ; then one would move forward till
about half-way to the man he had pitched upon, and cast
h l ® boomerang 1 at the latter ; and this would go on turn
ana turn about until one would be seriously hurt, then
the show was over. This heavy war-boomerang is very
different from the toy or returning boomerang, though
even the latter can give an ugly wound. After the tour-
nament the assemblage would either (if the moon were
favorable) prepare for a grand corroboree or otherwise go
in for as great a “ gorge ” as the larder permitted.
“J.L.K. ’: The Maoriland cockatoo’s
fourth commandment is : “ Six days shalt thou
labour and on the seventh shoot rabbits.”
The rabbit-inspector is his supreme being 1 . To
him the cookie abases himself, when he is
present, and him does he heartily curse
when he is round the bend. The irre-
sponsible autocrat must be propitiated,
for if he thinks he sees signs of rabbits
on cocky’s land, he may send on a rab-
biter and compel the farmer to pay him
7s. a day for trying to find the rabbits.
Small wonder, therefore, that the in-
spector is an honored guest and can
depend upon getting filled up with
the best whisky. And still bunny is
everywhere, despite the fact that
his skin is exported by the million
yearly, and he himself industri-
ously canned.
“ Yang ” : Re canine tooth-
drawing to make bogus South Sea
necklaces (B. 18/2/’99). Most of
the hair decoration on parang
(Dyak sword) sheaths, supposed to
come from the “ nob ” of the head-
hunter’s fallen foe, is really Chinkie
pigtail—much of it being supplied by
hospital attendants and prison warders.

Bakblox town. Times very dull.
Drunken bushie rolls out of pub. and
addresses Salvarmy captain, who is (joing
no business : “Look here, cap’n. Stand
on yer head and I’ll give yer sixpence.”
The captain did so immediately, and
raked in the tanner. The officer’s skill
in corporeal inversion became a per-
manent asset to the corps, and was
regularly utilised after knee-drill.

“ 0.W.”: At Redfern (Sydney) a
crowd had collected round an old man
crying over his dead collie. When he
wrapped the corpse up in his blanket and
made back for the railway-station, T fol-
lowed, and was told the story: “You
see, sir, we was strangers in town, and
when we got on the tram the guard
pushed Charley off, and the dog got
rounding up the engine and trying to stop
it, and got boxed. I'd give my cheque
to have him back (sob). Only last season
we shared our last water and tucker, and,
though I s’pose you won’t believe it, Char-
ley would look up at me twice before he’d-
touch the last drop of the water.”

“P.L.” : Many secrets are to be won
from the blacks by a keen observer.
Writer remembers a well-known Cen-
tralian bushman who made a small pile
some years ago by manufacturing eucalyp-
tus-extract according to an aboriginal re-
cipe. The aboriginals used to bring him
two species of gum-leaves which he blended
and distilled, producing a very powerful
yellow liquid much superior to the eucalyp-
tus sold by ordinary druggists. The
blacks used to booze on the stuff, a very
little of which would soon make them
drunk.

tion of “two bob.”

“ Pukaki ” illustrates the working of par-
tial prohibition in Maoriland
A buggy stopped at the gate. The driver clambered
down and walked unsteadily up the drive and on to the
verandah. It was only 10 a.m., but he was plainly “ the
worse. ’ He held out a shaking hand, and smiled beerily.
“Good morning, Mr. Jones,”he said. “Does my heart
good to see you 1 Couldn’t help calling on my way
through, to New Plymouth.” “ Very good of you to say
so,” I said, “ but realty you have the advantage of me.”
“ Why, don’t you remember Jack Smithson, of Nelson ?
I’m his brother Tom.” “ Oh, indeed ? I remember your
brother very well; but I must confess I don’t recollect
you. But what can I do for you?” “Oh, nothing,
nothing. You see, it’s this way. My wife’s got a pro-
hibition-order out against me. I’ve got a few pounds
together, and I’m going into New Plymouth to have a
real good bust. Your village pub is the first one outside
my district. I stopped there last night.” “ Well, if
you’ll take my advice, Mr. Smithson,” I said, “you’ll go
home again.” “No fear, cocky ! Know a trick worth
two of that. .Bye-bye, old man.” He stumbled off,
climbed into his trap, and went off at a gallop. I opened
the paper I. held in my hand, and the first thing I read
was—“ To-night Mr. L. M. Isitt will give an address on
“ Prohibition as an Elevating Factor in our Humanity 1”

“ Aramac ” : Lately saw a fight between
a pair of kookaburras and a sparrowhawk which
came after their young. Female K. got in front
of hawk, and while latter attacked her, male K.
came behind and gave him a drive on the back of
the head that dropped him dead at my feet.

“ Dareel ” writes :
Dear Bulletin.—lf “Albion Park” lives in Illawarra
district, he should know that the scarcity of native frame
is due to systematic slaughter thereof at all seasons by
professional bird-shooters, for Sydney exporting firms.
The police never interfere with these men, who openly
send game to Sydney during close season, nor do they
take notice of the use of punt-guns, much above calibre
allowed by Game Act. Things same up North, though
effects are, so far, less noticeable. S.M. Herald had re-
cently a leaderette commending some French society’s
protest against export of live quail during laying season.'
This caused me to address Granny re our own game-
export, but the old lady was apparently not taking any
local application. Vested interests?
* * * *
“ Kooka ” : Re notion that snakes are at-
tracted by music. Have never met an instance,
but know one sound that will attract any snake
within hearing : the sharpening of a saw.

Two men shaft-sinking. Man on brace
just finished hauling up dirt. Just then paper-
man on bike passes shaft ; braceman goes over to
him, buys a Bulletin, walks back to dump, sits
down and starts reading. Manager puts in an
appearance. Manager : “Hi, there, my man ; I
don’t pay you to read papers ; come up to the
office and get your time.” Braceman (in a stage
whisper) : “ Shut up, you blanky fool, you’ll wake
the man below ! ”

“Yar.”: Many South Coast (N.S.W.)
dairymen now feed their pigs on treacle and
wheat. With water, they boil it into a thick
soup, of which the animals are very fond. It fat-
tens quicker than anything else, barring, perhaps,
malt ; and the bacon, which has not yet got on
market, is the sweetest writer ever tasted.

One of the most wonderful inventions of
the age is Willson’s discovery of crystalline carbide
of caloium. There is little doubt that acetylene
gas is about to revolutionise artificial lighting.
As with all successful patents, a host of pirates
have started up to try and rob Willson of his
rights. However, the recent decision of the High
Courts at Leipsic, Germany, will somewhat damp
the ardour of Willson’s opponents. It has been
decided that Willson is undoubtedly the discoverer
of crystalline carbide of calcium. Following up
this success, the English Acetylene Company have
issued a batch of writs against infringers, and the
Acetylene Gas Co. of Australasia Ltd., of 36 Pitt-
street, Sydney—who own the Willson patents for
Australasia—are getting ready to give the Aus-
tralian pirates a surprise. Thisco. have now fitted
up some hundreds of installations around Sydney,
and are prepared to give estimates for any kind of
acetylene installations. Write direct to 36 Pitt-
street, and beware of infringers.*
“The Patentees—Maeniven and Cameron, Ltd.,
deserve a NATIONAL MEMORIAL
for their excellent inventions.” —Dover Chronicle.

That the great boom in West Australian mining
stocks was due in some measure to the increased richness
of the alluvial products there can be no doubt, and most
of the foreign speculation is done on that account. But
local dealers find an equally potent factor in the effects of
the thorough reorganisation that has been taking, place
for some time past. Many of these mines have been run
on high pressure lines that are not tolerated to-day.
Brainy men of science are taking the reins and every
move is known in advance to be an assured success. Im-
mense quantities of gold have been thrown away in tail-
ings which are now being converted into liquid gold by
the cyanide process. The vats used in this process are
made of wood or steel and are of enormous size. Great
waste of this liquid gold has resulted from absorption,
leakage or corrosion. This also has been corrected by the
useof that wonderfully effective material, P & B Paint,
which has come as a boon to mine-managers at a most
opportune time, and is now recognised as the best cyanide
paint in the world. Among the larger mines using P& B
Paint are the North Boulder, the Great Boulder, the
Brown Hill, Queen Margaret, the Australia, the As-
sociated, etc. Many others are preparing to use P& B
Paint in this and other ways known only to the up-to-date
mine-manager. E. Rich and Co., Ltd.,'are the agents for
N.S.W. Queensland and Tasmania.*

“ Davig ” : No Australian snake is really
deadly, in the same sense as the rattlesnake and
cobra are, to a healthy adult. Nevertheless, I
have known a man nearly die of fright from the
bite of a harmless carpet-snake, and other men
have died of wasp and scorpion stings. Was
scorpion-stung once myself, but suffered little. ”

“V.D.L.” : Re sheep as swimmers. A
Melb. to Launceston s.s. carried a deck-load of
sheep. On arriving at Launceston something
caused one of the woollies to jump over the side,
and soon nearly all had followed their leader. It
took close upon an hour to get them all on to the
wharf ; yet not one was drowned.

A dissertation on snakes :
Dear Bulletin.—The M‘Cooey asserts (8., 20/4/’99) that
snakes are not numerous in the Macquarie (N.S.W.) reed-
beds. When camped once near such a reed-bed between
Warren and Narromine (17 miles from the nearest snake-
juice foundry) we used to kill two or three snakes every
morning, and see plenty more while looking for “baits”
(frogs) round the swamp edge. Used at one time to kill
time and snakes between shearing and “whaling” up and
down the Macquarie. Found big, red-bellied black and
small brown very plentiful. Your renowned snakologist
asserts also that 12ft. carpet-snakes are “ abnormal,” and
that the c.s. never reaches 16ft. Have killed several of
10, 11, and 12ft., and seen many others as big. In’96,
two 14-footers (saw and measured the corpses) were
killed near “Campadore,” Winbar-woolshed, on “the
gutter” (the Darling), N.S.W. Also, in the Mossman
and Daintree river ranges in far N.Q., 1 6, 17, and even 20-
feet “ carpets ” occur not seldom—L. F. Sachs has, or had,
in his possession the skin of one over 22ft., killed by him.
Should like to know if there’s any truth in the yarns
about “ carpets ” taking to the man-crushing practices of
their relatives, the boas.

Hang Fat was found dead, and was buried
in bis clothes without an inquiry. Coroner

W. J. Sowden, the new
editor of S. A. Register, has
done more than almost any
other man in his province for
Federation. A dozen years
ago he and Speaker Bray
(afterwards Agent-General)
and Paris Nesbit were the
Grand Panjandrums of the
Natives in Adelaide ; and
of several who talked much
about Federation, Sowden
was the one man who had
really studied the question.
He drafted the scheme that
was, with modifications,
adopted by the solemn
Natives’ Conference that
met under Bray’s presi-
dency in Melb. Town Hall
in ’BO. And since then he
has kept talking and writing
about it (Sowden is probably
as effective a speaker as any
regular paper-worker in the
Australias), and he kept
the Register going straight
even when the Advertiser
wobbled.
General Gallifet, the
new French War Minister,
an intimate of the Princ e of
Wales, is an old man, a M tr-
quis, and a cavalry-officer
outside the staff-clique. He
commanded a cavalry brig-
ade in 1870, and did good
service. He belongs to the
useful class of regimental
officers. In the French
army, an officer, once on the
staff, stays there, and gets
all the good things there-
unto belonging, while the
regimental officer gets all
the hard work and hard
knocks, dull country-quar-
ters, and slow promotion.
There is no love lost be-
tween the two classes, consequently the staff lias
nothing to hope, but much to fear, from Gallifet.
Mr. Walter Bentley.
That Mr. Walter Bentley (whose portrait we
present in this issue) would have made a good
politician is doubtful ; but that he is a
capable actor has seldom been questioned. It
was with feelings of regret that his many
admirers saw him relinquish his stage career
to embark on the troublous sea of politics.
His recent defeat at Carnarvon by the Queensland
Home Secretary is fresh in the memory of all,
and it is a matter for congratulation to play-
goers that he has abandoned such aspirations
and decided to return to the footlights. Messrs.
Williamson and Mnsgrove have secured Mr.
Bentley’s services, and it was intended by them
that he should make his re-appearance in the
“Silver King” at the Princess’ Theatre, Mel-
bourne on this (Saturday) evening, but the enor-
mous business which is being done by “The
King’s Musketeer ” has led to a postponement of
that intention, and Mr. Bentley will appear
instead as Cardinal Richelieu in that play.
As it is Mr. Bentley’s intention to remain in the
colonies, and as he is a comparatively young man,
no doubt the stage will be the richer in that he
has returned to it.


Extract from Grand Master Wheeler’s address,
Sydney Town Half 12th July .
“ But thanks to the existence of this institu-
tion and to the Orangemen, who have always
been active, she has not obtained the supremacy.
(Applause.) To attract the attention of the
people she has secured the most commanding
positions and erected thereon most palatial build-
ings. It has. often struck me that these elevated
positions with their sky-reaching buildings must
be intended to be put to some use, and it is rather
strange, in these days of wireless telegraphy, that
the chain is so complete that a signal from any
one of these points could be sent round the circuit
in a very few minutes, but, of course, there is no
object to be served by such a scheme. I leave you
to work it out for yourselves.”

MELBOURNIANA.


Victor Daley’s book of verses “At Dawn
and Dusk,” seems to have been badly handled in
England, so ineagre was the notice it got ;
but there is no question of the author’s apprecia-
tion in Australia. A stupid English writer has
been lately declaiming against the praise it re-
ceived. Daley is, and knows himself, a very
minor poet in comparison with the great names of
literature ; but that does not alter the value of his
contribution to the enrichment of Australian
letters. His book may be had direct from this
office ; post-free, ss.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
27 of this issue.*

WESTRALTA.
The State progresses swiftest when
Its back is to its leading men.
The early-closing resolutions re shops, now
programmed for N.S.W. Parliament, will pro-
bably become law this session if certain woolly-
headed, allegedly-democratie members of Assem-
bly will practise the early closing of their mouths
,in the debate.
B. R. Wise, when spouting at the Salva-
tion Army meeting in Sydney, the other day, was
taken quietly by old hands. Parkes told Wise,
and Wise is a docile pupil : “Always stand in, if
they will let you, with the governing powers of
large religious bodies. It's policy.”
The ex-Labor legislators of Queensland
still cling to the paternal State. Ryott-Maughan
is again employed in the Engineering Depart-
ment ; Turley is working in a Govt, store, and J.
M. Gross, beside being secretary for the Federa-
tion League, is reported to have on hand certain
statistical commissions.
A month ago we were assured that Presi-
dent M'Kinley would not reinforce the U.S.
Army in the Philippines. Now the cable sud-
denly announces :
LONDON, July 6.
Thirteen thousand volunteers are being despatched to
reinforce the U.S. troops in the Philippines.
The sending of these men in the middle of the un-
healthy season shows that the experiences of U.S.
Army are not unlike those of the French in Hayti.
Perhaps they don’t know much about
electioneering out-back, but they do score occa-
sionally. Some Federalists in Speaker Abbott’s
Wentworth electorate, the most scattered in
N. doubted whether the Commonwealth
Bill was quite enough to attract the fellows from
the outside into the townships, so they organised
picnic sports-meetings; and the result was a
record vote, though there had been no canvassing.
Wentworth district figures were—Yes, BGS ;
No, 88.

The beauteous Charles Tucker, who has
lost his seat in S.A. Assembly on an appeal to the
Court of Disputed Returns, has fought three par-
ticularly hard elections, and has just missed it
each time. Though he wants to get into Parlia-
ment pretty badly, he seems to go round hunting
up the hard places. He has tried Pt. Adelaide,
North Adelaide, and Encounter Bay in succession.
Westralian news-item :
At the beginning of May, a J.P. (related to the Carnar-
von Brockman, struck off the list of J.’sP. for cruelty to
aboriginals) had a dozen blacks before him at his private
house, charged with spearing sheep. They were bound
hands, feet, and necks with trace-chains. The constable
gave his evidence, and sentence was passed without call-
ing on the blacks for a defence. The sentence was one
year on eacli of them.
At time of writing, the only Q. Minister
who has taken the stump on behalf of Federation
is Treas. Philp. As the bulk of the Federal vote
must come from the workers, it is perhaps better
that Dickson and Co. should remain silent and
vote against the Bill than enter the struggle and
drive thousands into indifference or hostility.
The Q. democracy won’t take their cue from the
Coalition crowd. ,
Disputes which in the eastern provinces
are dealt with by the Qualifications Committees of
Parliament go in S. Aus. to the Court of Disputed
Returns—a Parliamentary committee with a
Supreme Court Judge as president. Boucaut, who
sat on the application to dislodge Tucker from the
Encounter Bay seat, was three times Premier of
S.A. ; it was a coincidence that Encounter Bay
was his last constituency.
Yic. Parliament is at last invited to shut
down on the quack doctor, and the regularity-
restorer, and the pill-man generally. Cook’s Bill
for the suppression of indecent or obscene ad vts.
is comprehensive enough to gather in the lot of
’em. All ads. are to be deemed indecent that
relate to certain diseases, to “ nervous debility,”
pregnancy, “irregularity,” or the treatment of
any complaint peculiar to females.
“ Circular ” writes :
I, like numbers of other Victorians, have had eyes on
Mr. Higgins during his anti-federal perambulal ion in
N.S.W. It seems by the reports that he has been warn-
ing the people over there to reject the Bill because the
Victorians reckon they are going to obtain all the bene-
fits. On his campaign over here, I heard him warn
electors that Victoria would be ruined because of the
preponderance of N.S.W. Now that the vote is carried,
I suggest that, a thanksgiving sacrifice be made of Mr.
Higgins by solemn drowning in the Murray.
The anti-Feds. in Q. are in desperate
straits. The editor of Brisbane Tkt.eoraph—an
ex-parson experienced in the art of cadging—is
personally hawking round among the local shop-
keepers for subscriptions. Calling upon a loud-
voiced and truculent provincialist the other day,
confidently expecting a big cheque, he was flabber-
gasted by the magnanimous offer of—two-and-six-
pence. Half-a-crown to save the province from
destruction 1
Seriously proposed by Neil Lewis, Tas.
M.L.A. and Conventioner, that the Parliament of
the Land of No Desire, due to expire this year,
should perpetuate itself indefinitely. Reason:
Present M’s.P. know more about arranging for
Federation than new members possibly could. Mr.
Lewis expects to be elected to the Federal Parlia-
ment ; if successful, he will probably move:
“ That, in the opinion of this House, death should
be treated as a capital offence.”

1ST LADY (to second lady): “Yes, me darter
wroites to tell me she has just got a degree
nizzy—I think you call it !”
Shiels, who has been in comparative retire-
ment ever since his short and miserable Premier-
ship, is reported to be coining out strong during
this Victorian session as an opponent of Old-Age
Pensions, and other innovations. Shiels is a
brilliant and useless destructionist, and he can be
against tilings in finer language and at greater
length than almost any other Australian politician.
But he never constructs anything. lie is only a
long, weedy, hired pessimist ; too scared to do
anything himself, and afraid to see any one else
doing anything. If this pleasant and graceful
back-number is against the Old-Age Pension Bill
it ought to succeed. Shiels is failure spelled short
to save two letters.
Hartnoll’s sweeping charges against Back’s
management of Tasmanian railways were almost
bound to fail for one sufficient reason. Hartnoll
was Minister of Railways in the Dobson-Henry
Cabinet, and Back was manager then, as he is
now. And when Hartnoll quitted office he left
behind him an enthusiastic minute about Back’s
splendid management. So Hartnoll was prac-
tically denouncing his own minute as a lie—which
is always an awkward thing to do.
One Q. Labor paper protests against
carrying on Federal legislation while so many
adults are still disfranchised, “imposing on them
a constitution to which their consent lias never
A LARGE ORDER.
“Please, sir, mother says could you please let her have change for a shillin' ,
an ’ she’ll send you the shillin’ to-morrer?”
been asked.” This argument would block all
legislation. It would even block the disfranchised
getting a vote, for the Parliament would have no
right to impose on men a privilege to which “their
consent had never been asked,’’ and to which also
many of them might be indifferent.
A Select Committee of the British House
of Commons has decided that an old-age pension
system is practicable, desirable, and should be
proceeded with at once. Hitherto the Australian
Tory has been wont to plead that the pension
idea was a rash experiment and contrary to the
sound, tough; well-considered policy of the dear
old mother-country. Now that the mother-
country, even under a Conservative regime, has so
definitely changed its views, only a brutally dis-
loyal Tory can stand out.
South Australia set a brilliant example
by deciding that the M.L.A. who promised
before election to distribute his salary among
local institutions was guilty of bribery. If
it is a bribe to offer money to one elector in
order to get his vote the offer of money to all the
electors is a still worse case. And if the custom
spread the Democratic principle of payment of
members might as well be abolished, for the poor
man who couldn’t afford to give away his salary
would be at a huge disadvantage as compared
with the rich man who could.
South Australia will probably soon be face
to face with a unique political crisis :
Last year the Kingston Govt, introduced into S.A. As-
sembly a measure to extend the riirht to vote for the Leg.
Council to every householder and his wife. The reform
was proposed as a remedy for continued Tory mutilation
and rejection of popular measures. The Bill passed the
Assembly in all its s ages without division. The gang of
Fatmen in the Council —who are in a small majority'—
threw out the Bill by 12 to 9, and it therefore shared a
similar fate to the dozens of other Democratic measures
which have been contemptuously rejected in that Cham-
ber during the last five years. S.A. has a law, however,
passed in ’Bl, which provides that, should a Bill pass the
Assembly, be rejected by the Council, and—a general
election having intervened—be again passed and rejected,
the Governor may adopt the alternative of dissolution
of both Houses, or the issuing of writs for the
return of eight additional members of Council, bringing
its membership up to 32. The question now troubling
S.A. democrats is, “Which is the better alternative ? ”
The Kingston Govt, has had the staunch and unswerving
support in the Assembly of a solid Radical party which
has just gone through the ordeal of an electoral cam
paign, and is prepared to carry this Council-reform
measure through again, but it naturally dislikes the
idea of fighting another election in another month or so.
On the other hand, the Tory crowd has a majority of
three in their Chamber on the Household Suffrage ques-
tion, and, in order to turn that majority into a minority,
the Government would have to capture at least six out of
the eight new seats—a formidable task. Premier King-
ston has doggedly determined that the verdict of the
people at the recent referendum on the question—they
affirmed the principle by a majority of IS,ooo—shall be
passed into law and a dissolution in the event of further
Council obstruction —which is well-uigh inevitable—is
almost certain.
The bulk of the Q. Federation agitation
seems to be falling into the willing and capable
hands of J. G. Drake, M.L.A., leader of the
Independent Opposition in Parliament. Drake,
whose name is democratically powerful, in addj.
tion to running a bright weekly Federation paper,
is doing most of the platform and organising work.
Up to now the following Labor members have
enrolled under the Federal flag : —Dawson (leader),
Dunsford, Glassey, McDonald, Browiie, Givens,
Maxwell, Higgs, Fisher, Rylands, and Hamilton.
The principal anti-Feds. of the party are the
Central Separationists.
The defeated
N.S.W. anti-Billites
just missed a chance
to raise a dust about
the referendum owing
to what might have
been but wasn’t an
informality in one big
district. Alma, Bro-
ken Hill, and Sturt by
special Act follow
S.A. standard time
in elections under the
Party Elections Act,
and all arrangements
had been made for
the booths to be open
from 8 to 6, S. A.
time. Then, on the
evening before the
vote was taken, it
was found that the
Act which had been
passed fixingthe hours
of the referendum poll
from 8 to 6 applied to
all electorates, and
meant 8 to 6 o’clock
N. S. W. standard
time. So there had
to be flying in all
directions —in some
instances away into
the backblocks —to
warn deputies and
clerks to be on hand
at what, according to
local reckoning, was
7.30. It was a nar-
row squeak, and only
a chance wire saved a
bad mess.
The little rift in
the Q. Government
lute has become very
evident at last. Mini-
ster Chataway, i n
his paper (Mackay Mkrcur'i ), denounces Federa-
tion to his heart’s content. According to
Chataway, Queensland is perfectly independent
of the other provinces, and well able to find a

(thank Heaven !) is perfectly independent of Chat-
away and his organ, and will undoubtedly show
him so on September 2nd.
Luya, M.L.A. for South Brisbane, just
deceased, was a prominent anti-Billite. Ex-Labor-
inember Turley is backed by the local Democrats
for the seat, and is worthy of all success. Turley
is a staunch Federationist, and has advocated the
cause on the public platform. Brisbane Worker,
which recently denounced the Federal system as
the fruitful parent of every form of monopoly and
corruption, supports Turley, who has pledged him-
self to do his best endeavor to bring about the
aforesaid abominable state of things—a singular
anomaly which will bear explanation.
How the Sydney Sabbath Tipster plays
jackal to Sydney D.T.’s lion :
With the Labor party against the Government, which
is regarded as a very probable contingency, a section of
the Opposition proper, and the disaffected Govt, sup-
porters, there would be formed an alliance strong enough
to oust the Govt. At any rate, that is what certain
members of the Opposition think. An attack would be
made more readily if, as is alleged, the finances are not
in the rosy state set out by the late Treasurer.

try and wrest the Opposition leadership from
Barton. Vain hope ! The flattened-out Parlia-
mentary Geebungs are not game to snatch at the
tainted bait, upset the Govt., and be promptly
sent to face that angry 104,000 majority.

Bicycle Don’ts for 1899.
DON’T buy an auction or sales wheel.
DON’T buy a wheel assembled from parts which are bought at a few
shillings a set.
DON’T buy a wheel because a friend recommended it to you; the friend
gets a commission.
DON’T buy a Cycle from a factory that does not manufacture Bicycles
exclusively.
DON’T buy a Cycle from a factory new to the business, unless you’re
, paid ; let some one else do the testing.
DON’T buy a Cycle from a dealer who sells a new make every few
months (they’ve no reputation to lose).
DON’T pay higher price than £l5 for a cycle with outside joints and cup
and cone bearings unless you don’t believe in value for money.
DON’T buy a wheel without a direct factory guarantee. You want
some security for your money.
DON’T buy a Bicycle until you have had a “ RED BIRD SPECIAL ”
shown and explained to you.
The Goold Bicycle Co. Limited .
