SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Grand National meet at Flemington,
favored by sunshine each day, must have been the most
profitable winter show in V.R.O. history. Also, it was a
bright, clean show in a sporting sense, for although the
favorites as a rule went down, and Ikey Mo, layer of
“doubles," got away with nearly the whole of his re-
ceipts, some of the winning gee-gees ran true to latest
form, which was a remarkable fact as times go. Fair-
light took a couple of hurdle races and made a good try
for a third one. Residue, after winning a flat race, started
a warm favorite for the Open Hurdle last Saturday, and
duly “romped in." Benedict was twice victorious on the
flat, and if punters didn’t get on him (at 10’s) the second
time they had only themselves to blame. Talavera cap-
tured a race and was “ placed ” for another. Borderer
and Ouyen each scored a couple of “thirds,” and veteran
Domino, who carries more public confidence than any
other Vic. horse in training, was only beaten by a short
head for the G.N. Steeplechase. This was a great race.
Twenty-one starters, only six falls, no serious accidents,
and the time 6min. 42see.—just one tick below record.
How about the alleged deterioration in racing over the
sticks ?
With Domino in front at the distance-post two
or three thousand punters started to rejoice aloud. The
grey-haired one carried 12st. 101 b., but his is a brawny back,
and the weight didn’t give pause to the public, anyway.
Cheers for good old Domino ! Then the adjacent Mysore
—an unconsidered 9-st.oner with string-halt—was seen to
be troubling the leader ; the certainty grew less and less
certain, and the multitude dropped its jaw. The Mani-»
folds’ Mysore had pipped Domino on the post. Maybe
the grey lost the race through clouting some of his fences.
He is a safe conveyance, but he is apt to take foolish
liberties with the top-rails.
Mysore, by the way, had been muoh fancied on
the Wednesday for the Steeplechasers’ Flat Race, wherein
he ran fast enough to give the stable some hope that he
might just manage to win over the big jumps. Never-
theless, the “ books,” who laid 25 to 1 against Mysore,
showed wisdom in paying little heed to the S. F.R.
running as a test of Steeplechase chances. Happy Jack
had come with a triumphant rush at the end of the two
miles on the flat, yet, when called upon to go three miles,
with obstacles en route, the South Australian nag was an
un-Happ.v Jack all the time. Likewise Gladstone, of
N.S.W., seemed quite a champion among steeplechasers
on the flat, but, as a jumper, he shaped like one born to
eome to grief.
* * * *
Told of one horse in G.N. Steeplechase field
that his owner, whilst apparently very drunk, was
pressed to put a price on the animal. “ Give me a
hundred and I'll sellim,” said the owner, whereupon the
“ sharps,” thinking to take advantage of his deplor-
able condition, put down £lOO in notes. The “ jug-
gins” gathered up the hoodie with shaky hands, stuffed
it into his pocket, and said, “ Now, I’ll sellim. My price
is two-thousand blanky quid.” Here we see the awful
effects of intemperance upon people who didn't get
intoxicated !
* * * »
No first favorites got home on the Wednesday
of Flemington G.N. meeting. One was a 5 to 4on chance,
and the last to get beaten went out. at 2 to 1 against, con-
sequently followers of the “system ’’ must have had a bad
time. Working on the £lO scale—i.e.,a win of £lO clear,
at starting price, the first, time a favorite scored—the
system men would lose about £75 on the day.
* * * *
The 2-miles Steeplechasers’ Flat Race promises
to be one of the most popular sporting V.R.O. events.
Fourteen horses started for it last week, and every one of
them had backers at prices ranging from 7 to 2 to 10 or
12 to 1. A glorious betting race for the “books” this
must have been, and certainly an interesting display for
the public. Old Gladstone, from N.S. W., who carried
top-weight (list. 101 b.) into second place, was the hero of
the slow-time struggle.
* * * •
Residue won a 1J mile flat race at Flemington
in such good style that his poor show in the G.N. Hurdle
four days previously may be set down to want of stamina.
The RusBley horse jumped cleverly enough, but the dis-
tance was too long for him, perhaps.
• * • •
A good example of the insufferable gall of Ikey
Mo was furnished on the second day of the recent V.R.O.
meeting. Not a single one of the favorites for the first
four races had won (two of them, Benedict at 12 to 1, and
Rockwell at 10 to 1, absolutely unbacked), yet the books
opened betting on the fifth race at the liheral odds of even
money and 6to 4 Chokebore (who did not win), which
price, on the backing of several other horses, was eventu-
ally lengthened to 2to 1. Where, oh where, is the tote 7
* * * ' *
The howl of Ikey Mo at the unexpected good
play of one “Prosper," in the Victorian Club billiard
tournament, was quite a heartrending joke. The dark
“ Prosper,” like many other punters and bookmakers
before him, contrived to get “thrown into’’ the handi-
cap. Perhaps he played a little “ stiff ” before the event,
or, more likely, he remained genuinely out of practice
until the time came for him to get fit to do his best. Any
man who, having learned to play a decent amateur game,
allows himself to get rusty, can improve his “ form ”
fully 40 or 50 points in 100 by a month’s assiduous prac-
tice. He can bring himself almost level with one who
could give him half the game before he went into train-
ing. “Prosper” was handicapped to receive 160 (out of
250) in the tournament; his revelation of improved form
made him a “dead bird” for first prize—and, lot the
blessed club, the congregation of professional sharps, and
flats whoaspire to be sharps—complained that “ Prosper”
had imposed upon its innocence. The agitation was
started by Ikey Mo, needless to say, for, although
the handicapper laid a formal complaint before the club
committee, the fact of “Prosper” winning one or two
heats easily wouldn't have been anything of an “ outrage”
but for the'wagers he had taken. The artful novice, it
seems, secured £l5OO about his chance, at an average of
20 to 1. That 'was where his 'wickedness came in. He
played so promisingly from the outset as to paralyse the
market and give his beaky “ victims” no chance to hedge
their bets on satisfactory terms—in other words, to pass
the loss on to somebody else.' The books would have
been glad to find half-a-dozen other “ Prospers” in the
field to make speculation lively, but as there was only
the one “ good thing” in the tournament the men who
had laid 20 to 1 against it were unable to get out of their
little trouble—wherefore they howled like dingoes. By the
way, “Prosper” (real name Graham) stated that he
didn’t back himself to win more than a fifth of £l5OO, but
even though he had succeeded in breaking the Ring
he wouldn’t have been a more reprehensible schemer
than the horse-owner who plans a big handicap coup, or
the Ikey who upsets his calculations by nobbling the
jockey. When Ikey is “ had ” the angels flap their wings
for joy.
• • * •
Lilley well earned his place in the test-team
over his rival, Storer. Against the Australian XI. he has
kept wickets in six matches, and dismissed 17 batsmen,
nearly three per innings. The batting average is 25.
Storer is supposed to have a pull over Lilley in so much
that he can bowl, but this year his googlers do not seem
to have succeeded.
* * • •
With Hill out of the way, Noble scored the
first 1000 runs easily enough. Hill being 200 short of the
four figures before his temporary retirement. It must be
noted, however, that Noble has had 29 innings to Hill’s
13. The race for the first 100 wickets promises to be
exciting. Trumble is not 10 ahead of his compeers,
Jones and Howell, but the Victorian’s consistency will
probably prevent him being deprived of his present lead.
* • * *
Considering that All-England is sorely in need
of a fast bowler, one would have expected the uncertain
Lockwood to be given another show as a representative
player. Sometimes the Surrey man is very deadly with
the ball, and he often manages to make runs when
“ crack ” batsmen are failing. He scored steadily in both
innings against the Australians in their opening match at
Crystal Palace, but was not included in the Surrey team
when Billowell took the 10 wickets for 28.
• * • *
Laver never made a cipher in big matches
in Australia during his career, but he has the record in
England for the learn in scoring “ eggs.”
* • * *
The luck of the toss has been considerably in
favor of Darling’s side thus far, but the weather has been
bad for them and the gate-money not nearly so much as
they might have made, given harder wickets and a larger
proportion of attractive fixtures. Their list doesn’t in-
clude matches with the Gentlemen, the Players, the
North of England, or any semi-representative team save
“ M. 0.0. and Ground ” —Ground meaning professional
bowlers in this connection.
* • * •
Remarkable that the Essex team which so fairly
held its own against the Australians on a bowler's
wicket should have collapsed before Lockwood and Brock-
well last week. 6 wickets for 18, and 4 for 19 were the
Surrey men’s averages.
[ • • * *
Peel's inclusion against the visitors in the Truro
picnic-match came as a surprise, for, as the result of
Bobby's fracas with the Yorkshire executive, he was ex-
cluded altogether from county cricket, and he is now
engaged in coaching Essex. Yorkshire must be a difficult
team, or else there is too much starch in the manage-
ment. Jackson, their boss amateur player, recently had
a row with the committee, and Rhodes also was missing
from the county’s second match with the Australians.
* • * *
The Bananalanders fairly romped over N.S.
Welshmen at Brisbane last Sat., getting more than quits
for the licking they sustained a week before. Q. men
(vastly superior to mother province’s reps.) were aggres-
sors throughout and unlucky not to score twice the
number of points they did. Game ended 12—3. Per-
sonnel of home combination somewhat different from that
of previous Saturday—four fresh men added—hence im-
provement. As stated in Bulletin last week N.S.VV. was
not represented by anything like her proper strength,
but victory in the first match came as a big surprise.
Rev. Mullineux’s British team plays its second test-match
against Australia in Northern capital to-day (Sat.).
• • * *
English footballer-parson Mullineux charac-
terises as “provoking ” and “disagreeable” an Austra-
lian “forward’s” gentle habit of driving his elbow
violently into an opponent’s face. Suggestive of a S.M.
Herald leader “deprecating” an assassination, or of
the celebrated Irish election intimation that “ the use of
iron bars instead of sticks is undesirable.”
• * • •
Rev. Mullineux’s chin-wagging at Rockhampton
(Q-). t’other day, clinched his gradually-growing unpopu-
larity. Ranji’s experience should have been a lesson to
him. Mr. Mull, had a growl at M.L. referee Garrard,
brought over to'Sydney so that no charge of partiality
could lie ; then the Brisbane official came in for more
severe treatment —his errors (according to Mull.) being
“unpardonable.” The rev. gentleman is alleged to have
said: “This is the first match we have played in which
there has been no pointing against us. By pointing I
mean dirty little tricks from which we have suffered in
the big cities.” Which recalls that in the Sydney match
the referee and many onlookers had something to say re
the “ fairness ” with which his reverence put the ball
into the scrum.
* * * •
S.A. and Victoria meet at lacrosse, on Adelaide
Oval, on 29th, and the match, on paper, looks a gift for
the former. Last year, Victoria beat a weak team from
S.A., in Melb., by 8 goals to 1, but on their home ground
the Adelaide players are almost invincible. The game
is as popular in the city of churches as football, whereas
Melb. clubs have never been able to fight “the Aus-
tralian game.”
• • * •
Thirty (now called hurling) clubs, “Michael
Davitt”and “Robert Emmet,” supply fast and furious
fun for thousands at Tempe, near Sydney, every Sunday
afternoon. Most go by train, and Railway Department
profits accordingly. Last Sunday's game beggared the
toughest football-match or the hottest fist-fight—4ooo
people looked on. An “ Emmet ” man had his “ shkull ”
laid open, and hlood fairly gushed from a big, gaping
wound. Game went on just the same, while - women
screeched and men roared with excitement. ' Another
match is billed for this Sabbath.
* * * •
“Old Timer”: Saw the goriest of gory fights
in connection with Golden Gate club’s (Sydney) 9st.
novice tourney last Wednesday night. Campbell and De
Vere (ye gods!) fought and fought like demons; they
bashed, jabbed and thumped so that their classic mugs
were barely distinguishable through the mask of gore.
The show would have satiated the veriest glutton for
“ber-lud.” Blood bespattered the ropes, the posts, the
spectators in immediate proximity; it showed in great
daubs on the referee’s shirt and covered his hands—still
the men were strong, and the crowd looking on howled
again. Campbell stood the brunt of the battering in first
few rounds, but literally wore his man down and won.
Campbell met Riley on Saturday night in third round of
same competition. All Wednesday’s wounds were re-
opened, together with one or two more. Riley contributed
his quota to the flow of ruby, and excitement reached
bursting-point when, during some mad in fighting, Riley’s
hard-as-flint head crashed against Campbell’s chin with a
mighty crash that could be heard yards away, and Camp-
bell lay spread-eagled—a dweller in sluinberland for the
time being. The crowd cheered frantically while sturdy
seconds half-dragged, half-carried theii limp, fallen prin-
cipal to his chair. Remember, beginning of present de-
cade, Harry Beckett and Wynne fighting at old S.A.G.
club, York-street, Sydney, for middleweight amateur
championship. Both were covered with gore so thickly
that they looked as if just come out of the shambles.
Through their heads coming together in rushes, Beckett’s
forehead was opened in three places, Wynne’s in four.
Sawdust had afterwards to be thrown on the ring-floor to
soak up the blood, and the gloves, new at the beginning
of the slaughter, were saturated through and through.
Club-secretary Corbett had them hanging in his office for
months as mementoes of the goriest of battles. Beckett
won.
* * * *
The tough element in pugilism begins to show
itself with the growth of the boom. On Saturday night
last, at Golden Gate Club (Sydney), one ornament of the
profession burst into the ring and rushed towards a well-
known sporting writer screeching with maniacal fury,
“I’ll shoot the 1” pulling something from his
pocket the while. The house was packed," and those
around the scribe begged him to get away, fearing the
howling bruiser might have a pistol. The pug. was soon
overpowered, however, and hurled, frothing at the
mouth, out into the night. The incident created a sen-
sation while it lasted, and about 50 people stampeded
through the long narrow passage. Golden Gate will be
short-lived if this man’s presence is tolerated there. The
last pug. to attack a reporter in Sydney was grizzly Joe
Goddard, but a heavy fine and costs effectively weaned
him of his zest for that particular pastime.
• • * •
Safe to say no fight in N.S.W., for years past,
has created so much interest as that, at Wyalong, to-
night (Thursday), between Jack Goggin and Jack Conlon,
for £lOO a-side and £5O purse. YVyalong is Goggin’s home,
and miners will put their all on him. He is president of
the Miners’ Association, and extremely popular. A solid
contingent of Sydney sports left Tuesday and Wednesday
to rake in the shekels on their dead-sure thing—Conlon.
They might “ fall in,” but writer hardly thinks so.
m * * *
A marvellous exhibition of grit and gruel was
the fight between Tim Murphy (10.4) and Fred Preston
(12st.) at Melb. Vic. Hall last Saturday night. From the
gong the men fought like tigers, appeals for fouls came
from both corners at short intervals, and yet there was
little clinching, and no hit that was more than technically
unfair. At the end of three rounds, Murphy, who has
learnt much since his last defeat by Preston, landed his
shorter and burlier antagonist with both hands, and had
he not had a rest through an appeal for fouling, Preston
would have outed right away. As it was, the rest brought
him round, and he knocked Murphy’s head like a China
mandarin's in the next couple of rounds. Then the out-
fighting of the lathy youngster scored—he brought
Preston down in the 6th round, and in the 7th the
veteran pug had to stop, dropping “all out” into
his chair. Murphy fairly smothered the heavy man at a
distance, and, though he was badly marked at close
quarters, Australia has mighty few, and the world not
many, men at 10.4 who could whip Murphy to-day. What
a showing-up does the crookedness of the average glove-
contest get when two real-goers like these come along
and give each other enough in 18 minutes !
* * * *
Cabled that Sydney champion sprinter Jack
Hellings won Southern Counties (Eng.) 100yds. swimming
championship in lmin. ssec. Hellings has been “down
under ” about three years. He “ went off” last season,
but his latest feat means return to old form, and that
he’ll be well up with his fellow countryman, Freddy
Lane, whenever they meet. J.H. was never too good
beyond 500yds.
* * * *
Cabled that English top-notch swimmer Jarvis
won 1000yds. ohampionship at Walsall last Saturday,
and put up a new world’s record—l4m. lOsec. Previous
best (15in. 2sec.) stood in Tyers’ name. Fastest ever done
in Australia was when Bill Corbett (N.S.W.) and Wally
M’lndoe (Vic.) swam their match at then Robinson’s, now
Hellings’, baths. Domain, 17 years ago. M‘lndoe won by
6yds., after a desperate finish, in 14m. 34sec. The
swimmers turned every 50,vds. without aid of push-off.
Australasian Champion Lane should figure in English
half-mile premiership at Brighton to-day (Saturday), and
if writer is not mistaken, will swim second, should Jarvis
start —first otherwise. Also noticed in cables that that
wh te-headed little speed-merchant from WoolloomoOloo
Bay (Hellings) again won City of London championship—
this time easily. Last year he just got home, and no
more. The advent of Ids fellow-countryman, Lane, has
apparently put Jack on his mettle.
• • * *
Atop of Gray’s and Miller’s we now have
Deeble’s starting-machine, which looks the simplest and
most certain of the lot. Two uprights on either side of
track and a rope stretched between, one end supported
by an inverted hook, and other by a pin. A lever at the
pin side releases both ends instanter, and simultaneously
rope drops to ground without twist or curl, before jockeys
or horses realise what has happened. Not the remotest
risk of tripping or in any way interfering with the
animals’ legs exists. Probable that the latest invention
will be given a trial at one of the horse meetings shortly
—perhaps Moorefield next Saturday.
* • • *
The third test-match seems to have played poor
little Briggs to a finish. The epileptic fit which struck
him down has left him permanently “queer ” in the head,
it is feared, although his burly body remains sound, Is
it the cricket or the “ recreation ” off the field that does
the damage ? Didn't Scotton, the good-looking Notting-
ham stone-waller, go mad ? Midwinter died in an asylum.
Uarry Trott and now Briggs are mentally run out.
• * • *
Ex-Australian Eleven man Walter Giffen —
George’s little brother—has quite renounced the family
devotion to cricket in the hot pursuit after slugs and
“ likely shows.” His party has done very well at Blum-
berg, S.A., for the last couple of years, and was repre-
sented by Giffen and another among the first rushers
after the Centralian tellurides.
• * « *
Curious thing how records chase each other.
Long ago, in the first test-match in England, Grace put
up 152, and Murdoch promptly topped it with 153, not
out. Brown and Tunnioliffe made 378-for first wicket in
’97, beating a record that had stood for five years—and
within a fortnight Abel and Brockwell had gone just one
run better. This season Howell takes 10 for 28. No one
had taken the whole 10 since ’95 ; but within a fortnight
there are two men with 9 for 28, and in a month the-
whole 10 are bagged again.

There must be a good market in India for smart
Australian ponies ; the price of Beccles (late Rose 1., of
Sydney renown) is just about £5OO.
* * * •
We are requested to inform Bulletin readers
that the management of the Sydney Safe Deposit (in Ash-
street, back of Paling’s, Sydney) have decided, with a
view to popularising the use of the safes, to offer a special
discount of 25 per cent, to persons paying rent for two
years in advance. At present the annual rents in advance
are from two to six guineas, and the new system will
have the effect of reducing the rents to from lj to 4£
guineas. It has also been decided to lease safes at from
15s. per quarter and 255. per half year, a further innova-
tion.*
The average person hears the alarming
word coffin-ship without a thrill. It isn’t his
coffin-ship, and he doesn’t intend it to be his
funeral. Even when he goes voyaging, he takes
stone building that everything is all right, and 99
times in 100 he is justified. The 100th time
well, this is what a Sydney Evening News re-
porter saw on a tour of inspection among active-
service steamers round Sydney harbour.
No. 1 vessel; a passenger steamer, “ which has
frequently carried many hundreds of people.” She lay
upon a slip receiving overhaul . . . The reporter
saw one plate taken otf, and as it was laid on the ground
he gently prodded it with his umbrella, spotting it with
holes as if a pencil had been jabbed into a sheet of note-
paper. There was no necessity to do this except curio-
si ty, for the plate was full of holes, varying in size from
that of a sixpence to a pin’s point. Daylight could be
seen through the plate in dozens of places, and the plate
had been worn to such a degree of thinness that pieces of
the rust-enc.rusted iron could readily be broken off by
hand with the aid of an occasional “ jab” of the umbrella
ferule. This particular plate, it may be stated, was the
second from the keel in the forepeak, and when the
vessel was afloat was well under water.
Further on, on the same vessel, a workman was
repairing a plate in situ. He—
was about to commence the making of a hole, when at
the first blow of the hammer, the chisel flew through the
plate up to the man’s fist, just as a sharp knife might be
plunged into a child’s toy drum. Another plate in the
same vessel had a big hole in it the size of a breakfast
cup.
To No. 2 vessel the reporter came too late—she
had been nicely painted up, and nothing was
visible. No. 3 was a cargo steamer—
This particular vessel, when seen by our reporter, had
been practically replated along the greater part of her
hull. Some of the old plates, however, remained, above
the water-line, bearing several large holes, and worn to
the thinness in places of a sheet of paper. Old plates
taken from this vessel were also examined, and many of
them were found to be completely riddled with holes, as
a wooden target might be if aimed at at close range with
a Nordenfeldt gun. This is only a cargo-carrier, but she
carries a crew of from 20 to 30 men.
And doubtless there are others in a similar condi-
tion, with the fossilised Marine Board blinking
cheerfully over all. Yet, when attention is
drawn to this state of things in Parlia-
ment, a steamship-owners’representative is bound
to get up and assure the public, on his honor and
his waistcoat, that Marine Board inspection is not
a farce, and that every vessel sailing out of Sydney
is as sound as a whistle—till the gale comes. Will
Premier Reid abolish the Marine Board this ses-
sion , and safeguard the public and the seamen ?
Cardinal Moran’s tirade against Protestant
missionary work in the Pacific was ill-timed.
The “ Glorious Twelfth ” just passed had
momentarily aroused the sleeping Yellow Pup,
and the chance for a snarl at Rome was too
plainly offered to resist. The accursed sec-
tarian battle cries are again being raised, and
the clumsy Cardinal is this time to blame for
startiug the row. If the Protestant missions
need condemnation the head of the opposition
shop is absolutely the very last person who
should condemn them. As charity begins
at home, it would be well for both sects
to absolutely drop their foreign missions
and attend to the crying wants of their own
local dependents, many of whom are in greater
need of counsel and salvation than the happy,
heedless, island blacks. Let the next volunteer
for China or India carefully read the deposi-
tions in the case of one John Williams, tried
for his life a few months ago in a Western
N.S.W. township, and then decide whether he
cannot devote his philanthropic energies to
civilising his own people ere embarking for the
better-advertised and more showy foreign work.
♦
The Mirquis of Tweeddale, the Grand
Panjandrum of the Eastern Telegraph Co., which
so loudly protests'against the proposed all-British
cable, comes from a stock in which the pug. ele-
ment was very strong. His father used to go
about rigged up in old, seedy clothes' searching
for “trouble,” and enjoyed a “dust-up” as
thoroughly as a bull-terrrier. Few professional
pugilists could best him, and then only after a
tough go, from which the other man never emerged
unscathed.
A London correspondent of Sydney D.T.
tells two good stories, of the Benjamin Trovato
order, about Queen Victoria. The first: —“A
young officer who had been on duty at Balmoral —
a nice-looking boy—was killed at Omdurman.
The Queen, with her faultless memory, recalled
his face, and recollected that she had heard of his
marriage some months before his death. On
inquiry, it was found that his widow, a girl of 19,
was in great trouble. Her baby, born since its
father’s death, was terribly ill. The Empress
Queen sat down and wrote a letter to
the young mother, which ended as follows:
‘ And, my dear, if you will take the advice of an
experienced mother, you will feed him on donkey’s
milk.’” The other : “ During the Sirdar’s trium-
phant progress through England after the Soudan
campaign, he, of course, went to visit the Queen.
Her Majesty was gracious to him, and it is re-
ported that the Sirdar made himself very agree-
able in return. The next day the Queen remarked
simply, ‘ I had been told he did not like women,
but he was very nice to me.’ ” •

What surprised Best and Trenwith most
in Maoriland was to find that, contrary to the ex-
perience of all other nations, the half-castes were
superior people, and prouder of their aboriginal
strain than of the other.
“ In Memoriara ” from Melb. Argus
KLAVVS. —In loving memory of my dear old friend,
Will Klaws, who died on the 17th June, 1898, at
St. Kilda. Good old Bill, hie very presence drove
dull care away. (Inserted by Ted Jones, Kalgoor-
lie, W.A.)
The Clydesdale Centrifugal Sluicing Co. is
another project for washing up a considerable
portion of this golden continent. There are
several attractive features set forth in prospeotus,
which appears in another column, which probably
account for shares being rapidly applied for.*
The Royal Humane Society doesn’t rush
things much. Thirty-nine years ago a Vio.
miner, Wm. Fraser, rescued a couple of mates
from foul air, and was awarded a silver medal.
Next year he lost it in a snowstorm in Maoriland,
and applied for a duplicate. Last month it came
to hand—37 years' afterwards ! For 36 years he
had thought that his claim had been disallowed.
At Wantabadgery station, near Junee, a
man said he would suicide if the Commonwealth
Bill were adopted—and he did :
He quite believed the Antis who
Made prophesies so foul and grim ;
. And sought the Hell the cleric drew
To dodge the one they promised him.
The Q. anti-Feds. are making elaborate
preparations to turn the approaching South Bris-
bane (Q.) bye-election to party advantage. They
have subscribed all the expenses of the alleged
Government candidate, and hope to use the result
as a conclusive demonstration of anti-Federalism
in the metropolis. When the figures go up some-
one will be anxiously enquiring what has been
done with his money.
Henry Turley, former member of Q.
Assembly, is now a candidate for his old seat —
rendered vacant by the sudden death of Mr.
Luya. Turley, who was much handicapped at the
general elections by a weak colleague, is an ardent
Federalist and a man of brains and grit. He is a
Laborist of acknowleged strength and rectitude.
His opponent is a commonplace alderman of anti-
Federal failings and no particular parts. The
result of the election will show to what extent —if
any —the Brisbane worker is influenced by the
jeremiads of the local Telegraph.
The colored-alien-labor-loving Brisbane
Telegraph sent the Q. metropolis into uncontrol-
lable hysterics the other day by hoisting the motto,
“Queensland for Queenslanders.” The parson-
ridden rag has consistently and bellicosely
barracked for the Kanaka and Coolie and Japanee
and Afghan and Dago and every other known
form of cheap foreigner likely to enter into unfair
competition with the local white worker ; but
when the democratic Commonwealth Bill comes
upon the scene and threatens to give a big impetus
to Q. Radicalism, and proposes to take over the
question of colored-alien importation, the black-
labor prophet has the front to pose before the
masses as the apostle of “ Queensland for Queens-
landers ! ” After that -anything.
From the letter of an Australian miner
who has spent several years on the Rand :
There are three parties in the Transvaal-the old Boer
party, who want things to remain as they are ; the great
body of Uitlanders, who want a republic with equal
rights for all; and lastly, the most insignificant in point
of numbers, a blatant body of excessively loyal English-
men, who want the country to pass under British con-
trol. Seventy-five per cent, of the Uitlanders are not
British in the proper sense of the word. Even most of
those who speak the English language on the Rand are
Americans and Australians, and these men are unani-
mously in agreement with the great majority of Wtlan-
ders that if they can effect the overthrow of Kruger and
his oligarchy' they' will fight with the Boers, if necessary,
to prevent Rhodes and his gang of speculative mining
monopolists from jumping the country and making it a
place in which a white man would soon find it hard to
get a job.
Sydney receives the glad news that the
goat is an illegal animal—in the daytime, anyhow.
At all events, Hatte, the large and well-known
Newtown (Sydney) draper tried to draw attention
to his cheap sale per medium of a gaudily-attired
advertising goat which was led through the streets.
And he was fined something considerable for
illegally advertising upon a noisome animal, or
conduct calculated to lead to a breach of the
peace, or something. The law has always been
more or less of an ass, and the ass and the goat
never got on well together.
There is already a movement in Australia
—principally in N.S.W.— to send a filibustering
band to help in the prospective war against the
Transvaal. If N.S.W. does help, even in the
smallest degree, to wipe out the Boer State be-
cause it treats its aliens rather better in the
matter of the franchise than Britain does, and
because the syndicate-mongers of Johannesburg
want privileges which Britain never dreams of
granting to the Dutchman in the United King-
dom, it will be a far worse blot on this country
than the foolish ass-capture in the Soudan. And
if N.S.W. in general approves of the iniquity,
then it will be only just retribution if, some day
when she is fighting for her own existence, some
other nation sends along a gang of filibusters to
shoot the Cornstalk down from pure love of
murder.

THE ACED.
Dear Bulletin.—l have spent a weekly
hard-earned 6d. on your “ red rag ” for many
years now, and will continue to do so from habit,
like the opium-habit and other vices. I have
never written you previously, but I now rise to
ask who is George Pile and what are the pedigree
and performances of his dawg?—W.H.M.
[G. Pile is a mild-looking little Sydney man whose
ferocious anti-Federal sentiments look quite out of keep-
ing with his beaming benevolence. And his dog looks so
fierce and sour that an impression arose that he supplied
the sentiments. Writer was going home one night with
two other Federationists when the dog came out and bit
all three, whereas Mr. Pile never bit anybody. Which
convinced him that the dog was the real anti-Federalist.
And thereafter, out of a sense tf justice, when Mr. Pile
delivered a wild anti-Federal oration, this paper set it
down to Mr. Pile’s dog, who composed the speech in his
kennel the previous night. Writer lost several nights’
sleep through Mr. Pile’s dog (which lives in same street)
delivering anti-Federal addresses to writer’s demoralised
Federal cat.]
About eight years ago a cosmopolitan pro-
fessor, with an unpronouneable name, staggered
the scientific world by solidifying air. The pro-
fessor produced a solid lump of air, about the size
of a walnut, at a cost of several hundred pounds.
While experimenting with his new toy something
happened—the professor woke up a fortnight
later and asked who had hit him, and why he was
not in his own home. He was informed that his
home vvas chiefly fragments, and people were
anxious to know what new explosive he had been
dabbling with. Now, one Charles E. Tripler, of
New York, has constructed machinery which re-
duces the temperature in its innards to 312 deg.
F. below zero. At this unutterable depth of chill
air is so contracted as to become a liquid. Com-
pared with 312 below zero the ordinary atmosphere
in which we eat, drink and have our love-making
is a raging furnace, and immediately the liquefied
air comes in contact with it it starts to vaporise,
just as water does into steam when it comes into
contact with 212 deg. of heat. The expansion of
air from liquid to vapor supplies 100 tunes more
force than that of water to steam, and as the
atmosphere takes the place of the furnace, in the
industrial and mechanical application of liquid
air there will be a mighty saving in the cost of
fuel, large boilers, and all the complicated
machinery in vogue to create and conserve power.
The N. Y. man says that even with his experimental
machinery, he can make the stuff at a cost of about
lOd. per gal. And as the expansion and power-pro-
duction of one gallon of liquid air is equal to that
of lOOgals. of water, with the cost of the furnace
saved in addition, the extent to which the utilisa-
tion of liquid air will reduce the cost of produc-
tion can be put down as revolutionary. The new
substance is as full of freaks as a new woman. It
“ boils ” or vapourises with a homely-kettle song
on a block of ice, as ice is, in comparison, a slow
fire; it turns alcoholic spirits into ice, makes
mercury hard as chilled steel, renders gold, silver,
copper, and other metals as jjliable as india-
rubber, snaps brass, steel and iron like glass, eats
away diseased flesh from wounds utterly and
without pain; cotton-waste soaked in it and
ignited explodes with a force to which that of
gun-cotton is but as an elephant’s kick. Alto-
gether it seems as if the use of steam was about to
end suddenly and be succeeded by that of liquid
air. In which case a manufacturing city will
cease to he the smoky, unhealthy horror it is
to-day.

BEFORE going into further particulars of what
the marvellous Talking Machine can do, it
will be as well to say a few words about the discs
on which the records are etched.
They are made of rubber, hardened, and are
separate from the machine. It is on this rubber
disc that the sound wave is preserved. They are
quite flat, 7in. in diameter, and the record of
songs, etc., is etched upon them in spiral circles,
sufficiently deep to be safe from all ordinary
handling. In fact, you can give them to the
baby to play with, or use them for pitching
quoits, without fear of injury.
Owing to their shape and durability, they are
very easily posted to any part of Australasia, per
parcels post, and one having purchased a

Clydesdale Centrifugal Sluicing Company,
CASTLEMAINE.
(To be Registered as a No Liability Company.)
CAPITAL, £15,000 in 40,000 SHARES of 7s. 6d. EACH
16,000 Shares, fully paid up, to be retained by the Vendors in full payment for the
property.
24,000 Shares are offered to the public at Is. on Application, Is. on Allotment, and the
balance in Monthly Instalments, not exceeding 6d. per Share, if required.’
SPECIAL NOTICE.
To all applicants of 50 Shares and upwards the Vendors will allot a SPECIAL BONUS of 10
PER CENT. IN ETTT.T.V PAID-UP SHARES.
The whole of the proceeds from sale of Shares will be placed to the credit of the company (less
brokerage and flotation expenses), and will be available for working capital.
NO CASH TO THE VENDORS.
DIRECTORS : To be appointed by the Shareholders at the First Meeting of the Company.
BANKERS : National Bank of Australasia, Castlemaine and Melbourne.
SOLICITORS : M‘Cay and Thwaites, Barker-street, Castlemaine.
LEGAL MANAGER and OFFICES of the COMPANY (pro tern.): Jno. Wm. Dunlop
407 Collins-street, Melbourne.
Application for Shares may be made through any registered member of the Melbourne, Ballarat
Bendigo, and Castlemaine Exchanges, or to Jno. Wm. Dunlop, 407 Collins-street.
This company is being formed for the purpose of acquiring and working a very valuable mining
property consisting of Lease No. 4244 absolutely granted (vide “ Govt rnmt-nt Gazette,” April 28, 1899),
also application for lease adjoining, comprising in all about 110 acres, taking in about one mile and a
quarter of the Jim Crow Creek, at Clydesdale, midway between Castlemaine and Daylesford, and
situate between the Pickpocket and Hardhill diggings, which were exceedingly rich; included in
these leases are very rich flats on either side of the creek, which it has been impossibe to work on
account of heavy water in the ground.
THE WHOLE OF THE GROUND FROM THE SURFACE DOWN CARRIES GOLD
AND THERE IS ADOUT sft. OF GRAVEL ON THE BOTTOM CARRYING ROUGH
GOLD.
This property will be worked on the centrifugal sluicing principle, similar to that nowin use at
Campbell’s Creek, Castlemaine, where gold to the extent of 7750 z. has already been won, from ground
that lias been turned over a great number of times during the last b 0 years, which speaks volumes for
this latest improved method of gold winning.
The machinery to be erected will be of the most modern type, similar to that now in use at Camp-
bell’s Creek, capable of treating at least 2000 tons per day of 24 hours at a cost of threepence per ton.
The system of working by centrifugal sluicing is one that aims at profitably working poor ground
by treating large quantities at a minimum cost, but in this case the ground is known to be payable,
and intending investors should not hesitate in applying for shares.
The ground immediately south of Lease No. 4244 has been worked in a small way by several
persons for some time past, all of whom have been getting splendid returns.
There is a plentiful supply of water in the creek.
Reports from Mr. W. Kershaw, M.E. (engineer in charge of the Campbell’s Creek Dredging Co.
also consulting engineer to the Livingstone Creek Gold-Dredging Co., Oineo, and Fryer’s Creek Gold-
Dredging Co., Fryerstown), Mr. T. Elliot, ex-mayor of Castlemaine; and Mr. C. Fenton, of Castle-
maine ; also affidavit sworn by James C. Strong, of Chewton, can be seen at the offices of the company,
407 Collins-street, Melbourne.
Early application for shares is necessary, as several thousand have been applied for privately.
Priority of application will be strictly adhered to.
Special Attention is Rsquested to tlie Polio wing 1 Features of this Property, viz.
Lease No. 4244 is Absolutely Granted.
Work can be Commenced without Delay, Directly
Company is Formed.
Should be Paying Dividends within Six Months
after Commencement of Operations.
No Cash to the Vendors.
Special Bonus of 10 Per Cent, in Paid-up Shares
on All Applications of 50 Shares and Up-
wards.

Collinsstrect, Melbourne.

NOTE—Our ONLY Australian Address-90 KING STREET, SYDNEY,
The Seddon Govt, meets Maoriland Par-
liament with a programme which includes the re-
ferendum, State accident-insurance, compensation
for workmen injured by accidents, an extension of
the State Land Bank system to towns, a reduction
of interest-rates on State Land Bank loans, and
various other reforms. This session will be an
awkward one for the Tory party. It appeals to
the country to put it in office with the promise
that it will give the existing “experimental legis-
lation ” (as it contemptuously calls it) a fair trial.
But if it opposes the new democratic legislation,
the country will know that it is as hostile as
ever to democratic legislation, and that its
“ fair trial ” won’t be worth a copper. And if it
supports the new ideas, then it is a question of
choosing between the new unreliable Democrat,
who only became a convert when he couldn’t get
office otherwise, or the old warrior who has been a
Democrat all along.

A Bit of the Truth.
The most pertinent and startling question ever
asked is this “ I Vhat is truth ?
A JRoman Governor propounded it onee, under
circumstances which greatly worried and peiplexed
his official mind. And he was not the first man
or the last' —not myriads. So far as I know
there has been no satisfactory answer. Some
people (radicals and come-outers of various sorts)
fancy that in this, the tail end of a rather braggart
and conceited century,'they have flushed a fair-
sized covey of truths by firing speculative shot-
guns into every wayside bush and bog.
But have they done it? No, gentle reader, no.
They have put up crows and sparrows, the same
crows that picked the bones of the cave-dwellers,
and the same sparrows that sold two for a farthing
in the time of Pilate. There were plenty of fools
of old, and there are plenty now. The ancient
doctors, indeed, prescribed some horrible stuff's as
medicine—they used electuaries of viper’s flesh and
recommended pomegranate seeds for toothache
because those seeds resemble human teeth. Very
shallow and silly, to be sure, this sounds to us.
But if you wanted to find things that come very
near matching them in modern practice, I could
show you where to look. On my table I have a
list of about 300 new “remedies ” introduced to a
suffering world within the past twelvemonth.
“ Must be some good ones among them,” do you
say? Possibly! Time will tell. Meanwhile let
us stick to whatever we are sure of. “We learn
how to cure diseases,” said Celsus, “by experi-
ence, not by reasoning.”
“ Some of the greatest truths in medicine,” said
a learned Scotch doctor, “ came by the humblest
means ; not by synthesis or venesection, but by
the observations of peasants and the experiments
of motherly women.”
Concerning a medicine discovered by one such
woman, thousands of stories have been told and
letters written. Here is an example:—
“For many years I have suffered from indiges-
tion and weakness. I seemed to have no energy
for anything. I had a poor appetite, and what
little food I ate caused me violent pains in the
chest and between the shoulders. Frequently I
had attacks of giddiness, and when I stooped I
suffered from an unnatural rush of blood to the
head. The pain which I was called upon to bear
was often very severe ; it affected all parts of my
body, and at night I got little proper and refresh-
ing sleep on account of it.
“As time went on and the complaint grew
fixed upon me, I came to be exceedingly weak,
and now and again was obliged to take to my bed.
I lost flesh and became quite thin, living, as I
did, only on milk, beef tea, and other kinds of
liquid food. It will be understood, of course, that
I had medical care, besides attending the South
London Dispensary. Yet I received no benefit
from what was done for me.
“It happened that in January, 1889, a friend
—Mr. Pullen—told me he had suffered in a similar-
manner, and been cured by a remedy called Mother
Seigel s Syrup., Acting on his suggestion, I got a
bottle, and, after having taken it, I found great
relief. Presently my appetite returned, and food
no longer distressed me. Convinced that Mother
Seigel’s Syrup was adapted to my ailment, I con-
tinued the use of it until it was no longer needed.
My health and strength were re-established, and I
have since been well. This medicine has done
what no others had been able to do. My husband,
who suffered from biliousness, used it with the same
result. You have my free consent to publish this
brief statement, if you desire to do so.”—(Signed)
Mrs. Julia Massey, 133 Lorrimore-road, Kenning-
ton, London, S.E., January 20th, 1898.
There is no royal road to the discovery of truth
or knowledge. Anybody may find it anywhere.
It is not always he who seeks that finds. Valuable
discoveries are usually made by what, for lack of
a better word, we call accident. r l he medicine
that cures is the medicine we want, no matter
whether it is as old as the earth or was picked up
yesterday in the fields by a child. That Mother
Seigel’s Syrup cures is proved by a cloud of wit-
nesses. It is a bit of the truth. Therefore it will
not die out, arid nothing can take its place.

Undermines his Health,
THEY TOLD HIM TO EFFECT
A CURE
Clements Tonic
Was a DEAD BIRD.
The Case of
Mr. Christopher Cullen.
[From thi Liverpool Herald, Aug. 18th, 1898.)
(by our own reporter).
“Please obtain full details as to his illness
and recovery, and also as to his present state
of health.” These were the instructions
received by our ( Liverpool Herald) reporter
to visit
Mr. Christopher CuHen, Atkin-
son Street, Liverpool,
Mr. Cullen is as widely known as esteemed.
He is a master painter, and the newspaper
man betook himself and discovered Mr.
tlullen on the top of a house which he was
)ainting.
“May I have a word with you about your
late illness,” asked our reporter? At which
Mr. Cullen descended, and stated, “I am
glad to get away from the paint pots for a time,
I can tell you, for a too close acquaintance
with them was the first cause of my sickness.
I got Lead Poisoning
from the paint. Pains in the limbs attacked
me, especially in the shin bones, and often I
have boon obliged to get up in middle of the
night, so intense was the suffering from this
cause. I became terribly constipated,which
was sometimes varied by severe attacks of
diarrhoea. I had always an unpleasant and
bitter taste in the mouth, and a bad breath.
I suffered from dyspepsia. There was a
tight feeling on my chest, and my food lay
still in my stomach and turned sour, and
caused belching of sour and bitter gas.”
“How was the appetite?”
“I hadn’t one. I couldn’t look at any-
thing to eat. I was, consequently, half
starved, and daily got worse and worse. A
dull, leaden pain began to come
Across the Back,
and my eyesight began to go wrong.”
“ Did it impair your eyesight ?”
Yes. Especially at night. I couldn’t go
out with safety after dark. I was con-
tinually troubled with dull, persistent
headaches, and, worst of all, my nervous
system became greatly weakened. My con-
stitution was undermined by the poison
absorbed from the paint, and my physical
strength was
Completely Run Down.”
“What started you on Clements Tonic ?’*
“Mr. Jas. Smith, who lives in this town,
hearing of the state I was in, called up to see
me.”
“ Clements Tonic will cure you, he said ?”
“ Nonsense, I replied. I’ve tried all sorts
of physic to no purpose. Mr. Smith said,“l
tell you Clements Tonic will cure you ;
It’s a Dead Bird.”
‘‘Now,all I can say is that I thank God ho
convinced me in the argument, and I started
cn Clements Tonic, and it did its work
f,teadily and thoroughly. It first did awny
with my costiveness, and the strange paini
that were in my limbs and back. My
nerves gradually improved, and 1 could soon
eat my meals like a man again. The queer
affection of my eyesight also speedily gavo
way to Clements Tonic.”
“ You must have been pleased ?”
“I was delighted. It was as much as I
could do when I was sick to walk, and no ,v
I felt inclined to run.”
“And about the lead ?”
“Clements Tonic eventually rid my blood
of every particle of lead it contained, for 1
have not had the slighest trace of the poison,
or felt any of its effects since I finished the
second bottle, and I do not believe there is
a particle of lead lurking within me. Ino w
Feel a. New Man,
and have not been troubled since my cure.
“And if you were?”
“Oh ! well, I know what to do—a fev,
doses of Clements Tonic taken in time, and -
ehould be all right?”
“This will all go in the paper, Mi
Cullen.”
“Certainly it can. It’s nothing but th
truth ; may it do some good.

A Wretched and
Hopeless Existence.
health
fully restored.
The Case of
Mrs. Elizabeth Mitchell.
(From the Windsor, N.S. W., «« Australian”),
(by our special reporter.)
News w c as brought to the office that Mrs
Elizabeth Mitchell, of March-street, Rich"
mond, N.S.W., had been afflicted with
dropsy and accessory symptoms, and had
recovered in a manner that was
As Remarkable as Complete.
In consequence a reporter was instructed to
make the closest enquiries and elicit the
full details of the case.
Mrs. Mitchell kindly volunteered to
furnish the fullest details.
“I found myself gradually losing mv
health,” said that lady. “I felt heavy
and dispirited, and
My Head always Ached.
Pains between the shoulders made them-
selves felt, and a sharp, alarming pain in
the side racked me suddenly now and then.”
“ From what did this pain in the side
arise ? ”
“ The doctor said it was from my heart,
and indeed my heart often used to flutter
strangely, and sometimes I should go quite
faint, and would nearly
Lose Consciousness Altogether
The rest I obtained at night was so dis-
turbed and deficient, and I was so weak and
fagged in the day time that I couldn’t get
through my work. Indeed, sometimes I
should be so sick and faint that I was forced
to go to bed in the middle of the day.”
“ What else, Mrs. Mitchell ? ”
“My legs began to swell. Sometimes the
swelling would extend to my foot, and I
was not able to get on my shoes. Some-
times my legs would get gradually larger
for days, and then they would decrease in
size for a time, leading me to believe that 1
was getting better; but the hope was
always disappointed, for, after a short
respite, they would commence to swell
again.”
“ What was the swelling like ?”
“ The skin was of a whitish colour, and
when a finger was pressed on the place it
would leave the mark for a long while.
When my legs were very bad it was
Very Painful to Walk,

COUNTRY TRIAL ORD€R RESPECTFULLY SOLICITED.
The Curing of Little Jan Tromp.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Thb teak-lined banks were flush with the schooner’s
upper deck. In the shadow of the grinning stone
god lay the 200 rifles, paid for by the Burmese
Revolution Committee, and delivered by Horace
and me. The Dacoits wanted guns, and we were
dead sick of trepang and Polynesia. Pearl had
“an immense future behind it ” once ; but since
they’d started a gaol down the Straits way you
couldn’t get shellers to look at chicken-oyster.
So Horace and I helped the revolution. Horace
is part owner of the Bully Hayes.
Queer place we were in—endless flats, grid-
ironed with rotting creeks, and mud that stank
till it colored the air. When the Deluge drained
the sewers of Asia, it evidently set up shop here-
abouts.
All the customs officials were absent, but the
thirteen pairs of ears, nailed to a near tamarind,
gave us a hint that the Government had been
smitten on the shoe. That afternoon an excited
patriot bore off the guns in a bullock-dray. He
farewelled us, from the mud bank, with snatches
of Burmese poetry, good in its way, no doubt,
but Horace hinted that his bullockese was sheer
drivel —a mere clustering of anaemic words.
Horace is a Bogan man.
The pagoda under the blue-jungled spur showed
a glimmer of light. It was getting dark, and
Tommy the cook was singing below to Typhoo
Shang; Typhoo responded with 36 middling
verses about a girl named Soo Lee and a number
of tame peacocks. The cook gathered himself
from the bunk and said that he objected to the
introduction of poultry into songs, and wound up
by calling the Emperor of China a “cow.”
I peeped below and saw Shang feeling for his
knife sorrowfully, and pondering the six feet of
cabin that separated him from the cook. Shang
was keener than a Malay to smell an insult. His
arm went out lazily, but there was murder dozing
in his little black eyes.
“ There’ll be no scrapping here," I said,
sharply.
“ And no bloomin’ mess to clean up,” said
Horace, flipping Shang in the eye with a banana.
Shang smiled sweetly ; the knife vanished up his
sleeve like a trick. You see, Horace had an un-
common way with him ; when he kicked you, you
understood him perfectly. The cook crawled
back to his bunk.
At this point there was an interruption a
strange voice hailed us from the shore. The three
of us leaped to the companion, like men bailed-up.
I was first on deck, and peered through the dark-
ness to eateh a glimpse of the queer marauder. A
light flickered on the pagoda-roof, and went out.
The aching silence filled us with a listening fear.
“Who called?” said I, and stepped back
sharply.
A pair of hands gripped the rail; something
dropped to the deck, salaaming in the dark.
“ Well,” I said, not taken aback. “ What’s
your particular trouble, Mr. Quick ? ”
A match fizzed above the pistol in Horace’s
hand. I looked under it at the ghostly shape, the
jewelled hands spread on the deck.
“ Salaam, sailor! Do I speak with the
master ? ”
“ That’s me,” said I. “ Rather kind of you to
look in.”
The shape sighed, and unloosened a silk slander-
dang, that trailed from neck to heel,
“ An English ship, sailor ? ”
“An Australian larrikin,” I replied. “Tea
and patent medicines up to the forehold.”
The cook staggered up with a lantern, holding
it before the Jew-faced, ear-ringed stranger.
He fingered his beard reflectively. “ Austra-
lia?”
“ Small island in the Pacific,” said I. “ Popu-
lation 150, three squatters, and the balance
mostly Chinkie and Kanaka. Lovely country ! ”
He bent forward musingly. “ I heard that it
belonged to Japan ; Yoshi Hannaken told me so,
last year, in Mandalay. Is it so ? ”
“ Bed-rock,” said I. “ That Yoshi Hannaken
is a man of parts.”
The stranger turned swiftly to the flare that
streaked the darkness above' the pagoda roof.
“ Would you run from here to Jandyong ? ” says
he.
“ Find me a cargo,” I said.
He looked at me cunningly. “ You are not a
particular man ? ” he whispered.
“ I’d run to Gehenna for lost souls and ashes if
it paid.” I’d trade that stone - god yonder
for a package of Bibles, or a hogshead of beer.
Better look in with your cargo now; there’ll be a
heathen uproar to-morrow.”
“Beer,” said Horace, dreamily.
I looked at him sternly, but he was wrapped in
thought.
The stranger turned to me with his hand on the
rail. “ Wait,” he said, and before you could
wink he had vanished in the darkness.
“He’s a mahout,” said Horace, “a king’s
mahout. He’s pinched a couple of elephants, and
you’re going to hog ’em down the settlement.
Fine pilot you’d have made —aboard the Ark.”
I said nothing, but guessed there was some
trickery afoot in the pagoda—the planting of loot,
maybe—if the king had been deposed. The light
flared again and went streaming through the
jungle. A moment later my strange visitor was
tripping amongst our shore-lines; beside him
crouched a tawny-limbed youngster, naked but
for a panther’s hide laced about his shoulders.
“ He has been to Calcutta,” said the stranger
hurriedly, “ to receive his education. There was
cholera, and we burnt his clothes. His name is
Jan Tromp ; observe how small he is, physically ;
but his mind is perfect; he is a Bachelor of Arts.”
The stranger indicated him proudly. But the
boy turned impatiently, and said: “Enough of
talk, Velarney. Ernest Mueller, who is only 13,
has done better.”
I liked him for that, and patted his panther’s
hide. He was one of those pleasant-faced little
Dutchies that you will meet anywhere between
Rangoon and Batavia.
“ Touching the passage-money ? ”
“The price of a new schooner, paid in notes on
the Bank of Bombay.”
That was good enough for me.
* * * *
By noon the following day we had left Cape
Negrais on our quarter. The night came up
clear, with a sou’-easterly driving a few ragged
clouds across the four points of mid-heaven.
Horace was in the trade - room yarning with a
beachcomber we’d picked up in Honolulu.
Typhoo Shang squatted under the bridge, and
every time I peeped over he would shake himself,
look up at the moon and spit over the clean deck.
“I’ll knock peacocks out of you,” I thought,
“ when my trick at the wheel’s over.”
Presently he said, “You eally passenger,
clap’n ? ”
“ I’ve carried pigs before to-day, you dirty son
of a comprador,” I answered.
Typhoo lit a cigarette and dozed. The wind
fell a space, and the “ Bully ” hogged in the gut of
two seas.
“Lillie passenger, him plully leper,” said
Typhoo.
“What’s that?” said I, leaning over, “say it
again.”
“Lillie Jan Tlomp, him leper. Me gotteo
bluller in Lillie Bluck-slee, him leper too. Hide
him under clabbege. Jan Tlomp leper.”
I called to a Dago hand. “ Send the passenger
to me.”
Three minutes later Velarney was standing on
the bridge. He offered me a cigar; I put it
aside.
“You’ve played it pretty low on me,” I observed.
“But I’m damned if I’ll jig lepers up and down
the ’Peligo ; it’s an outrage on my clean schooner,
sir ! ”
He stepped back, holding the rail, and the
jewels lit up his fingers as if he’d just taken them
from a kettle of phosphorus.
“ You speak of little Jan Tromp.”
“Of the leper,” said I, “ that you covered with
a panther’s skin. But ashore he goes, first rock in
the Malacca. I’d put him over now only I fear to
pollute the Almighty’s sea. I’m sorry, sir, but
I’m not running a lazaretto for the benefit of my
health.”
“Mere Caucasian prejudice,” he remarked, fir-
ing up.
“ A rose, by any other name, would smell as
sweet,” I rapped out, remembering my schooling.
“You area painfully ignorant man,” said he.
“ You come of a breed that encouraged vaccinator
Jenner —the charlatan, the pedlar, the cattle-
doctor.”
He stopped, and reached for my hand apologeti-
cally.
“ You will think me a jealons fakir ; indeed, I
am nothing more than a scientist, and within a
week the skin of Jan Tromp will be as clean as
your own.”
“I’m not acquainted with physics,” I said,
“ but I guess it takes more than a green pill to
clear a hide-bound leper. I’d like to see ye do the
trick.”
“ Trick,” says he. “By God! It is an art that
was lost in Egypt nearly 4000 years ago.”
* * * *
I landed them at the mouth of a boot-shaped
peninsula. A big surf thumped the dingey across
the sand-spit to where the reef-points threatened
us like swords. Jan Tromp leaped ashore.
Velarney beckoned us to follow. So I tramped
in their wake until we topped the rocky altar,
humped above the jungle-line. Jan Tromp ran
ahead, dodging amongst the ghostly banks-palnis,
and flapping the tail of the panther’s hide. A
solitary gull flew seaward. In the west, the
dropping sun streamed like yellow rain through
the half-lit jungle. Peering across the banded
foliage, I saw a low-roofed house cross-shaped
and pillared from wing to wing, like those queer
monkey-temples at Benares. The din of a
Burmese gong shook the painful stillness, and
suddenly, from every nook and arch, came a score
of silk-trousered, salaaming Javanese. A fat
fellow with betel-flecked lips and long hair picked
himself out of the dirt and said :
“ Welcome, Highness, welcome. Thy house
mourned for thee as dead. Every day we have
done thy bidding, keeping oil and fire in readi-
ness. Listen ! ”
The gong-clamor ceased, and from within came
a sound like the droning of a million bees.
“ Jikur,” said Velarney, “thou art indeed my
brother.”
He took the fat fellow’s hand, and pressed it.
Suddenly little Jan Tromp caught Velarney’s
sleeve, and the pair stood with bent heads in the
portico. Across the terrace came a woman, hold-
ing a lamp. I saw her naked arms, the lines of
bust and throat, the full crushed lips. I heard
the rush of silk as she mounted the steps, like
Sheba in the picture. I saw Velarney lean across
the panther’s liide, and ward her off like a blow.
“At last! my God ! at last! ”
The wind blew out her lamp ; she was on her
knees.
“ Not yet, mother—not yet,” said Velarney.
“Thy son is unclean.”
Some invisible hand flung open a brass-lined
door ; I was thrust inside with Velarney and little
Jan Tromp. The room was octagon-shaped, and
smelt like a bit of excavated Pompeii. An oblong
bath filled the centre ; three brass poles spanned
it from end to end. A Siemens dynamo
hummed in the far corner ; there were switch-
boards and a patent galvanometer attached.
Thought I, if this be the great Egyptian cure
revived, it proves that the excellent Pharaoh ha'd
more than a Cl)inky notion of science.
I heard a spl .sliing behind me, and saw Jan
Tromp emerging from a smaller bath; his
drenched body glistened with white crystals.
Velarney took a copper belt and bound him by
the waist; then he beckoned to me.
“ The boy is heavy—help me, sailor ! ”
“ I will,” said I. “ in the interests of humanity.”
I took him, head and heels, and swung him into
the big bath. Velarney fastened the belt with
wires to the centre pole; the neck and ankles
rested in loops of copper; and Jan Tromp lay
submerged in the tea-colored solution to the tips
of his nose. Velarney drew away swiftly and
touched a belt; the hand of the galvanometer
trembled across the dial ; Jan Tromp made no
sign ; his body swayed under the poles that were
licked from end to end by electric sparks. Velar-
ney’s straining eyes were fixed on the moving
finger of the dial. I looked through the lozenge-
shaped window that faced the sea ; the smoke of a
tramp blew inshore; it was almost dark, and
strange sepias were roofing all over the wide hol-
low in the east.
Velarney approached the bath like some strange
enchanter : his hands were across his breast, and I
thought, as the faint light shadowed his face,
what a model he would have made for Dore’s
Christ—the lissom body, the nervous lips that
prayed in the rift of magnificent beard, the roof of
forehead that seemed indeed a fit repository for all
the garnered wisdom of Asia.
I stepped to the bath and took a peep at little
Jan Tromp. He was motionless and rigid, and
his skin had changed to the color of orange-peel.
“I guess your leper’s gold-plated enough,” I
said to Verlaney.
A change had come over the man ; his fingers
gripped the enamelled edge of the bath, his eye 3
glowered with animal ferocity, the smooth flesh
reeved over his forehead into a thousand wrinkles.
“ God of Islam ! God of Judah !” he began.
“That’s a weak spot in the entertainment,”
said I. “ Science needs no prayers.”
He rocked forward with his back humped, until
the cone of his beard skimmed the solution.
“Silence ! thou—thou Christ-man ! ”
“ Pardon my Caucasian impulse,” I said; “ But
I have a mind to take up this leper-curing racket ;
there’s a good opening for a reliable man in Aus-
tralia. Let’s look at Jan Tromp.”
With that I broke the lashings that oound nirn
eo the pole and lifted him out. The body rustled
in my arms like tinsel; I stretched it on the tiled
floor and shook it; the golden skin cracked and

the flesh was hidden as completely as if he had

“ Free from infection at least, and good to
handle. It is curious,” I said, passing my hand
over the heart region, “how the soul could have
escaped through so fine a casement.”
Velarney reached upwards, his fingers dragged
the air as if he were playing an invisible harp.
Then he fell, face-down, on the body of little Jan
Tromp. I guessed the harp-strings had snapped
somewhere in his brain.
I felt for the door and groped across the ter-
race. A dusky shape slid past; the brass-lined
door clashed. That was all.
Scrambling down the rocky altar I stopped
awhile to listen ; cry after cry pierced the jungle
overhead until every leaf seemed stirred by the
note of human misery.
“Sheba with the lamp. Sheba looking at her
son.”
An opening in the palm-lined shelves showed
me a league of surf, and the schooner’s ligh*
beyond it.
ALBA DORION.
The Jones’s Honeymoon.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was in the winter-time,
Yet the year seemed in its prime;
Soft and balmy was the air,
Full of rich delight and ease;
And the world was very fair;
And the branches of the trees
Swayed to a melodious tune:
’Twas the Jones’s Honeymoon.
All about the place she went,
Singing songs of sweet content;
With his eyes he followed her,
With his fond adoring eyes;
Ev’ry gesture, ev’ry stir
Was to him a new surprise—
New surprise and blessed boon :
’Twas the Jones’s Honeymoon.
Sometimes down the path they paced,
He with arm around her waist,
Calling her his Dearest One,
Rose of Joy and Heart of Gold,
While the great free-handed sun—
Lord of lovers from of old—
Framed them in a splendid noon :
’Twas the Jones’s Honeymoon.
Through that rosy haze of sense
Lovely loomed the dog-leg fence—
Looking pretty for her sake;
So she thought, sweet simple soul—
And a lone romantic lake
Seemed the muddy water hole.
She re-named it Love Lagoon,
In the Jones’s Honeymoon.
Built of slabs and roofed with tin,
Jones’s house would make you grin.
Jones’s Humpy was the name
It went by, the district through.
This the sentimental damo
Straightway changed to'Gastle Coo,
Castle. Coo in Cumbalune —
In the Jones’s Hon ymoon.
Through her small new world she Went,
With a fancy opulent,
And vocabulary title,
(Jones looked on, a blissful man)
Called the bay cow Clementine,
Called the white cow Lilian,
All the place with names was strevvn,
In the Jones’s Honeymoon.
Thus through Eden, side by side,
Went these mortals glorified.
She was scarce the prize he deemed ;
He was yellow as a quince;
Yet to each the other seemed
Fairy Princess, Fairy Prince—
In that wonder-month of June,
In the Jones’s Honeymoon.
Does he still her waist enfold ?
Call her Deafest ? Heart of gold ?
Does she yet, with fancy fine—
Making him a blissful man—
Call the bay cow Clementine?
Call the white cow Lilian ?
Call the mud-hole Love Lagoon,
As in that glad Honeymoon ?
Ask me not ! Ido not know:
But I dream that vision rare,
And, to keep that vision rare
Ever lovely, ever true,
By their house I never fare—'
Never pass by Castle Coo ;
Castle Coo in Cumbalune,
Where they kept their Hon ymoon.

British nationality and military position, and
take up his residence in Germany—all for th
dubious honor of figuring as a guasf-kinglet under
William the Bounder.

The Tree.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
The woman lolled gracefully in a quaint low ohaiv
on the verandah, and the-man,' leaning again-t a
post at a little distance, gazed out upon the garden
in moody silence. The night was bright and
clear, and the lilies—true flowers of the moon—
moved like a troop of stately and beautiful spec-
tres in the passing breeze.
She was a fair, fleecy woman of about thirty,
with an even temperature and a passion for feathers.
She admired ■ Schopenhauer and Frederick
Nietzsche immensely, and thought “-The Picture
of Dorian Gray ” the best novel ever written. She
had a certain intellectual flippancy and a clever
tongue, and had veneered her nature with a thin
coating of philosophy, and now mistook tin- veneer
for the substance. She would not under any. cir-
cumstances have been nearly as bad as she im-
agined, but most people over-estimate their capa-
city for evil, and have a fine contempt for those
sinners who fail.
He was a big, slow man with a fine head, and
would have been considered an ass had he kicked
under provocation. But he did not kick, and she
liked to have him amongst her guests because he
was so large a butt that no shaft missed him.
Hitherto she had discovered no trace of irritation
in the man, and his want of sensibility piqued
her and excited her anger. Where is the use of
wielding the lash if no one feels the sting?
“You enjoyed yourself immensely at dinner.
My God ! woman, you are the most vicious devil I
have ever met! ”
The voice was harsh, sudden, and full of male-
volence—not loud, but it struck upon the ivomah’s
ears like a thunder-clap in the still night. Mrs.
Warner, who prided herself on her delightful
repose, sat up suddenly, and peered at him with
startled eyes.
“Was that you who spoke, George ? ” she asked,
amazed ly.
“You take a fiendish delight in torture,” the
man continued in the same tone of suppressed
rage. “ I can imagine you clapping your thin
white hands at an auto da f 6. What sensations
Torquemada could have given you, you beast! ”
He approached her, and, resting against the
window-sill, leaned over her with an expression
that was almost murderous. Mrs. Warner
cowered under his eyes. She had longed for
some signs of emotion in the big animal, but this
was so disproportionate, so utterly out of reason,
it terrified her, and she was silent under the
insult.
“ You touch me over with your pale, soft
fingers, and where there is a tenderness you stab,
and stab, and burn and bite. The others—damn
them ! It is you who start the fire and heat the
irons, and show the idiots how to nip, and probe,
and singe.”
“ How dare you ! How dare you ! ” she mur-
mured in feeble protestation.
“You make me the victim of your damnable
craving for ‘sensations.’ Listen to me. You
have not enjoyed them as you might have done
had you known the truth; I will give them a
keener zest, a sweeter flavor. I love you ! Do
you hear? I love you like hell and heaven. I
long for you with every breath of me. I adore
you, and you can sting me as neither God nor
devil could sting in a whole eternity of torment.
Remember this for the future when you are work-
ing the rack and the thumbscrew. I would not
rob you of one thrill that the dear torture might
give you, you beautiful fiend ! ”
Sho arose and confronted him, white as a corpse,
and trembling in every limb. Was he mad ’ It
was the language of madness. After all, she had
only joked and jibed. His attitude was prepos-
terously melodramatic. He was certainly mad.
She ; attempted to escape, but he caught' her wrist
and held her fast.
“ You vv ant sensations—sensations—it is one of
your cant-phrases. I have given you a sensation
to night. 1 will give you another, a grander one
before many days have fled. Your bedroom
fronts on to that old gum tree; look out one of
these fine mornings, and you may see my corpse
hanging from a limb, mouthing hideously in at
your window.”
She repressed a cry with a great effort, and
other guests thronged on to the verandah lau°-h-
-mg and chattering, and under her breath she
thanked the Hod in whom she had implicitly dis-
believed for years.
Shortly after/daylight next morning Mrs. War-
ner crept from her bed, looking pale and haggard
and stole to the window. She lifted a quaking
hand to open the blind, and recoiled from the act
in terror. She was suffering from abject fear a
horror had haunted her pillow all night. Again
and again she.had heard awful movements in the
old tree, the thud of a falling body, the jerk of the
rope. The. woman approached the window again
with eyes tightly shut; she opened the blind, and
then slowly opened her eyes. A sob of relief
burst from her laboring breast, and she vve.pt in a
huddled heap on the floor. No ghastly fruit luing
from the limbs of the great gum.
During the following day Mrs. Warner laughed
at her terrors, and knew them to be absurd, but at
night, lying in the darkness, hearing the familiar
noises of the wind in the tree, the agony of dread
stole into her bones once more, and she suffered
horribly. Again and again she stole to the win-
dow to peep out. Once she w ; as deceived by a
heavy shadow amongst the leaves, and shrieked
aloud. Another night she sat for hours, cold as a
stone, watching the tree. At the end of a: week
her friends found her looking old and worn, and,
meanwhile, she had seen nothing of Oeorge Eg-
mont since the night of his strange declaration. ° .
When George called again, Mrs. Warner was in
the garden with two or three workmen ; stout
ropes were fastened to the big gum, and one man
stood by the butt, armed with a keen new axe.
George was surprised at the change in her.
“ It will ruin your beautiful garden,” he said.
She did not reply, but there was a pitiful ex*
pression in her face and an appealing light in her
eye. He approached her more closely, and
gripped the hand by his side.
“ There is another way ! ” he whispered.
They looked into each other’s faces, and Mrs.
Warner’s was that of a chained captive.
“There is another way !”
“ Tell them to stop. I—l will not destroy the
tree,” she faltered.
When Egmont was leaving the.house that night
he looked up at the tree for a moment, and.
laughed softly in his abundant beard.
EDWARD DYSON.
THE TRIALS OF A PRINCIPAL
BOY.
The Music Hall and Variety Artists’ Protection Associa-
tion is seriously considering the advisability of contesting
the right of an agent to draw commission upon an engage-
ment obtained by him when the artist is unable to fulfil it
by reason of ill-health. The case in point is not without
interest. A well-known serio-comic, whose salary ap-
proaches often between £BO and £lOO a week, recently be-
came so indisposed that she was unable to give her full
“ show,” and the management of the hall decided to deduct
a portion of her salary. Her agent, however, claimed
commission upon the full salary, and this is the point to
be fought.
That the health of the professional is a matter of the
utmost importance is beyond all question, seeing that the
public often pay lavishly in order to be present at some
MISS DAISY JAMES.
performance, and
they naturally feel
disappointed, to say
the least-, when they
read the announce-
ment that the par-
ticular artist is “in-
disposed and unable
to appear.” From
a professional point
of view the mainte-
nance of good health
is the keystone to
success, and, this
being so, the story
told by one of Lon-
don’s most promis-
ing lady artists,
Miss Daisy James,
cannot be without
interest to her ad-
mirers, who are le-
gion, and to the
public generally.
Miss James, who in
private life is Mrs.
Harry Villiers, was
formerly one of tile
sisters Fortescue,
but hard work under trying conditions told in the end, and
considerable suffering and pecuniary loss resulted. It was
not an easy matter to get this quiet, lady-like little artiste
to te>i the story of her suffering, because, she argued,’
apart from causing her friends alarm, it might militate
considerably in the matter of future engagements. How-
ever (says a Weekly Dispatch commissioner), she listened
to what I had to say, and, when I pointed out the good she’
might do from a public point of view, the objection was
withdrawn, and I was told the following story :
“ When one has been before the public professionally
for som* vonrs, t hey acre apt to be thought older than they
l eally are, but still I shall not be twenty until next May.
For some years I worked with my sister under the name of
the Sisters Fortescue. My sister is a fine, well-made girl,
but, although I have never admitted it, I have been told
that I was more developed than she. Whilst we were in
America we had to work very hard, not always under
healthy conditions, and, in spite of the
fact that I was stronger than my sister, I
soon fell ill ; in fact, I had an attack of
low fever. I managed to pull through
and get to England, but although I had
the best of advice I caught scarlet fever,
and was laid up for some time. I got
well, but I found I was thin and pale,
and sometimes felt as if I had not an
ounce of blood in the whole of my body.
I was told I was in a decline. I went to
doctor after doctor, but still to no pur-
pose ; the more medicine I had the more
ill I seemed to get.
“ About this time I began to perform
by myself, and, having some good songs
and business, I was able to find plenty
of engagements, and my agents booked
me right up. I was overjoyed at the
prospect of plenty of “turns” at good
salaries. In certain towns I had a
great reception, but I found my strength
was not equal to the task. Once, 1 re-
member, I was so weak that I had not
the strength to go on the stage and lake
an encore, and while the audience were
shouting and whistling for me, I was
lying in the arms of my dresser, begging
the manager to go on and say something
to the audience. Well, this went on for
some time, and gradually I got worse
and worse, until many friends began to
speculate as to the date of my funeral.
It’s about four months ago now that one
night my husband happened to overhear
a conversation in the dressing-room that
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills had
SAVED A WELL-KNOWN ACTOll’s LIFE,
and as a kind of grasp at the last straw I
determined, after what he told me, to
try.a box. I did so, and began to take
them. I assure you I felt better the fol-
lowing day. I went on with them, and
they have saved not only my professional
reputation, but almost my life. They
must really be a wonderful medicine for
all diseases of the blood and nerves,
rheumatism, bronchitis, consumption and
fevers. Since taking the pills I have
sung as many as eight or ten songs a
night. My health is so good that lam booked up till the
end of the year 1900 ; I haven’t a vacant week, but you
bet that when I go away the first thing that I pack in my
make-up case 'is a box of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. I
always see that I get the genuine, with ‘ Dr. Williams'
Pink Pills for Pale People ’ printed in red ink on a white
wrapper. Before going to a long and tedious rehearsal
I take one of the pills, and I feel as well as possible, and I
don’t know what nervous breakdown is. I feel it is
only right to Dr. Williams that I should do what I cam
to help him in his good work, because he cures where
hundreds of doctors fail.”
